Available online at\r    http://classics.mit.edu//Homer/iliad.html\r\rThe Iliad\rBy Homer\r\r\rTranslated by Samuel Butler\r\r----------------------------------------------------------------------\r\rBOOK I\r\rSing, O goddess, the anger of Achilles son of Peleus, that brought\rcountless ills upon the Achaeans. Many a brave soul did it send hurrying\rdown to Hades, and many a hero did it yield a prey to dogs and vultures,\rfor so were the counsels of Jove fulfilled from the day on which the\rson of Atreus, king of men, and great Achilles, first fell out with\rone another. \r\rAnd which of the gods was it that set them on to quarrel? It was the\rson of Jove and Leto; for he was angry with the king and sent a pestilence\rupon the host to plague the people, because the son of Atreus had\rdishonoured Chryses his priest. Now Chryses had come to the ships\rof the Achaeans to free his daughter, and had brought with him a great\rransom: moreover he bore in his hand the sceptre of Apollo wreathed\rwith a suppliant\'s wreath and he besought the Achaeans, but most of\rall the two sons of Atreus, who were their chiefs. \r\r"Sons of Atreus," he cried, "and all other Achaeans, may the gods\rwho dwell in Olympus grant you to sack the city of Priam, and to reach\ryour homes in safety; but free my daughter, and accept a ransom for\rher, in reverence to Apollo, son of Jove." \r\rOn this the rest of the Achaeans with one voice were for respecting\rthe priest and taking the ransom that he offered; but not so Agamemnon,\rwho spoke fiercely to him and sent him roughly away. "Old man," said\rhe, "let me not find you tarrying about our ships, nor yet coming\rhereafter. Your sceptre of the god and your wreath shall profit you\rnothing. I will not free her. She shall grow old in my house at Argos\rfar from her own home, busying herself with her loom and visiting\rmy couch; so go, and do not provoke me or it shall be the worse for\ryou." \r\rThe old man feared him and obeyed. Not a word he spoke, but went by\rthe shore of the sounding sea and prayed apart to King Apollo whom\rlovely Leto had borne. "Hear me," he cried, "O god of the silver bow,\rthat protectest Chryse and holy Cilla and rulest Tenedos with thy\rmight, hear me oh thou of Sminthe. If I have ever decked your temple\rwith garlands, or burned your thigh-bones in fat of bulls or goats,\rgrant my prayer, and let your arrows avenge these my tears upon the\rDanaans." \r\rThus did he pray, and Apollo heard his prayer. He came down furious\rfrom the summits of Olympus, with his bow and his quiver upon his\rshoulder, and the arrows rattled on his back with the rage that trembled\rwithin him. He sat himself down away from the ships with a face as\rdark as night, and his silver bow rang death as he shot his arrow\rin the midst of them. First he smote their mules and their hounds,\rbut presently he aimed his shafts at the people themselves, and all\rday long the pyres of the dead were burning. \r\rFor nine whole days he shot his arrows among the people, but upon\rthe tenth day Achilles called them in assembly- moved thereto by Juno,\rwho saw the Achaeans in their death-throes and had compassion upon\rthem. Then, when they were got together, he rose and spoke among them.\r\r"Son of Atreus," said he, "I deem that we should now turn roving home\rif we would escape destruction, for we are being cut down by war and\rpestilence at once. Let us ask some priest or prophet, or some reader\rof dreams (for dreams, too, are of Jove) who can tell us why Phoebus\rApollo is so angry, and say whether it is for some vow that we have\rbroken, or hecatomb that we have not offered, and whether he will\raccept the savour of lambs and goats without blemish, so as to take\raway the plague from us." \r\rWith these words he sat down, and Calchas son of Thestor, wisest of\raugurs, who knew things past present and to come, rose to speak. He\rit was who had guided the Achaeans with their fleet to Ilius, through\rthe prophesyings with which Phoebus Apollo had inspired him. With\rall sincerity and goodwill he addressed them thus:- \r\r"Achilles, loved of heaven, you bid me tell you about the anger of\rKing Apollo, I will therefore do so; but consider first and swear\rthat you will stand by me heartily in word and deed, for I know that\rI shall offend one who rules the Argives with might, to whom all the\rAchaeans are in subjection. A plain man cannot stand against the anger\rof a king, who if he swallow his displeasure now, will yet nurse revenge\rtill he has wreaked it. Consider, therefore, whether or no you will\rprotect me." \r\rAnd Achilles answered, "Fear not, but speak as it is borne in upon\ryou from heaven, for by Apollo, Calchas, to whom you pray, and whose\roracles you reveal to us, not a Danaan at our ships shall lay his\rhand upon you, while I yet live to look upon the face of the earth-\rno, not though you name Agamemnon himself, who is by far the foremost\rof the Achaeans." \r\rThereon the seer spoke boldly. "The god," he said, "is angry neither\rabout vow nor hecatomb, but for his priest\'s sake, whom Agamemnon\rhas dishonoured, in that he would not free his daughter nor take a\rransom for her; therefore has he sent these evils upon us, and will\ryet send others. He will not deliver the Danaans from this pestilence\rtill Agamemnon has restored the girl without fee or ransom to her\rfather, and has sent a holy hecatomb to Chryse. Thus we may perhaps\rappease him." \r\rWith these words he sat down, and Agamemnon rose in anger. His heart\rwas black with rage, and his eyes flashed fire as he scowled on Calchas\rand said, "Seer of evil, you never yet prophesied smooth things concerning\rme, but have ever loved to foretell that which was evil. You have\rbrought me neither comfort nor performance; and now you come seeing\ramong Danaans, and saying that Apollo has plagued us because I would\rnot take a ransom for this girl, the daughter of Chryses. I have set\rmy heart on keeping her in my own house, for I love her better even\rthan my own wife Clytemnestra, whose peer she is alike in form and\rfeature, in understanding and accomplishments. Still I will give her\rup if I must, for I would have the people live, not die; but you must\rfind me a prize instead, or I alone among the Argives shall be without\rone. This is not well; for you behold, all of you, that my prize is\rto go elsewhither." \r\rAnd Achilles answered, "Most noble son of Atreus, covetous beyond\rall mankind, how shall the Achaeans find you another prize? We have\rno common store from which to take one. Those we took from the cities\rhave been awarded; we cannot disallow the awards that have been made\ralready. Give this girl, therefore, to the god, and if ever Jove grants\rus to sack the city of Troy we will requite you three and fourfold."\r\rThen Agamemnon said, "Achilles, valiant though you be, you shall not\rthus outwit me. You shall not overreach and you shall not persuade\rme. Are you to keep your own prize, while I sit tamely under my loss\rand give up the girl at your bidding? Let the Achaeans find me a prize\rin fair exchange to my liking, or I will come and take your own, or\rthat of Ajax or of Ulysses; and he to whomsoever I may come shall\rrue my coming. But of this we will take thought hereafter; for the\rpresent, let us draw a ship into the sea, and find a crew for her\rexpressly; let us put a hecatomb on board, and let us send Chryseis\ralso; further, let some chief man among us be in command, either Ajax,\ror Idomeneus, or yourself, son of Peleus, mighty warrior that you\rare, that we may offer sacrifice and appease the the anger of the\rgod." \r\rAchilles scowled at him and answered, "You are steeped in insolence\rand lust of gain. With what heart can any of the Achaeans do your\rbidding, either on foray or in open fighting? I came not warring here\rfor any ill the Trojans had done me. I have no quarrel with them.\rThey have not raided my cattle nor my horses, nor cut down my harvests\ron the rich plains of Phthia; for between me and them there is a great\rspace, both mountain and sounding sea. We have followed you, Sir Insolence!\rfor your pleasure, not ours- to gain satisfaction from the Trojans\rfor your shameless self and for Menelaus. You forget this, and threaten\rto rob me of the prize for which I have toiled, and which the sons\rof the Achaeans have given me. Never when the Achaeans sack any rich\rcity of the Trojans do I receive so good a prize as you do, though\rit is my hands that do the better part of the fighting. When the sharing\rcomes, your share is far the largest, and I, forsooth, must go back\rto my ships, take what I can get and be thankful, when my labour of\rfighting is done. Now, therefore, I shall go back to Phthia; it will\rbe much better for me to return home with my ships, for I will not\rstay here dishonoured to gather gold and substance for you."\r\rAnd Agamemnon answered, "Fly if you will, I shall make you no prayers\rto stay you. I have others here who will do me honour, and above all\rJove, the lord of counsel. There is no king here so hateful to me\ras you are, for you are ever quarrelsome and ill affected. What though\ryou be brave? Was it not heaven that made you so? Go home, then, with\ryour ships and comrades to lord it over the Myrmidons. I care neither\rfor you nor for your anger; and thus will I do: since Phoebus Apollo\ris taking Chryseis from me, I shall send her with my ship and my followers,\rbut I shall come to your tent and take your own prize Briseis, that\ryou may learn how much stronger I am than you are, and that another\rmay fear to set himself up as equal or comparable with me."\r\rThe son of Peleus was furious, and his heart within his shaggy breast\rwas divided whether to draw his sword, push the others aside, and\rkill the son of Atreus, or to restrain himself and check his anger.\rWhile he was thus in two minds, and was drawing his mighty sword from\rits scabbard, Minerva came down from heaven (for Juno had sent her\rin the love she bore to them both), and seized the son of Peleus by\rhis yellow hair, visible to him alone, for of the others no man could\rsee her. Achilles turned in amaze, and by the fire that flashed from\rher eyes at once knew that she was Minerva. "Why are you here," said\rhe, "daughter of aegis-bearing Jove? To see the pride of Agamemnon,\rson of Atreus? Let me tell you- and it shall surely be- he shall pay\rfor this insolence with his life." \r\rAnd Minerva said, "I come from heaven, if you will hear me, to bid\ryou stay your anger. Juno has sent me, who cares for both of you alike.\rCease, then, this brawling, and do not draw your sword; rail at him\rif you will, and your railing will not be vain, for I tell you- and\rit shall surely be- that you shall hereafter receive gifts three times\ras splendid by reason of this present insult. Hold, therefore, and\robey." \r\r"Goddess," answered Achilles, "however angry a man may be, he must\rdo as you two command him. This will be best, for the gods ever hear\rthe prayers of him who has obeyed them." \r\rHe stayed his hand on the silver hilt of his sword, and thrust it\rback into the scabbard as Minerva bade him. Then she went back to\rOlympus among the other gods, and to the house of aegis-bearing Jove.\r\rBut the son of Peleus again began railing at the son of Atreus, for\rhe was still in a rage. "Wine-bibber," he cried, "with the face of\ra dog and the heart of a hind, you never dare to go out with the host\rin fight, nor yet with our chosen men in ambuscade. You shun this\ras you do death itself. You had rather go round and rob his prizes\rfrom any man who contradicts you. You devour your people, for you\rare king over a feeble folk; otherwise, son of Atreus, henceforward\ryou would insult no man. Therefore I say, and swear it with a great\roath- nay, by this my sceptre which shalt sprout neither leaf nor\rshoot, nor bud anew from the day on which it left its parent stem\rupon the mountains- for the axe stripped it of leaf and bark, and\rnow the sons of the Achaeans bear it as judges and guardians of the\rdecrees of heaven- so surely and solemnly do I swear that hereafter\rthey shall look fondly for Achilles and shall not find him. In the\rday of your distress, when your men fall dying by the murderous hand\rof Hector, you shall not know how to help them, and shall rend your\rheart with rage for the hour when you offered insult to the bravest\rof the Achaeans." \r\rWith this the son of Peleus dashed his gold-bestudded sceptre on the\rground and took his seat, while the son of Atreus was beginning fiercely\rfrom his place upon the other side. Then uprose smooth-tongued Nestor,\rthe facile speaker of the Pylians, and the words fell from his lips\rsweeter than honey. Two generations of men born and bred in Pylos\rhad passed away under his rule, and he was now reigning over the third.\rWith all sincerity and goodwill, therefore, he addressed them thus:-\r\r"Of a truth," he said, "a great sorrow has befallen the Achaean land.\rSurely Priam with his sons would rejoice, and the Trojans be glad\rat heart if they could hear this quarrel between you two, who are\rso excellent in fight and counsel. I am older than either of you;\rtherefore be guided by me. Moreover I have been the familiar friend\rof men even greater than you are, and they did not disregard my counsels.\rNever again can I behold such men as Pirithous and Dryas shepherd\rof his people, or as Caeneus, Exadius, godlike Polyphemus, and Theseus\rson of Aegeus, peer of the immortals. These were the mightiest men\rever born upon this earth: mightiest were they, and when they fought\rthe fiercest tribes of mountain savages they utterly overthrew them.\rI came from distant Pylos, and went about among them, for they would\rhave me come, and I fought as it was in me to do. Not a man now living\rcould withstand them, but they heard my words, and were persuaded\rby them. So be it also with yourselves, for this is the more excellent\rway. Therefore, Agamemnon, though you be strong, take not this girl\raway, for the sons of the Achaeans have already given her to Achilles;\rand you, Achilles, strive not further with the king, for no man who\rby the grace of Jove wields a sceptre has like honour with Agamemnon.\rYou are strong, and have a goddess for your mother; but Agamemnon\ris stronger than you, for he has more people under him. Son of Atreus,\rcheck your anger, I implore you; end this quarrel with Achilles, who\rin the day of battle is a tower of strength to the Achaeans."\r\rAnd Agamemnon answered, "Sir, all that you have said is true, but\rthis fellow must needs become our lord and master: he must be lord\rof all, king of all, and captain of all, and this shall hardly be.\rGranted that the gods have made him a great warrior, have they also\rgiven him the right to speak with railing?" \r\rAchilles interrupted him. "I should be a mean coward," he cried, "were\rI to give in to you in all things. Order other people about, not me,\rfor I shall obey no longer. Furthermore I say- and lay my saying to\ryour heart- I shall fight neither you nor any man about this girl,\rfor those that take were those also that gave. But of all else that\ris at my ship you shall carry away nothing by force. Try, that others\rmay see; if you do, my spear shall be reddened with your blood."\r\rWhen they had quarrelled thus angrily, they rose, and broke up the\rassembly at the ships of the Achaeans. The son of Peleus went back\rto his tents and ships with the son of Menoetius and his company,\rwhile Agamemnon drew a vessel into the water and chose a crew of twenty\roarsmen. He escorted Chryseis on board and sent moreover a hecatomb\rfor the god. And Ulysses went as captain. \r\rThese, then, went on board and sailed their ways over the sea. But\rthe son of Atreus bade the people purify themselves; so they purified\rthemselves and cast their filth into the sea. Then they offered hecatombs\rof bulls and goats without blemish on the sea-shore, and the smoke\rwith the savour of their sacrifice rose curling up towards heaven.\r\rThus did they busy themselves throughout the host. But Agamemnon did\rnot forget the threat that he had made Achilles, and called his trusty\rmessengers and squires Talthybius and Eurybates. "Go," said he, "to\rthe tent of Achilles, son of Peleus; take Briseis by the hand and\rbring her hither; if he will not give her I shall come with others\rand take her- which will press him harder." \r\rHe charged them straightly further and dismissed them, whereon they\rwent their way sorrowfully by the seaside, till they came to the tents\rand ships of the Myrmidons. They found Achilles sitting by his tent\rand his ships, and ill-pleased he was when he beheld them. They stood\rfearfully and reverently before him, and never a word did they speak,\rbut he knew them and said, "Welcome, heralds, messengers of gods and\rmen; draw near; my quarrel is not with you but with Agamemnon who\rhas sent you for the girl Briseis. Therefore, Patroclus, bring her\rand give her to them, but let them be witnesses by the blessed gods,\rby mortal men, and by the fierceness of Agamemnon\'s anger, that if\rever again there be need of me to save the people from ruin, they\rshall seek and they shall not find. Agamemnon is mad with rage and\rknows not how to look before and after that the Achaeans may fight\rby their ships in safety." \r\rPatroclus did as his dear comrade had bidden him. He brought Briseis\rfrom the tent and gave her over to the heralds, who took her with\rthem to the ships of the Achaeans- and the woman was loth to go. Then\rAchilles went all alone by the side of the hoar sea, weeping and looking\rout upon the boundless waste of waters. He raised his hands in prayer\rto his immortal mother, "Mother," he cried, "you bore me doomed to\rlive but for a little season; surely Jove, who thunders from Olympus,\rmight have made that little glorious. It is not so. Agamemnon, son\rof Atreus, has done me dishonour, and has robbed me of my prize by\rforce." \r\rAs he spoke he wept aloud, and his mother heard him where she was\rsitting in the depths of the sea hard by the old man her father. Forthwith\rshe rose as it were a grey mist out of the waves, sat down before\rhim as he stood weeping, caressed him with her hand, and said, "My\rson, why are you weeping? What is it that grieves you? Keep it not\rfrom me, but tell me, that we may know it together." \r\rAchilles drew a deep sigh and said, "You know it; why tell you what\ryou know well already? We went to Thebe the strong city of Eetion,\rsacked it, and brought hither the spoil. The sons of the Achaeans\rshared it duly among themselves, and chose lovely Chryseis as the\rmeed of Agamemnon; but Chryses, priest of Apollo, came to the ships\rof the Achaeans to free his daughter, and brought with him a great\rransom: moreover he bore in his hand the sceptre of Apollo, wreathed\rwith a suppliant\'s wreath, and he besought the Achaeans, but most\rof all the two sons of Atreus who were their chiefs. \r\r"On this the rest of the Achaeans with one voice were for respecting\rthe priest and taking the ransom that he offered; but not so Agamemnon,\rwho spoke fiercely to him and sent him roughly away. So he went back\rin anger, and Apollo, who loved him dearly, heard his prayer. Then\rthe god sent a deadly dart upon the Argives, and the people died thick\ron one another, for the arrows went everywhither among the wide host\rof the Achaeans. At last a seer in the fulness of his knowledge declared\rto us the oracles of Apollo, and I was myself first to say that we\rshould appease him. Whereon the son of Atreus rose in anger, and threatened\rthat which he has since done. The Achaeans are now taking the girl\rin a ship to Chryse, and sending gifts of sacrifice to the god; but\rthe heralds have just taken from my tent the daughter of Briseus,\rwhom the Achaeans had awarded to myself. \r\r"Help your brave son, therefore, if you are able. Go to Olympus, and\rif you have ever done him service in word or deed, implore the aid\rof Jove. Ofttimes in my father\'s house have I heard you glory in that\ryou alone of the immortals saved the son of Saturn from ruin, when\rthe others, with Juno, Neptune, and Pallas Minerva would have put\rhim in bonds. It was you, goddess, who delivered him by calling to\rOlympus the hundred-handed monster whom gods call Briareus, but men\rAegaeon, for he is stronger even than his father; when therefore he\rtook his seat all-glorious beside the son of Saturn, the other gods\rwere afraid, and did not bind him. Go, then, to him, remind him of\rall this, clasp his knees, and bid him give succour to the Trojans.\rLet the Achaeans be hemmed in at the sterns of their ships, and perish\ron the sea-shore, that they may reap what joy they may of their king,\rand that Agamemnon may rue his blindness in offering insult to the\rforemost of the Achaeans." \r\rThetis wept and answered, "My son, woe is me that I should have borne\ror suckled you. Would indeed that you had lived your span free from\rall sorrow at your ships, for it is all too brief; alas, that you\rshould be at once short of life and long of sorrow above your peers:\rwoe, therefore, was the hour in which I bore you; nevertheless I will\rgo to the snowy heights of Olympus, and tell this tale to Jove, if\rhe will hear our prayer: meanwhile stay where you are with your ships,\rnurse your anger against the Achaeans, and hold aloof from fight.\rFor Jove went yesterday to Oceanus, to a feast among the Ethiopians,\rand the other gods went with him. He will return to Olympus twelve\rdays hence; I will then go to his mansion paved with bronze and will\rbeseech him; nor do I doubt that I shall be able to persuade him."\r\rOn this she left him, still furious at the loss of her that had been\rtaken from him. Meanwhile Ulysses reached Chryse with the hecatomb.\rWhen they had come inside the harbour they furled the sails and laid\rthem in the ship\'s hold; they slackened the forestays, lowered the\rmast into its place, and rowed the ship to the place where they would\rhave her lie; there they cast out their mooring-stones and made fast\rthe hawsers. They then got out upon the sea-shore and landed the hecatomb\rfor Apollo; Chryseis also left the ship, and Ulysses led her to the\raltar to deliver her into the hands of her father. "Chryses," said\rhe, "King Agamemnon has sent me to bring you back your child, and\rto offer sacrifice to Apollo on behalf of the Danaans, that we may\rpropitiate the god, who has now brought sorrow upon the Argives."\r\rSo saying he gave the girl over to her father, who received her gladly,\rand they ranged the holy hecatomb all orderly round the altar of the\rgod. They washed their hands and took up the barley-meal to sprinkle\rover the victims, while Chryses lifted up his hands and prayed aloud\ron their behalf. "Hear me," he cried, "O god of the silver bow, that\rprotectest Chryse and holy Cilla, and rulest Tenedos with thy might.\rEven as thou didst hear me aforetime when I prayed, and didst press\rhardly upon the Achaeans, so hear me yet again, and stay this fearful\rpestilence from the Danaans." \r\rThus did he pray, and Apollo heard his prayer. When they had done\rpraying and sprinkling the barley-meal, they drew back the heads of\rthe victims and killed and flayed them. They cut out the thigh-bones,\rwrapped them round in two layers of fat, set some pieces of raw meat\ron the top of them, and then Chryses laid them on the wood fire and\rpoured wine over them, while the young men stood near him with five-pronged\rspits in their hands. When the thigh-bones were burned and they had\rtasted the inward meats, they cut the rest up small, put the pieces\rupon the spits, roasted them till they were done, and drew them off:\rthen, when they had finished their work and the feast was ready, they\rate it, and every man had his full share, so that all were satisfied.\rAs soon as they had had enough to eat and drink, pages filled the\rmixing-bowl with wine and water and handed it round, after giving\revery man his drink-offering. \r\rThus all day long the young men worshipped the god with song, hymning\rhim and chaunting the joyous paean, and the god took pleasure in their\rvoices; but when the sun went down, and it came on dark, they laid\rthemselves down to sleep by the stern cables of the ship, and when\rthe child of morning, rosy-fingered Dawn, appeared they again set\rsail for the host of the Achaeans. Apollo sent them a fair wind, so\rthey raised their mast and hoisted their white sails aloft. As the\rsail bellied with the wind the ship flew through the deep blue water,\rand the foam hissed against her bows as she sped onward. When they\rreached the wide-stretching host of the Achaeans, they drew the vessel\rashore, high and dry upon the sands, set her strong props beneath\rher, and went their ways to their own tents and ships. \r\rBut Achilles abode at his ships and nursed his anger. He went not\rto the honourable assembly, and sallied not forth to fight, but gnawed\rat his own heart, pining for battle and the war-cry. \r\rNow after twelve days the immortal gods came back in a body to Olympus,\rand Jove led the way. Thetis was not unmindful of the charge her son\rhad laid upon her, so she rose from under the sea and went through\rgreat heaven with early morning to Olympus, where she found the mighty\rson of Saturn sitting all alone upon its topmost ridges. She sat herself\rdown before him, and with her left hand seized his knees, while with\rher right she caught him under the chin, and besought him, saying-\r\r"Father Jove, if I ever did you service in word or deed among the\rimmortals, hear my prayer, and do honour to my son, whose life is\rto be cut short so early. King Agamemnon has dishonoured him by taking\rhis prize and keeping her. Honour him then yourself, Olympian lord\rof counsel, and grant victory to the Trojans, till the Achaeans give\rmy son his due and load him with riches in requital." \r\rJove sat for a while silent, and without a word, but Thetis still\rkept firm hold of his knees, and besought him a second time. "Incline\ryour head," said she, "and promise me surely, or else deny me- for\ryou have nothing to fear- that I may learn how greatly you disdain\rme." \r\rAt this Jove was much troubled and answered, "I shall have trouble\rif you set me quarrelling with Juno, for she will provoke me with\rher taunting speeches; even now she is always railing at me before\rthe other gods and accusing me of giving aid to the Trojans. Go back\rnow, lest she should find out. I will consider the matter, and will\rbring it about as wish. See, I incline my head that you believe me.\rThis is the most solemn that I can give to any god. I never recall\rmy word, or deceive, or fail to do what I say, when I have nodded\rmy head." \r\rAs he spoke the son of Saturn bowed his dark brows, and the ambrosial\rlocks swayed on his immortal head, till vast Olympus reeled.\r\rWhen the pair had thus laid their plans, they parted- Jove to his\rhouse, while the goddess quitted the splendour of Olympus, and plunged\rinto the depths of the sea. The gods rose from their seats, before\rthe coming of their sire. Not one of them dared to remain sitting,\rbut all stood up as he came among them. There, then, he took his seat.\rBut Juno, when she saw him, knew that he and the old merman\'s daughter,\rsilver-footed Thetis, had been hatching mischief, so she at once began\rto upbraid him. "Trickster," she cried, "which of the gods have you\rbeen taking into your counsels now? You are always settling matters\rin secret behind my back, and have never yet told me, if you could\rhelp it, one word of your intentions." \r\r"Juno," replied the sire of gods and men, "you must not expect to\rbe informed of all my counsels. You are my wife, but you would find\rit hard to understand them. When it is proper for you to hear, there\ris no one, god or man, who will be told sooner, but when I mean to\rkeep a matter to myself, you must not pry nor ask questions."\r\r"Dread son of Saturn," answered Juno, "what are you talking about?\rI? Pry and ask questions? Never. I let you have your own way in everything.\rStill, I have a strong misgiving that the old merman\'s daughter Thetis\rhas been talking you over, for she was with you and had hold of your\rknees this self-same morning. If the\rOlympian Thunderer wants to hurl us all from our seats, he can do\rso, for he is far the strongest, so give him fair words, and he will\rthen soon be in a good humour with us." \r\rAs he spoke, he took a double cup of nectar, and placed it in his\rmother\'s hand. Once before when I was trying to\rhelp you, he caught me by the foot and flung me from the heavenly\rthreshold. So Jove, the Olympian\rLord of Thunder, hied him to the bed in which he always slept; and\rwhen he had got on to it he went to sleep, with Juno of the golden\rthrone by his side. \r\r----------------------------------------------------------------------\r\rBOOK II\r\rNow the other gods and the armed warriors on the plain slept soundly,\rbut Jove was wakeful, for he was thinking how to do honour to Achilles,\rand destroyed much people at the ships of the Achaeans. In the end\rhe deemed it would be best to send a lying dream to King Agamemnon;\rso he called one to him and said to it, "Lying Dream, go to the ships\rof the Achaeans, into the tent of Agamemnon, and say to him word to\rword as I now bid you. This was the work of\rVulcan, who gave it to Jove the son of Saturn. Jove gave it to Mercury,\rslayer of Argus, guide and guardian. As when the waves\rrun high before the blast of the south wind and break on some lofty\rheadland, dashing against it and buffeting it without ceasing, as\rthe storms from every quarter drive them, even so did the Achaeans\rrise and hurry in all directions to their ships. As some great bull that lords it\rover the herds upon the plain, even so did Jove make the son of Atreus\rstand peerless among the multitude of heroes. \r\rAnd now, O Muses, dwellers in the mansions of Olympus, tell me- for\ryou are goddesses and are in all places so that you see all things,\rwhile we know nothing but by report- who were the chiefs and princes\rof the Danaans? As for the common soldiers, they were so that I could\rnot name every single one of them though I had ten tongues, and though\rmy voice failed not and my heart were of bronze within me, unless\ryou, O Olympian Muses, daughters of aegis-bearing Jove, were to recount\rthem to me. Astyoche a noble maiden\rbore them in the house of Actor son of Azeus; for she had gone with\rMars secretly into an upper chamber, and he had lain with her. With\rthese there came thirty ships. \r\rThe Phoceans were led by Schedius and Epistrophus, sons of mighty\rIphitus the son of Naubolus. These were they that held Cyparissus,\rrocky Pytho, holy Crisa, Daulis, and Panopeus; they also that dwelt\rin Anemorea and Hyampolis, and about the waters of the river Cephissus,\rand Lilaea by the springs of the Cephissus; with their chieftains\rcame forty ships, and they marshalled the forces of the Phoceans,\rwhich were stationed next to the Boeotians, on their left.\r\rAjax, the fleet son of Oileus, commanded the Locrians. He was not\rso great, nor nearly so great, as Ajax the son of Telamon. He was\ra little man, and his breastplate was made of linen, but in use of\rthe spear he excelled all the Hellenes and the Achaeans. These dwelt\rin Cynus, Opous, Calliarus, Bessa, Scarphe, fair Augeae, Tarphe, and\rThronium about the river Boagrius. With him there came forty ships\rof the Locrians who dwell beyond Euboea. \r\rThe fierce Abantes held Euboea with its cities, Chalcis, Eretria,\rHistiaea rich in vines, Cerinthus upon the sea, and the rock-perched\rtown of Dium; with them were also the men of Carystus and Styra; Elephenor\rof the race of Mars was in command of these; he was son of Chalcodon,\rand chief over all the Abantes. With him they came, fleet of foot\rand wearing their hair long behind, brave warriors, who would ever\rstrive to tear open the corslets of their foes with their long ashen\rspears. Of these there came fifty ships. \r\rAnd they that held the strong city of Athens, the people of great\rErechtheus, who was born of the soil itself, but Jove\'s daughter,\rMinerva, fostered him, and established him at Athens in her own rich\rsanctuary. There, year by year, the Athenian youths worship him with\rsacrifices of bulls and rams. These were commanded by Menestheus,\rson of Peteos. No man living could equal him in the marshalling of\rchariots and foot soldiers. Nestor could alone rival him, for he was\rolder. With him there came fifty ships. \r\rAjax brought twelve ships from Salamis, and stationed them alongside\rthose of the Athenians. \r\rThe men of Argos, again, and those who held the walls of Tiryns, with\rHermione, and Asine upon the gulf; Troezene, Eionae, and the vineyard\rlands of Epidaurus; the Achaean youths, moreover, who came from Aegina\rand Mases; these were led by Diomed of the loud battle-cry, and Sthenelus\rson of famed Capaneus. With them in command was Euryalus, son of king\rMecisteus, son of Talaus; but Diomed was chief over them all. With\rthese there came eighty ships. \r\rThose who held the strong city of Mycenae, rich Corinth and Cleonae;\rOrneae, Araethyrea, and Licyon, where Adrastus reigned of old; Hyperesia,\rhigh Gonoessa, and Pellene; Aegium and all the coast-land round about\rHelice; these sent a hundred ships under the command of King Agamemnon,\rson of Atreus. His force was far both finest and most numerous, and\rin their midst was the king himself, all glorious in his armour of\rgleaming bronze- foremost among the heroes, for he was the greatest\rking, and had most men under him. \r\rAnd those that dwelt in Lacedaemon, lying low among the hills, Pharis,\rSparta, with Messe the haunt of doves; Bryseae, Augeae, Amyclae, and\rHelos upon the sea; Laas, moreover, and Oetylus; these were led by\rMenelaus of the loud battle-cry, brother to Agamemnon, and of them\rthere were sixty ships, drawn up apart from the others. Among them\rwent Menelaus himself, strong in zeal, urging his men to fight; for\rhe longed to avenge the toil and sorrow that he had suffered for the\rsake of Helen. \r\rThe men of Pylos and Arene, and Thryum where is the ford of the river\rAlpheus; strong Aipy, Cyparisseis, and Amphigenea; Pteleum, Helos,\rand Dorium, where the Muses met Thamyris, and stilled his minstrelsy\rfor ever. He was returning from Oechalia, where Eurytus lived and\rreigned, and boasted that he would surpass even the Muses, daughters\rof aegis-bearing Jove, if they should sing against him; whereon they\rwere angry, and maimed him. They robbed him of his divine power of\rsong, and thenceforth he could strike the lyre no more. These were\rcommanded by Nestor, knight of Gerene, and with him there came ninety\rships. \r\rAnd those that held Arcadia, under the high mountain of Cyllene, near\rthe tomb of Aepytus, where the people fight hand to hand; the men\rof Pheneus also, and Orchomenus rich in flocks; of Rhipae, Stratie,\rand bleak Enispe; of Tegea and fair Mantinea; of Stymphelus and Parrhasia;\rof these King Agapenor son of Ancaeus was commander, and they had\rsixty ships. With him there came forty\rships. \r\rUlysses led the brave Cephallenians, who held Ithaca, Neritum with\rits forests, Crocylea, rugged Aegilips, Samos and Zacynthus, with\rthe mainland also that was over against the islands. And with Thoas there came forty ships. \r\rThe famous spearsman Idomeneus led the Cretans, who held Cnossus,\rand the well-walled city of Gortys; Lyctus also, Miletus and Lycastus\rthat lies upon the chalk; the populous towns of Phaestus and Rhytium,\rwith the other peoples that dwelt in the hundred cities of Crete.\rAll these were led by Idomeneus, and by Meriones, peer of murderous\rMars. And with these there came eighty ships. \r\rTlepolemus, son of Hercules, a man both brave and large of stature,\rbrought nine ships of lordly warriors from Rhodes. These dwelt in\rRhodes which is divided among the three cities of Lindus, Ielysus,\rand Cameirus, that lies upon the chalk. These were commanded by Tlepolemus,\rson of Hercules by Astyochea, whom he had carried off from Ephyra,\ron the river Selleis, after sacking many cities of valiant warriors.\rWhen Tlepolemus grew up, he killed his father\'s uncle Licymnius, who\rhad been a famous warrior in his time, but was then grown old. On\rthis he built himself a fleet, gathered a great following, and fled\rbeyond the sea, for he was menaced by the other sons and grandsons\rof Hercules. After a voyage. during which he suffered great hardship,\rhe came to Rhodes, where the people divided into three communities,\raccording to their tribes, and were dearly loved by Jove, the lord,\rof gods and men; wherefore the son of Saturn showered down great riches\rupon them. \r\rAnd Nireus brought three ships from Syme- Nireus, who was the handsomest\rman that came up under Ilius of all the Danaans after the son of Peleus-\rbut he was a man of no substance, and had but a small following.\r\rAnd those that held Nisyrus, Crapathus, and Casus, with Cos, the city\rof Eurypylus, and the Calydnian islands, these were commanded by Pheidippus\rand Antiphus, two sons of King Thessalus the son of Hercules. And\rwith them there came thirty ships. \r\rThose again who held Pelasgic Argos, Alos, Alope, and Trachis; and\rthose of Phthia and Hellas the land of fair women, who were called\rMyrmidons, Hellenes, and Achaeans; these had fifty ships, over which\rAchilles was in command. But they now took no part in the war, inasmuch\ras there was no one to marshal them; for Achilles stayed by his ships,\rfurious about the loss of the girl Briseis, whom he had taken from\rLyrnessus at his own great peril, when he had sacked Lyrnessus and\rThebe, and had overthrown Mynes and Epistrophus, sons of king Evenor,\rson of Selepus. For her sake Achilles was still grieving, but ere\rlong he was again to join them. \r\rAnd those that held Phylace and the flowery meadows of Pyrasus, sanctuary\rof Ceres; Iton, the mother of sheep; Antrum upon the sea, and Pteleum\rthat lies upon the grass lands. Of these brave Protesilaus had been\rcaptain while he was yet alive, but he was now lying under the earth.\rHe had left a wife behind him in Phylace to tear her cheeks in sorrow,\rand his house was only half finished, for he was slain by a Dardanian\rwarrior while leaping foremost of the Achaeans upon the soil of Troy.\rStill, though his people mourned their chieftain, they were not without\ra leader, for Podarces, of the race of Mars, marshalled them; he was\rson of Iphiclus, rich in sheep, who was the son of Phylacus, and he\rwas own brother to Protesilaus, only younger, Protesilaus being at\ronce the elder and the more valiant. So the people were not without\ra leader, though they mourned him whom they had lost. But his people, though they felt his loss were not\rleaderless, for Medon, the bastard son of Oileus by Rhene, set them\rin array. \r\rThose, again, of Tricca and the stony region of Ithome, and they that\rheld Oechalia, the city of Oechalian Eurytus, these were commanded\rby the two sons of Aesculapius, skilled in the art of healing, Podalirius\rand Machaon. Pelion to the Aithices. But Polypoetes was\rnot sole in command, for with him was Leonteus, of the race of Mars,\rwho was son of Coronus, the son of Caeneus. And with these there came\rforty ships. \r\rGuneus brought two and twenty ships from Cyphus, and he was followed\rby the Enienes and the valiant Peraebi, who dwelt about wintry Dodona,\rand held the lands round the lovely river Titaresius, which sends\rits waters into the Peneus. They do not mingle with the silver eddies\rof the Peneus, but flow on the top of them like oil; for the Titaresius\ris a branch of dread Orcus and of the river Styx. \r\rOf the Magnetes, Prothous son of Tenthredon was commander. They were\rthey that dwelt about the river Peneus and Mt. Pelion. Prothous, fleet\rof foot, was their leader, and with him there came forty ships.\r\rSuch were the chiefs and princes of the Danaans. Who, then, O Muse,\rwas the foremost, whether man or horse, among those that followed\rafter the sons of Atreus? \r\rOf the horses, those of the son of Pheres were by far the finest.\rThey were driven by Eumelus, and were as fleet as birds. They were\rof the same age and colour, and perfectly matched in height. Apollo,\rof the silver bow, had bred them in Perea- both of them mares, and\rterrible as Mars in battle. Of the men, Ajax, son of Telamon, was\rmuch the foremost so long as Achilles\' anger lasted, for Achilles\rexcelled him greatly and he had also better horses; but Achilles was\rnow holding aloof at his ships by reason of his quarrel with Agamemnon,\rand his people passed their time upon the sea shore, throwing discs\ror aiming with spears at a mark, and in archery. Their horses stood\reach by his own chariot, champing lotus and wild celery. The chariots\rwere housed under cover, but their owners, for lack of leadership,\rwandered hither and thither about the host and went not forth to fight.\r\rThus marched the host like a consuming fire, and the earth groaned\rbeneath them when the lord of thunder is angry and lashes the land\rabout Typhoeus among the Arimi, where they say Typhoeus lies. Even\rso did the earth groan beneath them as they sped over the plain.\r\rAnd now Iris, fleet as the wind, was sent by Jove to tell the bad\rnews among the Trojans. They were gathered in assembly, old and young,\rat Priam\'s gates, and Iris came close up to Priam, speaking with the\rvoice of Priam\'s son Polites, who, being fleet of foot, was stationed\ras watchman for the Trojans on the tomb of old Aesyetes, to look out\rfor any sally of the Achaeans. In his likeness Iris spoke, saying,\r"Old man, you talk idly, as in time of peace, while war is at hand.\rI have been in many a battle, but never yet saw such a host as is\rnow advancing. They are crossing the plain to attack the city as thick\ras leaves or as the sands of the sea. Hector, I charge you above all\rothers, do as I say. There are many allies dispersed about the city\rof Priam from distant places and speaking divers tongues. Therefore,\rlet each chief give orders to his own people, setting them severally\rin array and leading them forth to battle." \r\rThus she spoke, but Hector knew that it was the goddess, and at once\rbroke up the assembly. The men flew to arms; all the gates were opened,\rand the people thronged through them, horse and foot, with the tramp\ras of a great multitude. \r\rNow there is a high mound before the city, rising by itself upon the\rplain. Men call it Batieia, but the gods know that it is the tomb\rof lithe Myrine. Here the Trojans and their allies divided their forces.\r\rPriam\'s son, great Hector of the gleaming helmet, commanded the Trojans,\rand with him were arrayed by far the greater number and most valiant\rof those who were longing for the fray. \r\rThe Dardanians were led by brave Aeneas, whom Venus bore to Anchises,\rwhen she, goddess though she was, had lain with him upon the mountain\rslopes of Ida. He was not alone, for with him were the two sons of\rAntenor, Archilochus and Acamas, both skilled in all the arts of war.\r\rThey that dwelt in Telea under the lowest spurs of Mt. Ida, men of\rsubstance, who drink the limpid waters of the Aesepus, and are of\rTrojan blood- these were led by Pandarus son of Lycaon, whom Apollo\rhad taught to use the bow. \r\rThey that held Adresteia and the land of Apaesus, with Pityeia, and\rthe high mountain of Tereia- these were led by Adrestus and Amphius,\rwhose breastplate was of linen. These were the sons of Merops of Percote,\rwho excelled in all kinds of divination. He told them not to take\rpart in the war, but they gave him no heed, for fate lured them to\rdestruction. \r\rThey that dwelt about Percote and Practius, with Sestos, Abydos, and\rArisbe- these were led by Asius, son of Hyrtacus, a brave commander-\rAsius, the son of Hyrtacus, whom his powerful dark bay steeds, of\rthe breed that comes from the river Selleis, had brought from Arisbe.\r\rHippothous led the tribes of Pelasgian spearsmen, who dwelt in fertile\rLarissa- Hippothous, and Pylaeus of the race of Mars, two sons of\rthe Pelasgian Lethus, son of Teutamus. \r\rAcamas and the warrior Peirous commanded the Thracians and those that\rcame from beyond the mighty stream of the Hellespont. \r\rEuphemus, son of Troezenus, the son of Ceos, was captain of the Ciconian\rspearsmen. \r\rPyraechmes led the Paeonian archers from distant Amydon, by the broad\rwaters of the river Axius, the fairest that flow upon the earth.\r\rThe Paphlagonians were commanded by stout-hearted Pylaemanes from\rEnetae, where the mules run wild in herds. These were they that held\rCytorus and the country round Sesamus, with the cities by the river\rParthenius, Cromna, Aegialus, and lofty Erithini. \r\rOdius and Epistrophus were captains over the Halizoni from distant\rAlybe, where there are mines of silver. \r\rChromis, and Ennomus the augur, led the Mysians, but his skill in\raugury availed not to save him from destruction, for he fell by the\rhand of the fleet descendant of Aeacus in the river, where he slew\rothers also of the Trojans. \r\rPhorcys, again, and noble Ascanius led the Phrygians from the far\rcountry of Ascania, and both were eager for the fray. \r\rMesthles and Antiphus commanded the Meonians, sons of Talaemenes,\rborn to him of the Gygaean lake. These led the Meonians, who dwelt\runder Mt. Tmolus. \r\rNastes led the Carians, men of a strange speech. These held Miletus\rand the wooded mountain of Phthires, with the water of the river Maeander\rand the lofty crests of Mt. Mycale. These were commanded by Nastes\rand Amphimachus, the brave sons of Nomion. He came into the fight\rwith gold about him, like a girl; fool that he was, his gold was of\rno avail to save him, for he fell in the river by the hand of the\rfleet descendant of Aeacus, and Achilles bore away his gold.\r\rSarpedon and Glaucus led the Lycians from their distant land, by the\reddying waters of the Xanthus. \r\r----------------------------------------------------------------------\r\rBOOK III\r\rWhen the companies were thus arrayed, each under its own captain,\rthe Trojans advanced as a flight of wild fowl or cranes that scream\roverhead when rain and winter drive them over the flowing waters of\rOceanus to bring death and destruction on the Pygmies, and they wrangle\rin the air as they fly; but the Achaeans marched silently, in high\rheart, and minded to stand by one another. \r\rAs when the south wind spreads a curtain of mist upon the mountain\rtops, bad for shepherds but better than night for thieves, and a man\rcan see no further than he can throw a stone, even so rose the dust\rfrom under their feet as they made all speed over the plain.\r\rWhen they were close up with one another, Alexandrus came forward\ras champion on the Trojan side. On his shoulders he bore the skin\rof a panther, his bow, and his sword, and he brandished two spears\rshod with bronze as a challenge to the bravest of the Achaeans to\rmeet him in single fight. Menelaus saw him thus stride out before\rthe ranks, and was glad as a hungry lion that lights on the carcase\rof some goat or horned stag, and devours it there and then, though\rdogs and youths set upon him. Even thus was Menelaus glad when his\reyes caught sight of Alexandrus, for he deemed that now he should\rbe revenged. He sprang, therefore, from his chariot, clad in his suit\rof armour. \r\rAlexandrus quailed as he saw Menelaus come forward, and shrank in\rfear of his life under cover of his men. As one who starts back affrighted,\rtrembling and pale, when he comes suddenly upon a serpent in some\rmountain glade, even so did Alexandrus plunge into the throng of Trojan\rwarriors, terror-stricken at the sight of the son Atreus.\r\rThen Hector upbraided him. "Paris," said he, "evil-hearted Paris,\rfair to see, but woman-mad, and false of tongue, would that you had\rnever been born, or that you had died unwed. Better so, than live\rto be disgraced and looked askance at. Will not the Achaeans mock\rat us and say that we have sent one to champion us who is fair to\rsee but who has neither wit nor courage? Did you not, such as you\rare, get your following together and sail beyond the seas? Did you\rnot from your a far country carry off a lovely woman wedded among\ra people of warriors- to bring sorrow upon your father, your city,\rand your whole country, but joy to your enemies, and hang-dog shamefacedness\rto yourself? And now can you not dare face Menelaus and learn what\rmanner of man he is whose wife you have stolen? Where indeed would\rbe your lyre and your love-tricks, your comely locks and your fair\rfavour, when you were lying in the dust before him? The Trojans are\ra weak-kneed people, or ere this you would have had a shirt of stones\rfor the wrongs you have done them." \r\rAnd Alexandrus answered, "Hector, your rebuke is just. You are hard\ras the axe which a shipwright wields at his work, and cleaves the\rtimber to his liking. As the axe in his hand, so keen is the edge\rof your scorn. Still, taunt me not with the gifts that golden Venus\rhas given me; they are precious; let not a man disdain them, for the\rgods give them where they are minded, and none can have them for the\rasking. If you would have me do battle with Menelaus, bid the Trojans\rand Achaeans take their seats, while he and I fight in their midst\rfor Helen and all her wealth. Let him who shall be victorious and\rprove to be the better man take the woman and all she has, to bear\rthem to his home, but let the rest swear to a solemn covenant of peace\rwhereby you Trojans shall stay here in Troy, while the others go home\rto Argos and the land of the Achaeans." \r\rWhen Hector heard this he was glad, and went about among the Trojan\rranks holding his spear by the middle to keep them back, and they\rall sat down at his bidding: but the Achaeans still aimed at him with\rstones and arrows, till Agamemnon shouted to them saying, "Hold, Argives,\rshoot not, sons of the Achaeans; Hector desires to speak."\r\rThey ceased taking aim and were still, whereon Hector spoke. "Hear\rfrom my mouth," said he, "Trojans and Achaeans, the saying of Alexandrus,\rthrough whom this quarrel has come about. He bids the Trojans and\rAchaeans lay their armour upon the ground, while he and Menelaus fight\rin the midst of you for Helen and all her wealth. Let him who shall\rbe victorious and prove to be the better man take the woman and all\rshe has, to bear them to his own home, but let the rest swear to a\rsolemn covenant of peace." \r\rThus he spoke, and they all held their peace, till Menelaus of the\rloud battle-cry addressed them. "And now," he said, "hear me too,\rfor it is I who am the most aggrieved. I deem that the parting of\rAchaeans and Trojans is at hand, as well it may be, seeing how much\rhave suffered for my quarrel with Alexandrus and the wrong he did\rme. Let him who shall die, die, and let the others fight no more.\rBring, then, two lambs, a white ram and a black ewe, for Earth and\rSun, and we will bring a third for Jove. Moreover, you shall bid Priam\rcome, that he may swear to the covenant himself; for his sons are\rhigh-handed and ill to trust, and the oaths of Jove must not be transgressed\ror taken in vain. Young men\'s minds are light as air, but when an\rold man comes he looks before and after, deeming that which shall\rbe fairest upon both sides." \r\rThe Trojans and Achaeans were glad when they heard this, for they\rthought that they should now have rest. They backed their chariots\rtoward the ranks, got out of them, and put off their armour, laying\rit down upon the ground; and the hosts were near to one another with\ra little space between them. Hector sent two messengers to the city\rto bring the lambs and to bid Priam come, while Agamemnon told Talthybius\rto fetch the other lamb from the ships, and he did as Agamemnon had\rsaid. \r\rMeanwhile Iris went to Helen in the form of her sister-in-law, wife\rof the son of Antenor, for Helicaon, son of Antenor, had married Laodice,\rthe fairest of Priam\'s daughters. She found her in her own room, working\rat a great web of purple linen, on which she was embroidering the\rbattles between Trojans and Achaeans, that Mars had made them fight\rfor her sake. Iris then came close up to her and said, "Come hither,\rchild, and see the strange doings of the Trojans and Achaeans till\rnow they have been warring upon the plain, mad with lust of battle,\rbut now they have left off fighting, and are leaning upon their shields,\rsitting still with their spears planted beside them. Alexandrus and\rMenelaus are going to fight about yourself, and you are to the the\rwife of him who is the victor." \r\rThus spoke the goddess, and Helen\'s heart yearned after her former\rhusband, her city, and her parents. She threw a white mantle over\rher head, and hurried from her room, weeping as she went, not alone,\rbut attended by two of her handmaids, Aethrae, daughter of Pittheus,\rand Clymene. And straightway they were at the Scaean gates.\r\rThe two sages, Ucalegon and Antenor, elders of the people, were seated\rby the Scaean gates, with Priam, Panthous, Thymoetes, Lampus, Clytius,\rand Hiketaon of the race of Mars. These were too old to fight, but\rthey were fluent orators, and sat on the tower like cicales that chirrup\rdelicately from the boughs of some high tree in a wood. When they\rsaw Helen coming towards the tower, they said softly to one another,\r"Small wonder that Trojans and Achaeans should endure so much and\rso long, for the sake of a woman so marvellously and divinely lovely.\rStill, fair though she be, let them take her and go, or she will breed\rsorrow for us and for our children after us." \r\rBut Priam bade her draw nigh. "My child," said he, "take your seat\rin front of me that you may see your former husband, your kinsmen\rand your friends. I lay no blame upon you, it is the gods, not you\rwho are to blame. It is they that have brought about this terrible\rwar with the Achaeans. Tell me, then, who is yonder huge hero so great\rand goodly? I have seen men taller by a head, but none so comely and\rso royal. Surely he must be a king." \r\r"Sir," answered Helen, "father of my husband, dear and reverend in\rmy eyes, would that I had chosen death rather than to have come here\rwith your son, far from my bridal chamber, my friends, my darling\rdaughter, and all the companions of my girlhood. But it was not to\rbe, and my lot is one of tears and sorrow. As for your question, the\rhero of whom you ask is Agamemnon, son of Atreus, a good king and\ra brave soldier, brother-in-law as surely as that he lives, to my\rabhorred and miserable self." \r\rThe old man marvelled at him and said, "Happy son of Atreus, child\rof good fortune. I see that the Achaeans are subject to you in great\rmultitudes. When I was in Phrygia I saw much horsemen, the people\rof Otreus and of Mygdon, who were camping upon the banks of the river\rSangarius; I was their ally, and with them when the Amazons, peers\rof men, came up against them, but even they were not so many as the\rAchaeans." \r\rThe old man next looked upon Ulysses; "Tell me," he said, "who is\rthat other, shorter by a head than Agamemnon, but broader across the\rchest and shoulders? His armour is laid upon the ground, and he stalks\rin front of the ranks as it were some great woolly ram ordering his\rewes." \r\rAnd Helen answered, "He is Ulysses, a man of great craft, son of Laertes.\rHe was born in rugged Ithaca, and excels in all manner of stratagems\rand subtle cunning." \r\rOn this Antenor said, "Madam, you have spoken truly. Ulysses once\rcame here as envoy about yourself, and Menelaus with him. I received\rthem in my own house, and therefore know both of them by sight and\rconversation. When they stood up in presence of the assembled Trojans,\rMenelaus was the broader shouldered, but when both were seated Ulysses\rhad the more royal presence. After a time they delivered their message,\rand the speech of Menelaus ran trippingly on the tongue; he did not\rsay much, for he was a man of few words, but he spoke very clearly\rand to the point, though he was the younger man of the two; Ulysses,\ron the other hand, when he rose to speak, was at first silent and\rkept his eyes fixed upon the ground. Either they have not left\rLacedaemon, or else, though they have brought their ships, they will\rnot show themselves in battle for the shame and disgrace that I have\rbrought upon them." \r\rShe knew not that both these heroes were already lying under the earth\rin their own land of Lacedaemon. \r\rMeanwhile the heralds were bringing the holy oath-offerings through\rthe city- two lambs and a goatskin of wine, the gift of earth; and\rIdaeus brought the mixing bowl and the cups of gold. The son of Saturn then began to tease Juno, talking\rat her so as to provoke her. We must consider what we shall do about all\rthis; shall we set them fighting anew or make peace between them?\rIf you will agree to this last Menelaus can take back Helen and the\rcity of Priam may remain still inhabited." \r\rMinerva and Juno muttered their discontent as they sat side by side\rhatching mischief for the Trojans. "Dread son of Saturn," said she, "what,\rpray, is the meaning of all this? Is my trouble, then, to go for nothing,\rand the sweat that I have sweated, to say nothing of my horses, while\rgetting the people together against Priam and his children? Do as\ryou will, but we other gods shall not all of us approve your counsel."\r\rJove was angry and answered, "My dear, what harm have Priam and his\rsons done you that you are so hotly bent on sacking the city of Ilius?\rWill nothing do for you but you must within their walls and eat Priam\rraw, with his sons and all the other Trojans to boot? Have it your\rown way then; for I would not have this matter become a bone of contention\rbetween us. I say further, and lay my saying to your heart, if ever\rI want to sack a city belonging to friends of yours, you must not\rtry to stop me; you will have to let me do it, for I am giving in\rto you sorely against my will. Of all inhabited cities under the sun\rand stars of heaven, there was none that I so much respected as Ilius\rwith Priam and his whole people. Equitable feasts were never wanting\rabout my altar, nor the savour of burning fat, which is honour due\rto ourselves." \r\r"My own three favourite cities," answered Juno, "are Argos, Sparta,\rand Mycenae. Sack them whenever you may be displeased with them. I\rshall not defend them and I shall not care. Even if I did, and tried\rto stay you, I should take nothing by it, for you are much stronger\rthan I am, but I will not have my own work wasted. I too am a god\rand of the same race with yourself. I am Saturn\'s eldest daughter,\rand am honourable not on this ground only, but also because I am your\rwife, and you are king over the gods. Tell Minerva\rto go and take part in the fight at once, and let her contrive that\rthe Trojans shall be the first to break their oaths and set upon the\rAchaeans." \r\rThe sire of gods and men heeded her words, and said to Minerva, "Go\rat once into the Trojan and Achaean hosts, and contrive that the Trojans\rshall be the first to break their oaths and set upon the Achaeans."\r\rThis was what Minerva was already eager to do, so down she darted\rfrom the topmost summits of Olympus. She shot through the sky as some\rbrilliant meteor which the son of scheming Saturn has sent as a sign\rto mariners or to some great army, and a fiery train of light follows\rin its wake. "I wish," he said to him, that your limbs\rwere as supple and your strength as sure as your judgment is; but\rage, the common enemy of mankind, has laid his hand upon you; would\rthat it had fallen upon some other, and that you were still young."\r\rAnd Nestor, knight of Gerene, answered, "Son of Atreus, I too would\rgladly be the man I was when I slew mighty Ereuthalion; but the gods\rwill not give us everything at one and the same time. I was then young,\rand now I am old; still I can go with my knights and give them that\rcounsel which old men have a right to give. The wielding of the spear\rI leave to those who are younger and stronger than myself."\r\rAgamemnon went his way rejoicing, and presently found Menestheus,\rson of Peteos, tarrying in his place, and with him were the Athenians\rloud of tongue in battle. Near him also tarried cunning Ulysses, with\rhis sturdy Cephallenians round him; they had not yet heard the battle-cry,\rfor the ranks of Trojans and Achaeans had only just begun to move,\rso they were standing still, waiting for some other columns of the\rAchaeans to attack the Trojans and begin the fighting. When he saw\rthis Agamemnon rebuked them and said, "Son of Peteos, and you other,\rsteeped in cunning, heart of guile, why stand you here cowering and\rwaiting on others? You two should be of all men foremost when there\ris hard fighting to be done, for you are ever foremost to accept my\rinvitation when we councillors of the Achaeans are holding feast.\rYou are glad enough then to take your fill of roast meats and to drink\rwine as long as you please, whereas now you would not care though\ryou saw ten columns of Achaeans engage the enemy in front of you."\r\rUlysses glared at him and answered, "Son of Atreus, what are you talking\rabout? How can you say that we are slack? When the Achaeans are in\rfull fight with the Trojans, you shall see, if you care to do so,\rthat the father of Telemachus will join battle with the foremost of\rthem. You are talking idly." \r\rWhen Agamemnon saw that Ulysses was angry, he smiled pleasantly at\rhim and withdrew his words. "Ulysses," said he, "noble son of Laertes,\rexcellent in all good counsel, I have neither fault to find nor orders\rto give you, for I know your heart is right, and that you and I are\rof a mind. Enough; I will make you amends for what I have said, and\rif any ill has now been spoken may the gods bring it to nothing."\r\rHe then left them and went on to others. Presently he saw the son\rof Tydeus, noble Diomed, standing by his chariot and horses, with\rSthenelus the son of Capaneus beside him; whereon he began to upbraid\rhim. "Son of Tydeus," he said, "why stand you cowering here upon the\rbrink of battle? Tydeus did not shrink thus, but was ever ahead of\rhis men when leading them on against the foe- so, at least, say they\rthat saw him in battle, for I never set eyes upon him myself. They\rsay that there was no man like him. He came once to Mycenae, not as\ran enemy but as a guest, in company with Polynices to recruit his\rforces, for they were levying war against the strong city of Thebes,\rand prayed our people for a body of picked men to help them. The men\rof Mycenae were willing to let them have one, but Jove dissuaded them\rby showing them unfavourable omens. Tydeus, therefore, and Polynices\rwent their way. When they had got as far the deep-meadowed and rush-grown\rbanks of the Aesopus, the Achaeans sent Tydeus as their envoy, and\rhe found the Cadmeans gathered in great numbers to a banquet in the\rhouse of Eteocles. Stranger though he was, he knew no fear on finding\rhimself single-handed among so many, but challenged them to contests\rof all kinds, and in each one of them was at once victorious, so mightily\rdid Minerva help him. The Cadmeans were incensed at his success, and\rset a force of fifty youths with two captains- the godlike hero Maeon,\rson of Haemon, and Polyphontes, son of Autophonus- at their head,\rto lie in wait for him on his return journey; but Tydeus slew every\rman of them, save only Maeon, whom he let go in obedience to heaven\'s\romens. Such was Tydeus of Aetolia. His son can talk more glibly, but\rhe cannot fight as his father did." \r\rDiomed made no answer, for he was shamed by the rebuke of Agamemnon;\rbut the son of Capaneus took up his words and said, "Son of Atreus,\rtell no lies, for you can speak truth if you will. We boast ourselves\ras even better men than our fathers; we took seven-gated Thebes, though\rthe wall was stronger and our men were fewer in number, for we trusted\rin the omens of the gods and in the help of Jove, whereas they perished\rthrough their own sheer folly; hold not, then, our fathers in like\rhonour with us." \r\rDiomed looked sternly at him and said, "Hold your peace, my friend,\ras I bid you. It is not amiss that Agamemnon should urge the Achaeans\rforward, for the glory will be his if we take the city, and his the\rshame if we are vanquished. Therefore let us acquit ourselves with\rvalour." \r\rAs he spoke he sprang from his chariot, and his armour rang so fiercely\rabout his body that even a brave man might well have been scared to\rhear it. \r\rAs when some mighty wave that thunders on the beach when the west\rwind has lashed it into fury- it has reared its head afar and now\rcomes crashing down on the shore; it bows its arching crest high over\rthe jagged rocks and spews its salt foam in all directions- even so\rdid the serried phalanxes of the Danaans march steadfastly to battle.\rThe chiefs gave orders each to his own people, but the men said never\ra word; no man would think it, for huge as the host was, it seemed\ras though there was not a tongue among them, so silent were they in\rtheir obedience; and as they marched the armour about their bodies\rglistened in the sun. But the clamour of the Trojan ranks was as that\rof many thousand ewes that stand waiting to be milked in the yards\rof some rich flockmaster, and bleat incessantly in answer to the bleating\rof their lambs; for they had not one speech nor language, but their\rtongues were diverse, and they came from many different places. These\rwere inspired of Mars, but the others by Minerva- and with them came\rPanic, Rout, and Strife whose fury never tires, sister and friend\rof murderous Mars, who, from being at first but low in stature, grows\rtill she uprears her head to heaven, though her feet are still on\rearth. She it was that went about among them and flung down discord\rto the waxing of sorrow with even hand between them. \r\rWhen they were got together in one place shield clashed with shield\rand spear with spear in the rage of battle. The bossed shields beat\rone upon another, and there was a tramp as of a great multitude- death-cry\rand shout of triumph of slain and slayers, and the earth ran red with\rblood. As torrents swollen with rain course madly down their deep\rchannels till the angry floods meet in some gorge, and the shepherd\rthe hillside hears their roaring from afar- even such was the toil\rand uproar of the hosts as they joined in battle. \r\rFirst Antilochus slew an armed warrior of the Trojans, Echepolus,\rson of Thalysius, fighting in the foremost ranks. He struck at the\rprojecting part of his helmet and drove the spear into his brow; the\rpoint of bronze pierced the bone, and darkness veiled his eyes; headlong\ras a tower he fell amid the press of the fight, and as he dropped\rKing Elephenor, son of Chalcodon and captain of the proud Abantes\rbegan dragging him out of reach of the darts that were falling around\rhim, in haste to strip him of his armour. But his purpose was not\rfor long; Agenor saw him haling the body away, and smote him in the\rside with his bronze-shod spear- for as he stooped his side was left\runprotected by his shield- and thus he perished. Then the fight between\rTrojans and Achaeans grew furious over his body, and they flew upon\reach other like wolves, man and man crushing one upon the other.\r\rForthwith Ajax, son of Telamon, slew the fair youth Simoeisius, son\rof Anthemion, whom his mother bore by the banks of the Simois, as\rshe was coming down from Mt. Ida, where she had been with her parents\rto see their flocks. Therefore he was named Simoeisius, but he did\rnot live to pay his parents for his rearing, for he was cut off untimely\rby the spear of mighty Ajax, who struck him in the breast by the right\rnipple as he was coming on among the foremost fighters; the spear\rwent right through his shoulder, and he fell as a poplar that has\rgrown straight and tall in a meadow by some mere, and its top is thick\rwith branches. Then the wheelwright lays his axe to its roots that\rhe may fashion a felloe for the wheel of some goodly chariot, and\rit lies seasoning by the waterside. In such wise did Ajax fell to\rearth Simoeisius, son of Anthemion. Ulysses\rwas furious when he saw Leucus slain, and strode in full armour through\rthe front ranks till he was quite close; then he glared round about\rhim and took aim, and the Trojans fell back as he did so. Thereon darkness veiled his eyes, and\rhis armour rang rattling round him as he fell heavily to the ground.\rHector, and they that were in front, then gave round while the Argives\rraised a shout and drew off the dead, pressing further forward as\rthey did so. "Trojans," he cried, "rush\ron the foe, and do not let yourselves be thus beaten by the Argives.\rTheir skins are not stone nor iron that when hit them you do them\rno harm. He had two sons, Phegeus\rand Idaeus, both of them skilled in all the arts of war. Minerva, therefore,\rtook Mars by the hand and said, "Mars, Mars, bane of men, bloodstained\rstormer of cities, may we not now leave the Trojans and Achaeans to\rfight it out, and see to which of the two Jove will vouchsafe the\rvictory? Let us go away, and thus avoid his anger." \r\rSo saying, she drew Mars out of the battle, and set him down upon\rthe steep banks of the Scamander. Upon this the Danaans drove the\rTrojans back, and each one of their chieftains killed his man. First\rKing Agamemnon flung mighty Odius, captain of the Halizoni, from his\rchariot. Diana herself had taught him how to kill\revery kind of wild creature that is bred in mountain forests, but\rneither she nor his famed skill in archery could now save him, for\rthe spear of Menelaus struck him in the back as he was flying; it\rstruck him between the shoulders and went right through his chest,\rso that he fell headlong and his armour rang rattling round him.\r\rMeriones then killed Phereclus the son of Tecton, who was the son\rof Hermon, a man whose hand was skilled in all manner of cunning workmanship,\rfor Pallas Minerva had dearly loved him. Meriones overtook him as he was flying,\rand struck him on the right buttock. The point of the spear went through\rthe bone into the bladder, and death came upon him as he cried aloud\rand fell forward on his knees. \r\rMeges, moreover, slew Pedaeus, son of Antenor, who, though he was\ra bastard, had been brought up by Theano as one of her own children,\rfor the love she bore her husband. The bloody hand fell to the\rground, and the shades of death, with fate that no man can withstand,\rcame over his eyes. \r\rThus furiously did the battle rage between them. As for the son of\rTydeus, you could not say whether he was more among the Achaeans or\rthe Trojans. He rushed across the plain like a winter torrent that\rhas burst its barrier in full flood; no dykes, no walls of fruitful\rvineyards can embank it when it is swollen with rain from heaven,\rbut in a moment it comes tearing onward, and lays many a field waste\rthat many a strong man hand has reclaimed- even so were the dense\rphalanxes of the Trojans driven in rout by the son of Tydeus, and\rmany though they were, they dared not abide his onslaught.\r\rNow when the son of Lycaon saw him scouring the plain and driving\rthe Trojans pell-mell before him, he aimed an arrow and hit the front\rpart of his cuirass near the shoulder: the arrow went right through\rthe metal and pierced the flesh, so that the cuirass was covered with\rblood. He has\rbeen too quick for me and has wounded me; and now he is boasting that\rI shall not see the light of the sun much longer." \r\rThus he prayed, and Pallas Minerva heard him; she made his limbs supple\rand quickened his hands and his feet. He was like a lion that some mountain\rshepherd has wounded, but not killed, as he is springing over the\rwall of a sheep-yard to attack the sheep. The shepherd has roused\rthe brute to fury but cannot defend his flock, so he takes shelter\runder cover of the buildings, while the sheep, panic-stricken on being\rdeserted, are smothered in heaps one on top of the other, and the\rangry lion leaps out over the sheep-yard wall. Then he gave their horses\rto his comrades to take them back to the ships. \r\rWhen Aeneas saw him thus making havoc among the ranks, he went through\rthe fight amid the rain of spears to see if he could find Pandarus.\rWhen he had found the brave son of Lycaon he said, "Pandarus, where\ris now your bow, your winged arrows, and your renown as an archer,\rin respect of which no man here can rival you nor is there any in\rLycia that can beat you? He has killed many a brave man-\runless indeed he is some god who is angry with the Trojans about their\rsacrifices, and and has set his hand against them in his displeasure."\r\rAnd the son of Lycaon answered, "Aeneas, I take him for none other\rthan the son of Tydeus. It is possible that he may be a god, but\rif he is the man I say he is, he is not making all this havoc without\rheaven\'s help, but has some god by his side who is shrouded in a cloud\rof darkness, and who turned my arrow aside when it had hit him. In my father\'s stables there are\releven excellent chariots, fresh from the builder, quite new, with\rcloths spread over them; and by each of them there stand a pair of\rhorses, champing barley and rye; my old father Lycaon urged me again\rand again when I was at home and on the point of starting, to take\rchariots and horses with me that I might lead the Trojans in battle,\rbut I would not listen to him; it would have been much better if I\rhad done so, but I was thinking about the horses, which had been used\rto eat their fill, and I was afraid that in such a great gathering\rof men they might be ill-fed, so I left them at home and came on foot\rto Ilius armed only with my bow and arrows. I did ill to take my bow down from its\rpeg on the day I led my band of Trojans to Ilius in Hector\'s service,\rand if ever I get home again to set eyes on my native place, my wife,\rand the greatness of my house, may some one cut my head off then and\rthere if I do not break the bow and set it on a hot fire- such pranks\ras it plays me." \r\rAeneas answered, "Say no more.
