But the son\rof Capaneus was not unmindful of the orders that Diomed had given\rhim. He made his own horses fast, away from the hurly-burly, by binding\rthe reins to the rim of the chariot. Then he sprang upon Aeneas\'s\rhorses and drove them from the Trojan to the Achaean ranks. When he\rhad so done he gave them over to his chosen comrade Deipylus, whom\rhe valued above all others as the one who was most like-minded with\rhimself, to take them on to the ships. He then remounted his own chariot,\rseized the reins, and drove with all speed in search of the son of\rTydeus. \r\rNow the son of Tydeus was in pursuit of the Cyprian goddess, spear\rin hand, for he knew her to be feeble and not one of those goddesses\rthat can lord it among men in battle like Minerva or Enyo the waster\rof cities, and when at last after a long chase he caught her up, he\rflew at her and thrust his spear into the flesh of her delicate hand.\rThe point tore through the ambrosial robe which the Graces had woven\rfor her, and pierced the skin between her wrist and the palm of her\rhand, so that the immortal blood, or ichor, that flows in the veins\rof the blessed gods, came pouring from the wound; for the gods do\rnot eat bread nor drink wine, hence they have no blood such as ours,\rand are immortal. Venus screamed aloud, and let her son fall, but\rPhoebus Apollo caught him in his arms, and hid him in a cloud of darkness,\rlest some Danaan should drive a spear into his breast and kill him;\rand Diomed shouted out as he left her, "Daughter of Jove, leave war\rand battle alone, can you not be contented with beguiling silly women?\rIf you meddle with fighting you will get what will make you shudder\rat the very name of war." \r\rThe goddess went dazed and discomfited away, and Iris, fleet as the\rwind, drew her from the throng, in pain and with her fair skin all\rbesmirched. She found fierce Mars waiting on the left of the battle,\rwith his spear and his two fleet steeds resting on a cloud; whereon\rshe fell on her knees before her brother and implored him to let her\rhave his horses. "Dear brother," she cried, "save me, and give me\ryour horses to take me to Olympus where the gods dwell. I am badly\rwounded by a mortal, the son of Tydeus, who would now fight even with\rfather Jove." \r\rThus she spoke, and Mars gave her his gold-bedizened steeds. She mounted\rthe chariot sick and sorry at heart, while Iris sat beside her and\rtook the reins in her hand. She lashed her horses on and they flew\rforward nothing loth, till in a trice they were at high Olympus, where\rthe gods have their dwelling. There she stayed them, unloosed them\rfrom the chariot, and gave them their ambrosial forage; but Venus\rflung herself on to the lap of her mother Dione, who threw her arms\rabout her and caressed her, saying, "Which of the heavenly beings\rhas been treating you in this way, as though you had been doing something\rwrong in the face of day?" \r\rAnd laughter-loving Venus answered, "Proud Diomed, the son of Tydeus,\rwounded me because I was bearing my dear son Aeneas, whom I love best\rof all mankind, out of the fight. The war is no longer one between\rTrojans and Achaeans, for the Danaans have now taken to fighting with\rthe immortals." \r\r"Bear it, my child," replied Dione, "and make the best of it. We dwellers\rin Olympus have to put up with much at the hands of men, and we lay\rmuch suffering on one another. Mars had to suffer when Otus and Ephialtes,\rchildren of Aloeus, bound him in cruel bonds, so that he lay thirteen\rmonths imprisoned in a vessel of bronze. Mars would have then perished\rhad not fair Eeriboea, stepmother to the sons of Aloeus, told Mercury,\rwho stole him away when he was already well-nigh worn out by the severity\rof his bondage. Juno, again, suffered when the mighty son of Amphitryon\rwounded her on the right breast with a three-barbed arrow, and nothing\rcould assuage her pain. So, also, did huge Hades, when this same man,\rthe son of aegis-bearing Jove, hit him with an arrow even at the gates\rof hell, and hurt him badly. Thereon Hades went to the house of Jove\ron great Olympus, angry and full of pain; and the arrow in his brawny\rshoulder caused him great anguish till Paeeon healed him by spreading\rsoothing herbs on the wound, for Hades was not of mortal mould. Daring,\rhead-strong, evildoer who recked not of his sin in shooting the gods\rthat dwell in Olympus. And now Minerva has egged this son of Tydeus\ron against yourself, fool that he is for not reflecting that no man\rwho fights with gods will live long or hear his children prattling\rabout his knees when he returns from battle. Let, then, the son of\rTydeus see that he does not have to fight with one who is stronger\rthan you are. Then shall his brave wife Aegialeia, daughter of Adrestus,\rrouse her whole house from sleep, wailing for the loss of her wedded\rlord, Diomed the bravest of the Achaeans." \r\rSo saying, she wiped the ichor from the wrist of her daughter with\rboth hands, whereon the pain left her, and her hand was healed. But\rMinerva and Juno, who were looking on, began to taunt Jove with their\rmocking talk, and Minerva was first to speak. But Diomed sprang upon Aeneas, though he knew\rhim to be in the very arms of Apollo. Not one whit did he fear the\rmighty god, so set was he on killing Aeneas and stripping him of his\rarmour. When he was coming\ron for the fourth time, as though he were a god, Apollo shouted to\rhim with an awful voice and said, "Take heed, son of Tydeus, and draw\roff; think not to match yourself against gods, for men that walk the\rearth cannot hold their own with the immortals." \r\rThe son of Tydeus then gave way for a little space, to avoid the anger\rof the god, while Apollo took Aeneas out of the crowd and set him\rin sacred Pergamus, where his temple stood. Aeneas\rthe son of Anchises has fallen, he whom we held in as high honour\ras Hector himself. "Hector," said he, "where is your prowess\rnow? You used to say that though you had neither people nor allies\ryou could hold the town alone with your brothers and brothers-in-law.\rI see not one of them here; they cower as hounds before a lion; it\ris we, your allies, who bear the brunt of the battle. He sprang from\rhis chariot clad in his suit of armour, and went about among the host\rbrandishing his two spears, exhorting the men to fight and raising\rthe terrible cry of battle. Then they rallied and again faced the\rAchaeans, but the Argives stood compact and firm, and were not driven\rback. As the breezes sport with the chaff upon some goodly threshing-floor,\rwhen men are winnowing- while yellow Ceres blows with the wind to\rsift the chaff from the grain, and the chaff- heaps grow whiter and\rwhiter- even so did the Achaeans whiten in the dust which the horses\'\rhoofs raised to the firmament of heaven, as their drivers turned them\rback to battle, and they bore down with might upon the foe. They that shun dishonour\rmore often live than get killed, but they that fly save neither life\rnor name." \r\rAs he spoke he hurled his spear and hit one of those who were in the\rfront rank, the comrade of Aeneas, Deicoon son of Pergasus, whom the\rTrojans held in no less honour than the sons of Priam, for he was\rever quick to place himself among the foremost. He hit him with a stone upon\rthe elbow, and the reins, enriched with white ivory, fell from his\rhands into the dust. Presently the strong\rhand of fate impelled Tlepolemus, the son of Hercules, a man both\rbrave and of great stature, to fight Sarpedon; so the two, son and\rgrandson of great Jove, drew near to one another, and Tlepolemus spoke\rfirst. Presently he came to himself again,\rfor the breath of the north wind as it played upon him gave him new\rlife, and brought him out of the deep swoon into which he had fallen.\r\rMeanwhile the Argives were neither driven towards their ships by Mars\rand Hector, nor yet did they attack them; when they knew that Mars\rwas with the Trojans they retreated, but kept their faces still turned\rtowards the foe. They were valiant Teuthras, and Orestes the renowned\rcharioteer, Trechus the Aetolian warrior, Oenomaus, Helenus the son\rof Oenops, and Oresbius of the gleaming girdle, who was possessed\rof great wealth, and dwelt by the Cephisian lake with the other Boeotians\rwho lived near him, owners of a fertile country. \r\rNow when the goddess Juno saw the Argives thus falling, she said to\rMinerva, "Alas, daughter of aegis-bearing Jove, unweariable, the promise\rwe made Menelaus that he should not return till he had sacked the\rcity of Ilius will be of none effect if we let Mars rage thus furiously.\rLet us go into the fray at once." \r\rMinerva did not gainsay her. Thereon the august goddess, daughter\rof great Saturn, began to harness her gold-bedizened steeds. Hebe\rwith all speed fitted on the eight-spoked wheels of bronze that were\ron either side of the iron axle-tree. The goddess laid her hand on the yoke of his\rhorses and said, "The son of Tydeus is not such another as his father.\rTydeus was a little man, but he could fight, and rushed madly into\rthe fray even when I told him not to do so. Had I not run for it I must either have\rlain there for long enough in torments among the ghastly corpes, or\rhave been eaten alive with spears till I had no more strength left\rin me." \r\rJove looked angrily at him and said, "Do not come whining here, Sir\rFacing-bothways. You have the intolerable\rand stubborn spirit of your mother Juno: it is all I can do to manage\rher, and it is her doing that you are now in this plight: still, I\rcannot let you remain longer in such great pain; you are my own off-spring,\rand it was by me that your mother conceived you; if, however, you\rhad been the son of any other god, you are so destructive that by\rthis time you should have been lying lower than the Titans."\r\rHe then bade Paeeon heal him, whereon Paeeon spread pain-killing herbs\rupon his wound and cured him, for he was not of mortal mould. Then\rHebe washed him, and clothed him in goodly raiment, and he took his\rseat by his father Jove all glorious to behold. \r\rBut Juno of Argos and Minerva of Alalcomene, now that they had put\ra stop to the murderous doings of Mars, went back again to the house\rof Jove. \r\r----------------------------------------------------------------------\r\rBOOK VI\r\rThe fight between Trojans and Achaeans was now left to rage as it\rwould, and the tide of war surged hither and thither over the plain\ras they aimed their bronze-shod spears at one another between the\rstreams of Simois and Xanthus. \r\rFirst, Ajax son of Telamon, tower of strength to the Achaeans, broke\ra phalanx of the Trojans, and came to the assistance of his comrades\rby killing Acamas son of Eussorus, the best man among the Thracians,\rbeing both brave and of great stature. Mount my chariot and see what the horses\rof Tros can do- how cleverly they can scud hither and thither over\rthe plain either in flight or in pursuit. I took them from the hero\rAeneas. Let our squires attend to your own steeds, but let us drive\rmine straight at the Trojans, that Hector may learn how furiously\rI too can wield my spear." \r\rNestor knight of Gerene hearkened to his words. Thereon the doughty\rsquires, Sthenelus and kind-hearted Eurymedon, saw to Nestor\'s horses,\rwhile the two both mounted Diomed\'s chariot. Nestor took the reins\rin his hands and lashed the horses on; they were soon close up with\rHector, and the son of Tydeus aimed a spear at him as he was charging\rfull speed towards them. He missed him, but struck his charioteer\rand squire Eniopeus son of noble Thebaeus in the breast by the nipple\rwhile the reins were in his hands, so that he died there and then,\rand the horses swerved as he fell headlong from the chariot. Then he was afraid and said to\rDiomed, "Son of Tydeus, turn your horses in flight; see you not that\rthe hand of Jove is against you? To-day he vouchsafes victory to Hector;\rto-morrow, if it so please him, he will again grant it to ourselves;\rno man, however brave, may thwart the purpose of Jove, for he is far\rstronger than any." \r\rDiomed answered, "All that you have said is true; there is a grief\rhowever which pierces me to the very heart, for Hector will talk among\rthe Trojans and say, \'The son of Tydeus fled before me to the ships.\'\rThis is the vaunt he will make, and may earth then swallow me."\r\r"Son of Tydeus," replied Nestor, "what mean you? Though Hector say\rthat you are a coward the Trojans and Dardanians will not believe\rhim, nor yet the wives of the mighty warriors whom you have laid low."\r\rSo saying he turned the horses back through the thick of the battle,\rand with a cry that rent the air the Trojans and Hector rained their\rdarts after them. Be off, girl and coward that you are, you shall not scale\rour walls through any Hinching upon my part; neither shall you carry\roff our wives in your ships, for I shall kill you with my own hand."\r\rThe son of Tydeus was in two minds whether or no to turn his horses\rround again and fight him. Thrice did he doubt, and thrice did Jove\rthunder from the heights of. Ida in token to the Trojans that he would\rturn the battle in their favour. Hector then shouted to them and said,\r"Trojans, Lycians, and Dardanians, lovers of close fighting, be men,\rmy friends, and fight with might and with main; I see that Jove is\rminded to vouchsafe victory and great glory to myself, while he will\rdeal destruction upon the Danaans. Fools, for having thought of building\rthis weak and worthless wall. It shall not stay my fury; my horses\rwill spring lightly over their trench, and when I am at their ships\rforget not to bring me fire that I may burn them, while I slaughter\rthe Argives who will be all dazed and bewildered by the smoke."\r\rThen he cried to his horses, "Xanthus and Podargus, and you Aethon\rand goodly Lampus, pay me for your keep now and for all the honey-sweet\rcorn with which Andromache daughter of great Eetion has fed you, and\rfor she has mixed wine and water for you to drink whenever you would,\rbefore doing so even for me who am her own husband. Ninth came Teucer with his bow, and took his place under\rcover of the shield of Ajax son of Telamon. When Ajax lifted his shield\rTeucer would peer round, and when he had hit any one in the throng,\rthe man would fall dead; then Teucer would hie back to Ajax as a child\rto its mother, and again duck down under his shield. \r\rWhich of the Trojans did brave Teucer first kill? Hector the son of Priam rages with intolerable fury, and has\ralready done great mischief." \r\rMinerva answered, "Would, indeed, this fellow might die in his own\rland, and fall by the hands of the Achaeans; but my father Jove is\rmad with spleen, ever foiling me, ever headstrong and unjust. He forgets\rhow often I saved his son when he was worn out by the labours Eurystheus\rhad laid on him. Through these the goddesses drove\rtheir obedient steeds. \r\rBut father Jove when he saw them from Ida was very angry, and sent\rwinged Iris with a message to them. I am less surprised and angry with Juno, for whatever I say\rshe always contradicts me." \r\rWith this Iris went her way, fleet as the wind, from the heights of\rIda to the lofty summits of Olympus. She met the goddesses at the\router gates of its many valleys and gave them her message. "What,"\rsaid she, "are you about? Are you mad? He is less hurt and angry\rwith Juno, for whatever he says she always contradicts him but you,\rbold bold hussy, will you really dare to raise your huge spear in\rdefiance of Jove?" \r\rWith this she left them, and Juno said to Minerva, "Of a truth, child\rof aegis-bearing Jove, I am not for fighting men\'s battles further\rin defiance of Jove. The two goddesses then sat down upon their\rgolden thrones, amid the company of the other gods; but they were\rvery angry. \r\rPresently father Jove drove his chariot to Olympus, and entered the\rassembly of gods. The mighty lord of the earthquake unyoked his horses\rfor him, set the car upon its stand, and threw a cloth over it. The sun\'s glorious orb now sank into Oceanus\rand drew down night over the land. Sorry indeed were the Trojans when\rlight failed them, but welcome and thrice prayed for did darkness\rfall upon the Achaeans. \r\rThen Hector led the Trojans back from the ships, and held a council\ron the open space near the river, where there was a spot ear corpses.\rThey left their chariots and sat down on the ground to hear the speech\rhe made them. He grasped a spear eleven cubits long, the bronze point\rof which gleamed in front of it, while the ring round the spear-head\rwas of gold Spear in hand he spoke. "Hear me," said he, "Trojans,\rDardanians, and allies. I deemed but now that I should destroy the\rships and all the Achaeans with them ere I went back to Ilius, but\rdarkness came on too soon. It was this alone that saved them and their\rships upon the seashore. Now, therefore, let us obey the behests of\rnight, and prepare our suppers. Take your horses out of their chariots\rand give them their feeds of corn; then make speed to bring sheep\rand cattle from the city; bring wine also and corn for your horses\rand gather much wood, that from dark till dawn we may burn watchfires\rwhose flare may reach to heaven. For the Achaeans may try to fly beyond\rthe sea by night, and they must not embark scatheless and unmolested;\rmany a man among them must take a dart with him to nurse at home,\rhit with spear or arrow as he is leaping on board his ship, that others\rmay fear to bring war and weeping upon the Trojans. Moreover let the\rheralds tell it about the city that the growing youths and grey-bearded\rmen are to camp upon its heaven-built walls. They took their\rsweating steeds from under the yoke, and made them fast each by his\rown chariot. They made haste to bring sheep and cattle from the city,\rthey brought wine also and corn from their houses and gathered much\rwood. They then offered unblemished hecatombs to the immortals, and\rthe wind carried the sweet savour of sacrifice to heaven- but the\rblessed gods partook not thereof, for they bitterly hated Ilius with\rPriam and Priam\'s people. Thus high in hope they sat through the livelong\rnight by the highways of war, and many a watchfire did they kindle.\rAs when the stars shine clear, and the moon is bright- there is not\ra breath of air, not a peak nor glade nor jutting headland but it\rstands out in the ineffable radiance that breaks from the serene of\rheaven; the stars can all of them be told and the heart of the shepherd\ris glad- even thus shone the watchfires of the Trojans before Ilius\rmidway between the ships and the river Xanthus. A thousand camp-fires\rgleamed upon the plain, and in the glow of each there sat fifty men,\rwhile the horses, champing oats and corn beside their chariots, waited\rtill dawn should come. \r\r----------------------------------------------------------------------\r\rBOOK IX\r\rThus did the Trojans watch. But Panic, comrade of blood-stained Rout,\rhad taken fast hold of the Achaeans and their princes were all of\rthem in despair. As when the two winds that blow from Thrace- the\rnorth and the northwest- spring up of a sudden and rouse the fury\rof the main- in a moment the dark waves uprear their heads and scatter\rtheir sea-wrack in all directions- even thus troubled were the hearts\rof the Achaeans. \r\rThe son of Atreus in dismay bade the heralds call the people to a\rcouncil man by man, but not to cry the matter aloud; he made haste\ralso himself to call them, and they sat sorry at heart in their assembly.\rAgamemnon shed tears as it were a running stream or cataract on the\rside of some sheer cliff; and thus, with many a heavy sigh he spoke\rto the Achaeans. "My friends," said he, "princes and councillors of\rthe Argives, the hand of heaven has been laid heavily upon me. Cruel\rJove gave me his solemn promise that I should sack the city of Troy\rbefore returning, but he has played me false, and is now bidding me\rgo ingloriously back to Argos with the loss of much people. Such is\rthe will of Jove, who has laid many a proud city in the dust as he\rwill yet lay others, for his power is above all. Now, therefore, let\rus all do as I say and sail back to our own country, for we shall\rnot take Troy." \r\rThus he spoke, and the sons of the Achaeans for a long while sat sorrowful\rthere, but they all held their peace, till at last Diomed of the loud\rbattle-cry made answer saying, "Son of Atreus, I will chide your folly,\ras is my right in council. Be not then aggrieved that I should do\rso. In the first place you attacked me before all the Danaans and\rsaid that I was a coward and no soldier. The Argives young and old\rknow that you did so. But the son of scheming Saturn endowed you by\rhalves only. He gave you honour as the chief ruler over us, but valour,\rwhich is the highest both right and might he did not give you. Sir,\rthink you that the sons of the Achaeans are indeed as unwarlike and\rcowardly as you say they are? If your own mind is set upon going home-\rgo- the way is open to you; the many ships that followed you from\rMycene stand ranged upon the seashore; but the rest of us stay here\rtill we have sacked Troy. Nay though these too should turn homeward\rwith their ships, Sthenelus and myself will still fight on till we\rreach the goal of Ilius, for for heaven was with us when we came."\r\rThe sons of the Achaeans shouted applause at the words of Diomed,\rand presently Nestor rose to speak. "Son of Tydeus," said he, "in\rwar your prowess is beyond question, and in council you excel all\rwho are of your own years; no one of the Achaeans can make light of\rwhat you say nor gainsay it, but you have not yet come to the end\rof the whole matter. You are still young- you might be the youngest\rof my own children- still you have spoken wisely and have counselled\rthe chief of the Achaeans not without discretion; nevertheless I am\rolder than you and I will tell you every" thing; therefore let no\rman, not even King Agamemnon, disregard my saying, for he that foments\rcivil discord is a clanless, hearthless outlaw. \r\r"Now, however, let us obey the behests of night and get our suppers,\rbut let the sentinels every man of them camp by the trench that is\rwithout the wall. I am giving these instructions to the young men;\rwhen they have been attended to, do you, son of Atreus, give your\rorders, for you are the most royal among us all. Prepare a feast for\ryour councillors; it is right and reasonable that you should do so;\rthere is abundance of wine in your tents, which the ships of the Achaeans\rbring from Thrace daily. You have everything at your disposal wherewith\rto entertain guests, and you have many subjects. When many are got\rtogether, you can be guided by him whose counsel is wisest- and sorely\rdo we need shrewd and prudent counsel, for the foe has lit his watchfires\rhard by our ships. Who can be other than dismayed? This night will\reither be the ruin of our host, or save it." \r\rThus did he speak, and they did even as he had said. The sentinels\rwent out in their armour under command of Nestor\'s son Thrasymedes,\ra captain of the host, and of the bold warriors Ascalaphus and Ialmenus:\rthere were also Meriones, Aphareus and Deipyrus, and the son of Creion,\rnoble Lycomedes. There were seven captains of the sentinels, and with\reach there went a hundred youths armed with long spears: they took\rtheir places midway between the trench and the wall, and when they\rhad done so they lit their fires and got every man his supper.\r\rThe son of Atreus then bade many councillors of the Achaeans to his\rquarters prepared a great feast in their honour. They laid their hands\ron the good things that were before them, and as soon as they had\renough to eat and drink, old Nestor, whose counsel was ever truest,\rwas the first to lay his mind before them. He, therefore, with all\rsincerity and goodwill addressed them thus. \r\r"With yourself, most noble son of Atreus, king of men, Agamemnon,\rwill I both begin my speech and end it, for you are king over much\rpeople. Jove, moreover, has vouchsafed you to wield the sceptre and\rto uphold righteousness, that you may take thought for your people\runder you; therefore it behooves you above all others both to speak\rand to give ear, and to out the counsel of another who shall have\rbeen minded to speak wisely. All turns on you and on your commands,\rtherefore I will say what I think will be best. No man will be of\ra truer mind than that which has been mine from the hour when you,\rsir, angered Achilles by taking the girl Briseis from his tent against\rmy judgment. I urged you not to do so, but you yielded to your own\rpride, and dishonoured a hero whom heaven itself had honoured- for\ryou still hold the prize that had been awarded to him. Now, however,\rlet us think how we may appease him, both with presents and fair speeches\rthat may conciliate him." \r\rAnd King Agamemnon answered, "Sir, you have reproved my folly justly.\rI was wrong. I own it. One whom heaven befriends is in himself a host,\rand Jove has shown that he befriends this man by destroying much people\rof the Achaeans. I was blinded with passion and yielded to my worser\rmind; therefore I will make amends, and will give him great gifts\rby way of atonement. I will tell them in the presence of you all.\rI will give him seven tripods that have never yet been on the fire,\rand ten talents of gold. I will give him twenty iron cauldrons and\rtwelve strong horses that have won races and carried off prizes. Rich,\rindeed, both in land and gold is he that has as many prizes as my\rhorses have won me. I will give him seven excellent workwomen, Lesbians,\rwhom I chose for myself when he took Lesbos- all of surpassing beauty.\rI will give him these, and with them her whom I erewhile took from\rhim, the daughter of Briseus; and I swear a great oath that I never\rwent up into her couch, nor have been with her after the manner of\rmen and women. \r\r"All these things will I give him now down, and if hereafter the gods\rvouchsafe me to sack the city of Priam, let him come when we Achaeans\rare dividing the spoil, and load his ship with gold and bronze to\rhis liking; furthermore let him take twenty Trojan women, the loveliest\rafter Helen herself. Then, when we reach Achaean Argos, wealthiest\rof all lands, he shall be my son-in-law and I will show him like honour\rwith my own dear son Orestes, who is being nurtured in all abundance.\rI have three daughters, Chrysothemis, Laodice, and lphianassa, let\rhim take the one of his choice, freely and without gifts of wooing,\rto the house of Peleus; I will add such dower to boot as no man ever\ryet gave his daughter, and will give him seven well established cities,\rCardamyle, Enope, and Hire, where there is grass; holy Pherae and\rthe rich meadows of Anthea; Aepea also, and the vine-clad slopes of\rPedasus, all near the sea, and on the borders of sandy Pylos. The\rmen that dwell there are rich in cattle and sheep; they will honour\rhim with gifts as though he were a god, and be obedient to his comfortable\rordinances. All this will I do if he will now forgo his anger. Let\rhim then yieldit is only Hades who is utterly ruthless and unyielding-\rand hence he is of all gods the one most hateful to mankind. Moreover\rI am older and more royal than himself. Therefore, let him now obey\rme." \r\rThen Nestor answered, "Most noble son of Atreus, king of men, Agamemnon.\rThe gifts you offer are no small ones, let us then send chosen messengers,\rwho may go to the tent of Achilles son of Peleus without delay. Let\rthose go whom I shall name. Let Phoenix, dear to Jove, lead the way;\rlet Ajax and Ulysses follow, and let the heralds Odius and Eurybates\rgo with them. Now bring water for our hands, and bid all keep silence\rwhile we pray to Jove the son of Saturn, if so be that he may have\rmercy upon us." \r\rThus did he speak, and his saying pleased them well. Men-servants\rpoured water over the hands of the guests, while pages filled the\rmixing-bowls with wine and water, and handed it round after giving\revery man his drink-offering; then, when they had made their offerings,\rand had drunk each as much as he was minded, the envoys set out from\rthe tent of Agamemnon son of Atreus; and Nestor, looking first to\rone and then to another, but most especially at Ulysses, was instant\rwith them that they should prevail with the noble son of Peleus.\r\rThey went their way by the shore of the sounding sea, and prayed earnestly\rto earth-encircling Neptune that the high spirit of the son of Aeacus\rmight incline favourably towards them. When they reached the ships\rand tents of the Myrmidons, they found Achilles playing on a lyre,\rfair, of cunning workmanship, and its cross-bar was of silver. It\rwas part of the spoils which he had taken when he sacked the city\rof Eetion, and he was now diverting himself with it and singing the\rfeats of heroes. He was alone with Patroclus, who sat opposite to\rhim and said nothing, waiting till he should cease singing. Ulysses\rand Ajax now came in- Ulysses leading the way -and stood before him.\rAchilles sprang from his seat with the lyre still in his hand, and\rPatroclus, when he saw the strangers, rose also. Achilles then greeted\rthem saying, "All hail and welcome- you must come upon some great\rmatter, you, who for all my anger are still dearest to me of the Achaeans."\r\rWith this he led them forward, and bade them sit on seats covered\rwith purple rugs; then he said to Patroclus who was close by him,\r"Son of Menoetius, set a larger bowl upon the table, mix less water\rwith the wine, and give every man his cup, for these are very dear\rfriends, who are now under my roof." \r\rPatroclus did as his comrade bade him; he set the chopping-block in\rfront of the fire, and on it he laid the loin of a sheep, the loin\ralso of a goat, and the chine of a fat hog. Automedon held the meat\rwhile Achilles chopped it; he then sliced the pieces and put them\ron spits while the son of Menoetius made the fire burn high. When\rthe flame had died down, he spread the embers, laid the spits on top\rof them, lifting them up and setting them upon the spit-racks; and\rhe sprinkled them with salt. When the meat was roasted, he set it\ron platters, and handed bread round the table in fair baskets, while\rAchilles dealt them their portions. Then Achilles took his seat facing\rUlysses against the opposite wall, and bade his comrade Patroclus\roffer sacrifice to the gods; so he cast the offerings into the fire,\rand they laid their hands upon the good things that were before them.\rAs soon as they had had enough to eat and drink, Ajax made a sign\rto Phoenix, and when he saw this, Ulysses filled his cup with wine\rand pledged Achilles. \r\r"Hail," said he, "Achilles, we have had no scant of good cheer, neither\rin the tent of Agamemnon, nor yet here; there has been plenty to eat\rand drink, but our thought turns upon no such matter. Sir, we are\rin the face of great disaster, and without your help know not whether\rwe shall save our fleet or lose it. The Trojans and their allies have\rcamped hard by our ships and by the wall; they have lit watchfires\rthroughout their host and deem that nothing can now prevent them from\rfalling on our fleet. Jove, moreover, has sent his lightnings on their\rright; Hector, in all his glory, rages like a maniac; confident that\rJove is with him he fears neither god nor man, but is gone raving\rmad, and prays for the approach of day. He vows that he will hew the\rhigh sterns of our ships in pieces, set fire to their hulls, and make\rhavoc of the Achaeans while they are dazed and smothered in smoke;\rI much fear that heaven will make good his boasting, and it will prove\rour lot to perish at Troy far from our home in Argos. Up, then, and\rlate though it be, save the sons of the Achaeans who faint before\rthe fury of the Trojans. You will repent bitterly hereafter if you\rdo not, for when the harm is done there will be no curing it; consider\rere it be too late, and save the Danaans from destruction.\r\r"My good friend, when your father Peleus sent you from Phthia to Agamemnon,\rdid he not charge you saying, \'Son, Minerva and Juno will make you\rstrong if they choose, but check your high temper, for the better\rpart is in goodwill. Eschew vain quarrelling, and the Achaeans old\rand young will respect you more for doing so.\' These were his words,\rbut you have forgotten them. Even now, however, be appeased, and put\raway your anger from you. Agamemnon will make you great amends if\ryou will forgive him; listen, and I will tell you what he has said\rin his tent that he will give you. He will give you seven tripods\rthat have never yet been on the fire, and ten talents of gold; twenty\riron cauldrons, and twelve strong horses that have won races and carried\roff prizes. Rich indeed both in land and gold is he who has as many\rprizes as these horses have won for Agamemnon. Moreover he will give\ryou seven excellent workwomen, Lesbians, whom he chose for himself,\rwhen you took Lesbos- all of surpassing beauty. He will give you these,\rand with them her whom he erewhile took from you, the daughter of\rBriseus, and he will swear a great oath, he has never gone up into\rher couch nor been with her after the manner of men and women. All\rthese things will he give you now down, and if hereafter the gods\rvouchsafe him to sack the city of Priam, you can come when we Achaeans\rare dividing the spoil, and load your ship with gold and bronze to\ryour liking. The men that\rdwell there are rich in cattle and sheep; they will honour you with\rgifts as though were a god, and be obedient to your comfortable ordinances.\rAll this will he do if you will now forgo your anger. Him do\rI hate even as the gates of hell who says one thing while he hides\ranother in his heart; therefore I will say what I mean. He that\rfights fares no better than he that does not; coward and hero are\rheld in equal honour, and death deals like measure to him who works\rand him who is idle. I have taken nothing by all my hardships- with\rmy life ever in my hand; as a bird when she has found a morsel takes\rit to her nestlings, and herself fares hardly, even so man a long\rnight have I been wakeful, and many a bloody battle have I waged by\rday against those who were fighting for their women. With my ships\rI have taken twelve cities, and eleven round about Troy have I stormed\rwith my men by land; I took great store of wealth from every one of\rthem, but I gave all up to Agamemnon son of Atreus. He stayed where\rhe was by his ships, yet of what came to him he gave little, and kept\rmuch himself. \r\r"Nevertheless he did distribute some meeds of honour among the chieftains\rand kings, and these have them still; from me alone of the Achaeans\rdid he take the woman in whom I delighted- let him keep her and sleep\rwith her. Why, pray, must the Argives needs fight the Trojans? What\rmade the son of Atreus gather the host and bring them? Was it not\rfor the sake of Helen? Are the sons of Atreus the only men in the\rworld who love their wives? Any man of common right feeling will love\rand cherish her who is his own, as I this woman, with my whole heart,\rthough she was but a fruitling of my spear. Agamemnon has taken her\rfrom me; he has played me false; I know him; let him tempt me no further,\rfor he shall not move me. He has built a wall; he has dug a trench deep and wide\rall round it, and he has planted it within with stakes; but even so\rhe stays not the murderous might of Hector. So long as I fought the\rAchaeans Hector suffered not the battle range far from the city walls;\rhe would come to the Scaean gates and to the oak tree, but no further.\rOnce he stayed to meet me and hardly did he escape my onset: now,\rhowever, since I am in no mood to fight him, I will to-morrow offer\rsacrifice to Jove and to all the gods; I will draw my ships into the\rwater and then victual them duly; to-morrow morning, if you care to\rlook, you will see my ships on the Hellespont, and my men rowing out\rto sea with might and main. If great Neptune vouchsafes me a fair\rpassage, in three days I shall be in Phthia. I have much there that\rI left behind me when I came here to my sorrow, and I shall bring\rback still further store of gold, of red copper, of fair women, and\rof iron, my share of the spoils that we have taken; but one prize,\rhe who gave has insolently taken away. Tell him all as I now bid you,\rand tell him in public that the Achaeans may hate him and beware of\rhim should he think that he can yet dupe others for his effrontery\rnever fails him. \r\r"As for me, hound that he is, he dares not look me in the face. I\rwill take no counsel with him, and will undertake nothing in common\rwith him. He has wronged me and deceived me enough, he shall not cozen\rme further; let him go his own way, for Jove has robbed him of his\rreason. I loathe his presents, and for himself care not one straw.\rHe may offer me ten or even twenty times what he has now done, nay-\rnot though it be all that he has in the world, both now or ever shall\rhave; he may promise me the wealth of Orchomenus or of Egyptian Thebes,\rwhich is the richest city in the whole world, for it has a hundred\rgates through each of which two hundred men may drive at once with\rtheir chariots and horses; he may offer me gifts as the sands of the\rsea or the dust of the plain in multitude, but even so he shall not\rmove me till I have been revenged in full for the bitter wrong he\rhas done me. I will not marry his daughter; she may be fair as Venus,\rand skilful as Minerva, but I will have none of her: let another take\rher, who may be a good match for her and who rules a larger kingdom.\rIf the gods spare me to return home, Peleus will find me a wife; there\rare Achaean women in Hellas and Phthia, daughters of kings that have\rcities under them; of these I can take whom I will and marry her.\rMany a time was I minded when at home in Phthia to woo and wed a woman\rwho would make me a suitable wife, and to enjoy the riches of my old\rfather Peleus. My life is more to me than all the wealth of Ilius\rwhile it was yet at peace before the Achaeans went there, or than\rall the treasure that lies on the stone floor of Apollo\'s temple beneath\rthe cliffs of Pytho. Cattle and sheep are to be had for harrying,\rand a man buy both tripods and horses if he wants them, but when his\rlife has once left him it can neither be bought nor harried back again.\r\r"My mother Thetis tells me that there are two ways in which I may\rmeet my end. If I stay here and fight, I shall not return alive but\rmy name will live for ever: whereas if I go home my name will die,\rbut it will be long ere death shall take me. To the rest of you, then,\rI say, \'Go home, for you will not take Ilius.\' Jove has held his hand\rover her to protect her, and her people have taken heart. As for Phoenix, let him\rsleep here that he may sail with me in the morning if he so will.\rBut I will not take him by force." \r\rThey all held their peace, dismayed at the sternness with which he\rhad denied them, till presently the old knight Phoenix in his great\rfear for the ships of the Achaeans, burst into tears and said, "Noble\rAchilles, if you are now minded to return, and in the fierceness of\ryour anger will do nothing to save the ships from burning, how, my\rson, can I remain here without you? Your father Peleus bade me go\rwith you when he sent you as a mere lad from Phthia to Agamemnon.\rYou knew nothing neither of war nor of the arts whereby men make their\rmark in council, and he sent me with you to train you in all excellence\rof speech and action. Therefore, my son, I will not stay here without\ryou- no, not though heaven itself vouchsafe to strip my years from\roff me, and make me young as I was when I first left Hellas the land\rof fair women. I was then flying the anger of father Amyntor, son\rof Ormenus, who was furious with me in the matter of his concubine,\rof whom he was enamoured to the wronging of his wife my mother. My\rmother, therefore, prayed me without ceasing to lie with the woman\rmyself, that so she hate my father, and in the course of time I yielded.\rBut my father soon came to know, and cursed me bitterly, calling the\rdread Erinyes to witness. He prayed that no son of mine might ever\rsit upon knees- and the gods, Jove of the world below and awful Proserpine,\rfulfilled his curse. I took counsel to kill him, but some god stayed\rmy rashness and bade me think on men\'s evil tongues and how I should\rbe branded as the murderer of my father: nevertheless I could not\rbear to stay in my father\'s house with him so bitter a against me.\rMy cousins and clansmen came about me, and pressed me sorely to remain;\rmany a sheep and many an ox did they slaughter, and many a fat hog\rdid they set down to roast before the fire; many a jar, too, did they\rbroach of my father\'s wine. Nine whole nights did they set a guard\rover me taking it in turns to watch, and they kept a fire always burning,\rboth in the cloister of the outer court and in the inner court at\rthe doors of the room wherein I lay; but when the darkness of the\rtenth night came, I broke through the closed doors of my room, and\rclimbed the wall of the outer court after passing quickly and unperceived\rthrough the men on guard and the women servants. I then fled through\rHellas till I came to fertile Phthia, mother of sheep, and to King\rPeleus, who made me welcome and treated me as a father treats an only\rson who will be heir to all his wealth. He made me rich and set me\rover much people, establishing me on the borders of Phthia where I\rwas chief ruler over the Dolopians. \r\r"It was I, Achilles, who had the making of you; I loved you with all\rmy heart: for you would eat neither at home nor when you had gone\rout elsewhere, till I had first set you upon my knees, cut up the\rdainty morsel that you were to eat, and held the wine-cup to your\rlips. Many a time have you slobbered your wine in baby helplessness\rover my shirt; I had infinite trouble with you, but I knew that heaven\rhad vouchsafed me no offspring of my own, and I made a son of you,\rAchilles, that in my hour of need you might protect me. Now, therefore,\rI say battle with your pride and beat it; cherish not your anger for\rever; the might and majesty of heaven are more than ours, but even\rheaven may be appeased; and if a man has sinned he prays the gods,\rand reconciles them to himself by his piteous cries and by frankincense,\rwith drink-offerings and the savour of burnt sacrifice. For prayers\rare as daughters to great Jove; halt, wrinkled, with eyes askance,\rthey follow in the footsteps of sin, who, being fierce and fleet of\rfoot, leaves them far behind him, and ever baneful to mankind outstrips\rthem even to the ends of the world; but nevertheless the prayers come\rhobbling and healing after. If a man has pity upon these daughters\rof Jove when they draw near him, they will bless him and hear him\rtoo when he is praying; but if he deny them and will not listen to\rthem, they go to Jove the son of Saturn and pray that he may presently\rfall into sin- to his ruing bitterly hereafter. Were not the son of Atreus offering you gifts\rand promising others later- if he were still furious and implacable-\rI am not he that would bid you throw off your anger and help the Achaeans,\rno matter how great their need; but he is giving much now, and more\rhereafter; he has sent his captains to urge his suit, and has chosen\rthose who of all the Argives are most acceptable to you; make not\rthen their words and their coming to be of none effect. Your anger\rhas been righteous so far. We have heard in song how heroes of old\rtime quarrelled when they were roused to fury, but still they could\rbe won by gifts, and fair words could soothe them. \r\r"I have an old story in my mind- a very old one- but you are all friends\rand I will tell it. The Curetes and the Aetolians were fighting and\rkilling one another round Calydon- the Aetolians defending the city\rand the Curetes trying to destroy it. For Diana of the golden throne\rwas angry and did them hurt because Oeneus had not offered her his\rharvest first-fruits. The other gods had all been feasted with hecatombs,\rbut to the daughter of great Jove alone he had made no sacrifice.\rHe had forgotten her, or somehow or other it had escaped him, and\rthis was a grievous sin. Thereon the archer goddess in her displeasure\rsent a prodigious creature against him- a savage wild boar with great\rwhite tusks that did much harm to his orchard lands, uprooting apple-trees\rin full bloom and throwing them to the ground. But Meleager son of\rOeneus got huntsmen and hounds from many cities and killed it- for\rit was so monstrous that not a few were needed, and many a man did\rit stretch upon his funeral pyre. On this the goddess set the Curetes\rand the Aetolians fighting furiously about the head and skin of the\rboar. \r\r"So long as Meleager was in the field things went badly with the Curetes,\rand for all their numbers they could not hold their ground under the\rcity walls; but in the course of time Meleager was angered as even\ra wise man will sometimes be. He was incensed with his mother Althaea,\rand therefore stayed at home with his wedded wife fair Cleopatra,\rwho was daughter of Marpessa daughter of Euenus, and of Ides the man\rthen living. He it was who took his bow and faced King Apollo himself\rfor fair Marpessa\'s sake; her father and mother then named her Alcyone,\rbecause her mother had mourned with the plaintive strains of the halcyon-bird\rwhen Phoebus Apollo had carried her off. Meleager, then, stayed at\rhome with Cleopatra, nursing the anger which he felt by reason of\rhis mother\'s curses. His mother, grieving for the death of her brother,\rprayed the gods, and beat the earth with her hands, calling upon Hades\rand on awful Proserpine; she went down upon her knees and her bosom\rwas wet with tears as she prayed that they would kill her son- and\rErinys that walks in darkness and knows no ruth heard her from Erebus.\r\r"Then was heard the din of battle about the gates of Calydon, and\rthe dull thump of the battering against their walls. Thereon the elders\rof the Aetolians besought Meleager; they sent the chiefest of their\rpriests, and begged him to come out and help them, promising him a\rgreat reward. They bade him choose fifty plough-gates, the most fertile\rin the plain of Calydon, the one-half vineyard and the other open\rplough-land. The old warrior Oeneus implored him, standing at the\rthreshold of his room and beating the doors in supplication. His sisters\rand his mother herself besought him sore, but he the more refused\rthem; those of his comrades who were nearest and dearest to him also\rprayed him, but they could not move him till the foe was battering\rat the very doors of his chamber, and the Curetes had scaled the walls\rand were setting fire to the city. Then at last his sorrowing wife\rdetailed the horrors that befall those whose city is taken; she reminded\rhim how the men are slain, and the city is given over to the flames,\rwhile the women and children are carried into captivity; when he heard\rall this, his heart was touched, and he donned his armour to go forth.\rThus of his own inward motion he saved the city of the Aetolians;\rbut they now gave him nothing of those rich rewards that they had\roffered earlier, and though he saved the city he took nothing by it.\rBe not then, my son, thus minded; let not heaven lure you into any\rsuch course. When the ships are burning it will be a harder matter\rto save them. Take the gifts, and go, for the Achaeans will then honour\ryou as a god; whereas if you fight without taking them, you may beat\rthe battle back, but you will not be held in like honour."\r\rAnd Achilles answered, "Phoenix, old friend and father, I have no\rneed of such honour. I have honour from Jove himself, which will abide\rwith me at my ships while I have breath in my body, and my limbs are\rstrong. I say further- and lay my saying to your heart- vex me no\rmore with this weeping and lamentation, all in the cause of the son\rof Atreus. Love him so well, and you may lose the love I bear you.\rYou ought to help me rather in troubling those that trouble me; be\rking as much as I am, and share like honour with myself; the others\rshall take my answer; stay here yourself and sleep comfortably in\ryour bed; at daybreak we will consider whether to remain or go."\r\rOn this she nodded quietly to Patroclus as a sign that he was to prepare\ra bed for Phoenix, and that the others should take their leave. Ajax\rson of Telamon then said, "Ulysses, noble son of Laertes, let us be\rgone, for I see that our journey is vain. We must now take our answer,\runwelcome though it be, to the Danaans who are waiting to receive\rit. Achilles is savage and remorseless; he is cruel, and cares nothing\rfor the love his comrades lavished upon him more than on all the others.\rHe is implacable- and yet if a man\'s brother or son has been slain\rhe will accept a fine by way of amends from him that killed him, and\rthe wrong-doer having paid in full remains in peace among his own\rpeople; but as for you, Achilles, the gods have put a wicked unforgiving\rspirit in your heart, and this, all about one single girl, whereas\rwe now offer you the seven best we have, and much else into the bargain.\rBe then of a more gracious mind, respect the hospitality of your own\rroof. We are with you as messengers from the host of the Danaans,\rand would fain he held nearest and dearest to yourself of all the\rAchaeans." \r\r"Ajax," replied Achilles, "noble son of Telamon, you have spoken much\rto my liking, but my blood boils when I think it all over, and remember\rhow the son of Atreus treated me with contumely as though I were some\rvile tramp, and that too in the presence of the Argives. Go, then,\rand deliver your message; say that I will have no concern with fighting\rtill Hector, son of noble Priam, reaches the tents of the Myrmidons\rin his murderous course, and flings fire upon their ships. For all\rhis lust of battle, I take it he will be held in check when he is\rat my own tent and ship." \r\rOn this they took every man his double cup, made their drink-offerings,\rand went back to the ships, Ulysses leading the way. But Patroclus\rtold his men and the maid-servants to make ready a comfortable bed\rfor Phoenix; they therefore did so with sheepskins, a rug, and a sheet\rof fine linen. The old man then laid himself down and waited till\rmorning came. But Achilles slept in an inner room, and beside him\rthe daughter of Phorbas lovely Diomede, whom he had carried off from\rLesbos. Patroclus lay on the other side of the room, and with him\rfair Iphis whom Achilles had given him when he took Scyros the city\rof Enyeus. \r\rWhen the envoys reached the tents of the son of Atreus, the Achaeans\rrose, pledged them in cups of gold, and began to question them. King\rAgamemnon was the first to do so. Tell me, Ulysses," said he, "will\rhe save the ships from burning, or did be refuse, and is he still\rfurious?" \r\rUlysses answered, "Most noble son of Atreus, king of men, Agamemnon,\rAchilles will not be calmed, but is more fiercely angry than ever,\rand spurns both you and your gifts. He bids you take counsel with\rthe Achaeans to save the ships and host as you best may; as for himself,\rhe said that at daybreak he should draw his ships into the water.\rHe said further that he should advise every one to sail home likewise,\rfor that you will not reach the goal of Ilius. \'Jove,\' he said, \'has\rlaid his hand over the city to protect it, and the people have taken\rheart.\' This is what he said, and the others who were with me can\rtell you the same story- Ajax and the two heralds, men, both of them,\rwho may be trusted. The old man Phoenix stayed where he was to sleep,\rfor so Achilles would have it, that he might go home with him in the\rmorning if he so would; but he will not take him by force."\r\rThey all held their peace, sitting for a long time silent and dejected,\rby reason of the sternness with which Achilles had refused them, till\rpresently Diomed said, "Most noble son of Atreus, king of men, Agamemnon,\ryou ought not to have sued the son of Peleus nor offered him gifts.\rHe is proud enough as it is, and you have encouraged him in his pride\ram further. Let him stay or go as he will. He will fight later when\rhe is in the humour, and heaven puts it in his mind to do so. Now,\rtherefore, let us all do as I say; we have eaten and drunk our fill,\rlet us then take our rest, for in rest there is both strength and\rstay. But when fair rosy-fingered morn appears, forthwith bring out\ryour host and your horsemen in front of the ships, urging them on,\rand yourself fighting among the foremost." \r\rThus he spoke, and the other chieftains approved his words. They then\rmade their drink-offerings and went every man to his own tent, where\rthey laid down to rest and enjoyed the boon of sleep. \r\r----------------------------------------------------------------------\r\rBOOK X\r\rNow the other princes of the Achaeans slept soundly the whole night\rthrough, but Agamemnon son of Atreus was troubled, so that he could\rget no rest. As when fair Juno\'s lord flashes his lightning in token\rof great rain or hail or snow when the snow-flakes whiten the ground,\ror again as a sign that he will open the wide jaws of hungry war,\reven so did Agamemnon heave many a heavy sigh, for his soul trembled\rwithin him. When he looked upon the plain of Troy he marvelled at\rthe many watchfires burning in front of Ilius, and at the sound of\rpipes and flutes and of the hum of men, but when presently he turned\rtowards the ships and hosts of the Achaeans, he tore his hair by handfuls\rbefore Jove on high, and groaned aloud for the very disquietness of\rhis soul. In the end he deemed it best to go at once to Nestor son\rof Neleus, and see if between them they could find any way of the\rAchaeans from destruction. He therefore rose, put on his shirt, bound\rhis sandals about his comely feet, flung the skin of a huge tawny\rlion over his shoulders- a skin that reached his feet- and took his\rspear in his hand. \r\rNeither could Menelaus sleep, for he, too, boded ill for the Argives\rwho for his sake had sailed from far over the seas to fight the Trojans.\rHe covered his broad back with the skin of a spotted panther, put\ra casque of bronze upon his head, and took his spear in his brawny\rhand. Then he went to rouse his brother, who was by far the most powerful\rof the Achaeans, and was honoured by the people as though he were\ra god. He found him by the stern of his ship already putting his goodly\rarray about his shoulders, and right glad was he that his brother\rhad come. \r\rMenelaus spoke first. "Why," said he, "my dear brother, are you thus\rarming? Are you going to send any of our comrades to exploit the Trojans?\rI greatly fear that no one will do you this service, and spy upon\rthe enemy alone in the dead of night. It will be a deed of great daring."\r\rAnd King Agamemnon answered, "Menelaus, we both of us need shrewd\rcounsel to save the Argives and our ships, for Jove has changed his\rmind, and inclines towards Hector\'s sacrifices rather than ours. I\rnever saw nor heard tell of any man as having wrought such ruin in\rone day as Hector has now wrought against the sons of the Achaeans-\rand that too of his own unaided self, for he is son neither to god\rnor goddess. The Argives will rue it long and deeply. Run, therefore,\rwith all speed by the line of the ships, and call Ajax and Idomeneus.\rMeanwhile I will go to Nestor, and bid him rise and go about among\rthe companies of our sentinels to give them their instructions; they\rwill listen to him sooner than to any man, for his own son, and Meriones\rbrother in arms to Idomeneus, are captains over them. It was to them\rmore particularly that we gave this charge." \r\rMenelaus replied, "How do I take your meaning? Am I to stay with them\rand wait your coming, or shall I return here as soon as I have given\ryour orders?" "Wait," answered King Agamemnon, "for there are so many\rpaths about the camp that we might miss one another. Call every man\ron your way, and bid him be stirring; name him by his lineage and\rby his father\'s name, give each all titular observance, and stand\rnot too much upon your own dignity; we must take our full share of\rtoil, for at our birth Jove laid this heavy burden upon us."\r\rWith these instructions he sent his brother on his way, and went on\rto Nestor shepherd of his people. He found him sleeping in his tent\rhard by his own ship; his goodly armour lay beside him- his shield,\rhis two spears and his helmet; beside him also lay the gleaming girdle\rwith which the old man girded himself when he armed to lead his people\rinto battle- for his age stayed him not. He raised himself on his\relbow and looked up at Agamemnon. "Who is it," said he, "that goes\rthus about the host and the ships alone and in the dead of night,\rwhen men are sleeping? Are you looking for one of your mules or for\rsome comrade? Do not stand there and say nothing, but speak. What\ris your business?" \r\rAnd Agamemnon answered, "Nestor, son of Neleus, honour to the Achaean\rname, it is I, Agamemnon son of Atreus, on whom Jove has laid labour\rand sorrow so long as there is breath in my body and my limbs carry\rme. I am thus abroad because sleep sits not upon my eyelids, but my\rheart is big with war and with the jeopardy of the Achaeans. I am\rin great fear for the Danaans. I am at sea, and without sure counsel;\rmy heart beats as though it would leap out of my body, and my limbs\rfail me. The enemy is encamped\rhard and we know not but he may attack us by night." \r\rNestor replied, "Most noble son of Atreus, king of men, Agamemnon,\rJove will not do all for Hector that Hector thinks he will; he will\rhave troubles yet in plenty if Achilles will lay aside his anger.\rI will go with you, and we will rouse others, either the son of Tydeus,\ror Ulysses, or fleet Ajax and the valiant son of Phyleus. He ought to be going about imploring\raid from all the princes of the Achaeans, for we are in extreme danger."\r\rAnd Agamemnon answered, "Sir, you may sometimes blame him justly,\rfor he is often remiss and unwilling to exert himself- not indeed\rfrom sloth, nor yet heedlessness, but because he looks to me and expects\rme to take the lead. On this occasion, however, he was awake before\rI was, and came to me of his own accord. I have already sent him to\rcall the very men whom you have named. And now let us be going. We\rshall find them with the watch outside the gates, for it was there\rI said that we would meet them." \r\r"In that case," answered Nestor, "the Argives will not blame him nor\rdisobey his orders when he urges them to fight or gives them instructions."\r\rWith this he put on his shirt, and bound his sandals about his comely\rfeet. He buckled on his purple coat, of two thicknesses, large, and\rof a rough shaggy texture, grasped his redoubtable bronze-shod spear,\rand wended his way along the line of the Achaean ships. First he called\rloudly to Ulysses peer of gods in counsel and woke him, for he was\rsoon roused by the sound of the battle-cry. He came outside his tent\rand said, "Why do you go thus alone about the host, and along the\rline of the ships in the stillness of the night? What is it that you\rfind so urgent?" And Nestor knight of Gerene answered, "Ulysses, noble\rson of Laertes, take it not amiss, for the Achaeans are in great straits.\rCome with me and let us wake some other, who may advise well with\rus whether we shall fight or fly." \r\rOn this Ulysses went at once into his tent, put his shield about his\rshoulders and came out with them. First they went to Diomed son of\rTydeus, and found him outside his tent clad in his armour with his\rcomrades sleeping round him and using their shields as pillows; as\rfor their spears, they stood upright on the spikes of their butts\rthat were driven into the ground, and the burnished bronze flashed\rafar like the lightning of father Jove. Can you not see that\rthe Trojans are encamped on the brow of the plain hard by our ships,\rwith but a little space between us and them?" \r\rOn these words Diomed leaped up instantly and said, "Old man, your\rheart is of iron; you rest not one moment from your labours. Are there\rno younger men among the Achaeans who could go about to rouse the\rprinces? There is no tiring you." \r\rAnd Nestor knight of Gerene made answer, "My son, all that you have\rsaid is true. Go then, for\ryou are younger than I, and of your courtesy rouse Ajax and the fleet\rson of Phyleus." \r\rDiomed threw the skin of a great tawny lion about his shoulders- a\rskin that reached his feet- and grasped his spear. As\rsheep dogs that watch their flocks when they are yarded, and hear\ra wild beast coming through the mountain forest towards them- forthwith\rthere is a hue and cry of dogs and men, and slumber is broken- even\rso was sleep chased from the eyes of the Achaeans as they kept the\rwatches of the wicked night, for they turned constantly towards the\rplain whenever they heard any stir among the Trojans. The old man\rwas glad bade them be of good cheer. "Watch on, my children," said\rhe, "and let not sleep get hold upon you, lest our enemies triumph\rover us." \r\rWith this he passed the trench, and with him the other chiefs of the\rAchaeans who had been called to the council. They sat down, therefore, and held debate with one\ranother. \r\rNestor spoke first. If he could learn all this and\rcome back safely here, his fame would be high as heaven in the mouths\rof all men, and he would be rewarded richly; for the chiefs from all\rour ships would each of them give him a black ewe with her lamb- which\ris a present of surpassing value- and he would be asked as a guest\rto all feasts and clan-gatherings." \r\rThey all held their peace, but Diomed of the loud war-cry spoke saying,\r"Nestor, gladly will I visit the host of the Trojans over against\rus, but if another will go with me I shall do so in greater confidence\rand comfort. When two men are together, one of them may see some opportunity\rwhich the other has not caught sight of; if a man is alone he is less\rfull of resource, and his wit is weaker." \r\rOn this several offered to go with Diomed. The two Ajaxes, servants\rof Mars, Meriones, and the son of Nestor all wanted to go, so did\rMenelaus son of Atreus; Ulysses also wished to go among the host of\rthe Trojans, for he was ever full of daring, and thereon Agamemnon\rking of men spoke thus: "Diomed," said he, "son of Tydeus, man after\rmy own heart, choose your comrade for yourself- take the best man\rof those that have offered, for many would now go with you. Do not\rthrough delicacy reject the better man, and take the worst out of\rrespect for his lineage, because he is of more royal blood."\r\rHe said this because he feared for Menelaus. The stars have gone forward,\rtwo-thirds of the night are already spent, and the third is alone\rleft us." \r\rThey then put on their armour. Brave Thrasymedes provided the son\rof Tydeus with a sword and a shield (for he had left his own at his\rship) and on his head he set a helmet of bull\'s hide without either\rpeak or crest; it is called a skull-cap and is a common headgear.\rMeriones found a bow and quiver for Ulysses, and on his head he set\ra leathern helmet that was lined with a strong plaiting of leathern\rthongs, while on the outside it was thickly studded with boar\'s teeth,\rwell and skilfully set into it; next the head there was an inner lining\rof felt. Pallas Minerva sent them a heron by the wayside upon\rtheir right hands; they could not see it for the darkness, but they\rheard its cry. Ulysses was glad when he heard it and prayed to Minerva:\r"Hear me," he cried, "daughter of aegis-bearing Jove, you who spy\rout all my ways and who are with me in all my hardships; befriend\rme in this mine hour, and grant that we may return to the ships covered\rwith glory after having achieved some mighty exploit that shall bring\rsorrow to the Trojans." \r\rThen Diomed of the loud war-cry also prayed: "Hear me too," said he,\r"daughter of Jove, unweariable; be with me even as you were with my\rnoble father Tydeus when he went to Thebes as envoy sent by the Achaeans.\rHe left the Achaeans by the banks of the river Aesopus, and went to\rthe city bearing a message of peace to the Cadmeians; on his return\rthence, with your help, goddess, he did great deeds of daring, for\ryou were his ready helper. I\rwill gild her horns and will offer her up to you in sacrifice."\r\rThus they prayed, and Pallas Minerva heard their prayer. When they\rhad done praying to the daughter of great Jove, they went their way\rlike two lions prowling by night amid the armour and blood-stained\rbodies of them that had fallen. \r\rNeither again did Hector let the Trojans sleep; for he too called\rthe princes and councillors of the Trojans that he might set his counsel\rbefore them. "Is there one," said he, "who for a great reward will\rdo me the service of which I will tell you? He shall be well paid\rif he will. I will give him a chariot and a couple of horses, the\rfleetest that can be found at the ships of the Achaeans, if he will\rdare this thing; and he will win infinite honour to boot; he must\rgo to the ships and find out whether they are still guarded as heretofore,\ror whether now that we have beaten them the Achaeans design to fly,\rand through sheer exhaustion are neglecting to keep their watches."\r\rThey all held their peace; but there was among the Trojans a certain\rman named Dolon, son of Eumedes, the famous herald- a man rich in\rgold and bronze. He was ill-favoured, but a good runner, and was an\ronly son among five sisters. He it was that now addressed the Trojans.\r"I, Hector," said he, "Will to the ships and will exploit them. But\rfirst hold up your sceptre and swear that you will give me the chariot,\rbedight with bronze, and the horses that now carry the noble son of\rPeleus. When he had left the horses\rand the troops behind him, he made all speed on his way, but Ulysses\rperceived his coming and said to Diomed, "Diomed, here is some one\rfrom the camp; I am not sure whether he is a spy, or whether it is\rsome thief who would plunder the bodies of the dead; let him get a\rlittle past us, we can then spring upon him and take him. The others gave chase\rat once, and as a couple of well-trained hounds press forward after\ra doe or hare that runs screaming in front of them, even so did the\rson of Tydeus and Ulysses pursue Dolon and cut him off from his own\rpeople. But when he had fled so far towards the ships that he would\rsoon have fallen in with the outposts, Minerva infused fresh strength\rinto the son of Tydeus for fear some other of the Achaeans might have\rthe glory of being first to hit him, and he might himself be only\rsecond; he therefore sprang forward with his spear and said, "Stand,\ror I shall throw my spear, and in that case I shall soon make an end\rof you." \r\rHe threw as he spoke, but missed his aim on purpose. The dart flew\rover the man\'s right shoulder, and then stuck in the ground. He stood\rstock still, trembling and in great fear; his teeth chattered, and\rhe turned pale with fear. The two came breathless up to him and seized\rhis hands, whereon he began to weep and said, "Take me alive; I will\rransom myself; we have great store of gold, bronze, and wrought iron,\rand from this my father will satisfy you with a very large ransom,\rshould he hear of my being alive at the ships of the Achaeans."\r\r"Fear not," replied Ulysses, "let no thought of death be in your mind;\rbut tell me, and tell me true, why are you thus going about alone\rin the dead of night away from your camp and towards the ships, while\rother men are sleeping? The two then went onwards amid the\rfallen armour and the blood, and came presently to the company of\rThracian soldiers, who were sleeping, tired out with their day\'s toil;\rtheir goodly armour was lying on the ground beside them all orderly\rin three rows, and each man had his yoke of horses beside him. He was doubting whether to take the chariot in which\rthe king\'s armour was lying, and draw it out by the pole, or to lift\rthe armour out and carry it off; or whether again, he should not kill\rsome more Thracians. He started up out of his sleep and saw that the horses\rwere no longer in their place, and that the men were gasping in their\rdeath-agony; on this he groaned aloud, and called upon his friend\rby name. Then the whole Trojan camp was in an uproar as the people\rkept hurrying together, and they marvelled at the deeds of the heroes\rwho had now got away towards the ships. \r\rWhen they reached the place where they had killed Hector\'s scout,\rUlysses stayed his horses, and the son of Tydeus, leaping to the ground,\rplaced the blood-stained spoils in the hands of Ulysses and remounted:\rthen he lashed the horses onwards, and they flew forward nothing loth\rtowards the ships as though of their own free will. Nestor was first\rto hear the tramp of their feet. "My friends," said he, "princes and\rcounsellors of the Argives, shall I guess right or wrong?- but I must\rsay what I think: there is a sound in my ears as of the tramp of horses.\rI hope it may Diomed and Ulysses driving in horses from the Trojans,\rbut I much fear that the bravest of the Argives may have come to some\rharm at their hands." \r\rHe had hardly done speaking when the two men came in and dismounted,\rwhereon the others shook hands right gladly with them and congratulated\rthem. Nestor knight of Gerene was first to question them. "Tell me,"\rsaid he, "renowned Ulysses, how did you two come by these horses?\rDid you steal in among the Trojan forces, or did some god meet you\rand give them to you? They are like sunbeams. I am well conversant\rwith the Trojans, for old warrior though I am I never hold back by\rthe ships, but I never yet saw or heard of such horses as these are.\rSurely some god must have met you and given them to you, for you are\rboth of dear to Jove, and to Jove\'s daughter Minerva." \r\rAnd Ulysses answered, "Nestor son of Neleus, honour to the Achaean\rname, heaven, if it so will, can give us even better horses than these,\rfor the gods are far mightier than we are. These horses, however,\rabout which you ask me, are freshly come from Thrace. Diomed killed\rtheir king with the twelve bravest of his companions. Hard by the\rships we took a thirteenth man- a scout whom Hector and the other\rTrojans had sent as a spy upon our ships." \r\rHe laughed as he spoke and drove the horses over the ditch, while\rthe other Achaeans followed him gladly. When they reached the strongly\rbuilt quarters of the son of Tydeus, they tied the horses with thongs\rof leather to the manger, where the steeds of Diomed stood eating\rtheir sweet corn, but Ulysses hung the blood-stained spoils of Dolon\rat the stern of his ship, that they might prepare a sacred offering\rto Minerva. As for themselves, they went into the sea and washed the\rsweat from their bodies, and from their necks and thighs. When the\rsea-water had taken all the sweat from off them, and had refreshed\rthem, they went into the baths and washed themselves. After they had\rso done and had anointed themselves with oil, they sat down to table,\rand drawing from a full mixing-bowl, made a drink-offering of wine\rto Minerva. \r\r----------------------------------------------------------------------\r\rBOOK XI\r\rAnd now as Dawn rose from her couch beside Tithonus, harbinger of\rlight alike to mortals and immortals, Jove sent fierce Discord with\rthe ensign of war in her hands to the ships of the Achaeans. She took\rher stand by the huge black hull of Ulysses\' ship which was middlemost\rof all, so that her voice might carry farthest on either side, on\rthe one hand towards the tents of Ajax son of Telamon, and on the\rother towards those of Achilles- for these two heroes, well-assured\rof their own strength, had valorously drawn up their ships at the\rtwo ends of the line. There she took her stand, and raised a cry both\rloud and shrill that filled the Achaeans with courage, giving them\rheart to fight resolutely and with all their might, so that they had\rrather stay there and do battle than go home in their ships.\r\rThe son of Atreus shouted aloud and bade the Argives gird themselves\rfor battle while he put on his armour. First he girded his goodly\rgreaves about his legs, making them fast with ankle clasps of silver;\rand about his chest he set the breastplate which Cinyras had once\rgiven him as a guest-gift. It had been noised abroad as far as Cyprus\rthat the Achaeans were about to sail for Troy, and therefore he gave\rit to the king. It had ten courses of dark cyanus, twelve of gold,\rand ten of tin. There were serpents of cyanus that reared themselves\rup towards the neck, three upon either side, like the rainbows which\rthe son of Saturn has set in heaven as a sign to mortal men. About\rhis shoulders he threw his sword, studded with bosses of gold; and\rthe scabbard was of silver with a chain of gold wherewith to hang\rit. He took moreover the richly-dight shield that covered his body\rwhen he was in battle- fair to see, with ten circles of bronze running\rall round see, wit it. On the body of the shield there were twenty\rbosses of white tin, with another of dark cyanus in the middle: this\rlast was made to show a Gorgon\'s head, fierce and grim, with Rout\rand Panic on either side. The band for the arm to go through was of\rsilver, on which there was a writhing snake of cyanus with three heads\rthat sprang from a single neck, and went in and out among one another.\rOn his head Agamemnon set a helmet, with a peak before and behind,\rand four plumes of horse-hair that nodded menacingly above it; then\rhe grasped two redoubtable bronze-shod spears, and the gleam of his\rarmour shot from him as a flame into the firmament, while Juno and\rMinerva thundered in honour of the king of rich Mycene. \r\rEvery man now left his horses in charge of his charioteer to hold\rthem in readiness by the trench, while he went into battle on foot\rclad in full armour, and a mighty uproar rose on high into the dawning.\rThe chiefs were armed and at the trench before the horses got there,\rbut these came up presently. He then went on to\rkill Isus and Antiphus two sons of Priam, the one a bastard, the other\rborn in wedlock; they were in the same chariot- the bastard driving,\rwhile noble Antiphus fought beside him. The battle was now in array and they stood face to face\rwith one another, Agamemnon ever pressing forward in his eagerness\rto be ahead of all others. \r\rTell me now ye Muses that dwell in the mansions of Olympus, who, whether\rof the Trojans or of their allies, was first to face Agamemnon? It\rwas Iphidamas son of Antenor, a man both brave and of great stature,\rwho was brought up in fertile Thrace the mother of sheep. His wife will tear her cheeks for grief\rand his children will be fatherless: there will he rot, reddening\rthe earth with his blood, and vultures, not women, will gather round\rhim." \r\rThus he spoke, but Ulysses came up and stood over him. The Trojans had gathered\rround Ulysses like ravenous mountain jackals round the carcase of\rsome homed stag that has been hit with an arrow- the stag has fled\rat full speed so long as his blood was warm and his strength has lasted,\rbut when the arrow has overcome him, the savage jackals devour him\rin the shady glades of the forest. Then heaven sends a fierce lion\rthither, whereon the jackals fly in terror and the lion robs them\rof their prey- even so did Trojans many and brave gather round crafty\rUlysses, but the hero stood at bay and kept them off with his spear.\rAjax then came up with his shield before him like a wall, and stood\rhard by, whereon the Trojans fled in all directions. Menelaus took\rUlysses by the hand, and led him out of the press while his squire\rbrought up his chariot, but Ajax rushed furiously on the Trojans and\rkilled Doryclus, a bastard son of Priam; then he wounded Pandocus,\rLysandrus, Pyrasus, and Pylartes; as some swollen torrent comes rushing\rin full flood from the mountains on to the plain, big with the rain\rof heaven- many a dry oak and many a pine does it engulf, and much\rmud does it bring down and cast into the sea- even so did brave Ajax\rchase the foe furiously over the plain, slaying both men and horses.\r\rHector did not yet know what Ajax was doing, for he was fighting on\rthe extreme left of the battle by the banks of the river Scamander,\rwhere the carnage was thickest and the war-cry loudest round Nestor\rand brave Idomeneus. Among these Hector was making great slaughter\rwith his spear and furious driving, and was destroying the ranks that\rwere opposed to him; still the Achaeans would have given no ground,\rhad not Alexandrus husband of lovely Helen stayed the prowess of Machaon\rshepherd of his people, by wounding him in the right shoulder with\ra triple-barbed arrow. The Achaeans were in great fear that as the\rfight had turned against them the Trojans might take him prisoner,\rand Idomeneus said to Nestor, "Nestor son of Neleus, honour to the\rAchaean name, mount your chariot at once; take Machaon with you and\rdrive your horses to the ships as fast as you can. A physician is\rworth more than several other men put together, for he can cut out\rarrows and spread healing herbs." \r\rNestor knight of Gerene did as Idomeneus had counselled; he at once\rmounted his chariot, and Machaon son of the famed physician Aesculapius\rwent with him. He lashed his horses and they flew onward nothing loth\rtowards the ships, as though of their own free will. \r\rThen Cebriones seeing the Trojans in confusion said to Hector from\rhis place beside him, "Hector, here are we two fighting on the extreme\rwing of the battle, while the other Trojans are in pell-mell rout,\rthey and their horses. Ajax son of Telamon is driving them before\rhim; I know him by the breadth of his shield: let us turn our chariot\rand horses thither, where horse and foot are fighting most desperately,\rand where the cry of battle is loudest." \r\rWith this he lashed his goodly steeds, and when they felt the whip\rthey drew the chariot full speed among the Achaeans and Trojans, over\rthe bodies and shields of those that had fallen: the axle was bespattered\rwith blood, and the rail round the car was covered with splashes both\rfrom the horses\' hoofs and from the tyres of the wheels.
