Hector tore\rhis way through and flung himself into the thick of the fight, and\rhis presence threw the Danaans into confusion, for his spear was not\rlong idle; nevertheless though he went among the ranks with sword\rand spear, and throwing great stones, he avoided Ajax son of Telamon,\rfor Jove would have been angry with him if he had fought a better\rman than himself. \r\rThen father Jove from his high throne struck fear into the heart of\rAjax, so that he stood there dazed and threw his shield behind him-\rlooking fearfully at the throng of his foes as though he were some\rwild beast, and turning hither and thither but crouching slowly backwards.\rAs peasants with their hounds chase a lion from their stockyard, and\rwatch by night to prevent his carrying off the pick of their herd-\rhe makes his greedy spring, but in vain, for the darts from many a\rstrong hand fall thick around him, with burning brands that scare\rhim for all his fury, and when morning comes he slinks foiled and\rangry away- even so did Ajax, sorely against his will, retreat angrily\rbefore the Trojans, fearing for the ships of the Achaeans. Or as some\rlazy ass that has had many a cudgel broken about his back, when he\rinto a field begins eating the corn- boys beat him but he is too many\rfor them, and though they lay about with their sticks they cannot\rhurt him; still when he has had his fill they at last drive him from\rthe field- even so did the Trojans and their allies pursue great Ajax,\rever smiting the middle of his shield with their darts. Now and again\rhe would turn and show fight, keeping back the battalions of the Trojans,\rand then he would again retreat; but he prevented any of them from\rmaking his way to the ships. Single-handed he stood midway between\rthe Trojans and Achaeans: the spears that sped from their hands stuck\rsome of them in his mighty shield, while many, though thirsting for\rhis blood, fell to the ground ere they could reach him to the wounding\rof his fair flesh. \r\rNow when Eurypylus the brave son of Euaemon saw that Ajax was being\roverpowered by the rain of arrows, he went up to him and hurled his\rspear. He struck Apisaon son of Phausius in the liver below the midriff,\rand laid him low. Eurypylus sprang upon him, and stripped the armour\rfrom his shoulders; but when Alexandrus saw him, he aimed an arrow\rat him which struck him in the right thigh; the arrow broke, but the\rpoint that was left in the wound dragged on the thigh; he drew back,\rtherefore, under cover of his comrades to save his life, shouting\ras he did so to the Danaans, "My friends, princes and counsellors\rof the Argives, rally to the defence of Ajax who is being overpowered,\rand I doubt whether he will come out of the fight alive. Hither, then,\rto the rescue of great Ajax son of Telamon." \r\rEven so did he cry when he was wounded; thereon the others came near,\rand gathered round him, holding their shields upwards from their shoulders\rso as to give him cover. Ajax then made towards them, and turned round\rto stand at bay as soon as he had reached his men. \r\rThus then did they fight as it were a flaming fire. Meanwhile the\rmares of Neleus, all in a lather with sweat, were bearing Nestor out\rof the fight, and with him Machaon shepherd of his people. Achilles\rsaw and took note, for he was standing on the stern of his ship watching\rthe hard stress and struggle of the fight. He called from the ship\rto his comrade Patroclus, who heard him in the tent and came out looking\rlike Mars himself- here indeed was the beginning of the ill that presently\rbefell him. "Why," said he, "Achilles do you call me? what do you\rwhat do you want with me?" And Achilles answered, "Noble son of Menoetius,\rman after my own heart, I take it that I shall now have the Achaeans\rpraying at my knees, for they are in great straits; go, Patroclus,\rand ask Nestor who is that he is bearing away wounded from the field;\rfrom his back I should say it was Machaon son of Aesculapius, but\rI could not see his face for the horses went by me at full speed."\r\rPatroclus did as his dear comrade had bidden him, and set off running\rby the ships and tents of the Achaeans. \r\rWhen Nestor and Machaon had reached the tents of the son of Neleus,\rthey dismounted, and an esquire, Eurymedon, took the horses from the\rchariot. The pair then stood in the breeze by the seaside to dry the\rsweat from their shirts, and when they had so done they came inside\rand took their seats. Fair Hecamede, whom Nestor had had awarded to\rhim from Tenedos when Achilles took it, mixed them a mess; she was\rdaughter of wise Arsinous, and the Achaeans had given her to Nestor\rbecause he excelled all of them in counsel. First she set for them\ra fair and well-made table that had feet of cyanus; on it there was\ra vessel of bronze and an onion to give relish to the drink, with\rhoney and cakes of barley-meal. There was also a cup of rare workmanship\rwhich the old man had brought with him from home, studded with bosses\rof gold; it had four handles, on each of which there were two golden\rdoves feeding, and it had two feet to stand on. Any one else would\rhardly have been able to lift it from the table when it was full,\rbut Nestor could do so quite easily. In this the woman, as fair as\ra goddess, mixed them a mess with Pramnian wine; she grated goat\'s\rmilk cheese into it with a bronze grater, threw in a handful of white\rbarley-meal, and having thus prepared the mess she bade them drink\rit. When they had done so and had thus quenched their thirst, they\rfell talking with one another, and at this moment Patroclus appeared\rat the door. \r\rWhen the old man saw him he sprang from his seat, seized his hand,\rled him into the tent, and bade him take his place among them; but\rPatroclus stood where he was and said, "Noble sir, I may not stay,\ryou cannot persuade me to come in; he that sent me is not one to be\rtrifled with, and he bade me ask who the wounded man was whom you\rwere bearing away from the field. I can now see for myself that he\ris Machaon shepherd of his people. I must go back and tell Achilles.\rYou, sir, know what a terrible man he is, and how ready to blame even\rwhere no blame should lie." \r\rAnd Nestor answered, "Why should Achilles care to know how many of\rthe Achaeans may be wounded? He recks not of the dismay that reigns\rin our host; our most valiant chieftains lie disabled, brave Diomed\rson of Tydeus is wounded; so are Ulysses and Agamemnon; Eurypylus\rhas been hit with an arrow in the thigh, and I have just been bringing\rthis man from the field- he too wounded- with an arrow; nevertheless\rAchilles, so valiant though he be, cares not and knows no ruth. Will\rhe wait till the ships, do what we may, are in a blaze, and we perish\rone upon the other? As for me, I have no strength nor stay in me any\rlonger; would that I Were still young and strong as in the days when\rthere was a fight between us and the men of Elis about some cattle-raiding.\rI then killed Itymoneus the valiant son of Hypeirochus a dweller in\rElis, as I was driving in the spoil; he was hit by a dart thrown my\rhand while fighting in the front rank in defence of his cows, so he\rfell and the country people around him were in great fear. We drove\roff a vast quantity of booty from the plain, fifty herds of cattle\rand as many flocks of sheep; fifty droves also of pigs, and as many\rwide-spreading flocks of goats. Of horses moreover we seized a hundred\rand fifty, all of them mares, and many had foals running with them.\rAll these did we drive by night to Pylus the city of Neleus, taking\rthem within the city; and the heart of Neleus was glad in that I had\rtaken so much, though it was the first time I had ever been in the\rfield. At daybreak the heralds went round crying that all in Elis\rto whom there was a debt owing should come; and the leading Pylians\rassembled to divide the spoils. There were many to whom the Epeans\rowed chattels, for we men of Pylus were few and had been oppressed\rwith wrong; in former years Hercules had come, and had laid his hand\rheavy upon us, so that all our best men had perished. Neleus had had\rtwelve sons, but I alone was left; the others had all been killed.\rThe Epeans presuming upon all this had looked down upon us and had\rdone us much evil. My father chose a herd of cattle and a great flock\rof sheep- three hundred in all- and he took their shepherds with him,\rfor there was a great debt due to him in Elis, to wit four horses,\rwinners of prizes. They and their chariots with them had gone to the\rgames and were to run for a tripod, but King Augeas took them, and\rsent back their driver grieving for the loss of his horses. Neleus\rwas angered by what he had both said and done, and took great value\rin return, but he divided the rest, that no man might have less than\rhis full share. \r\r"Thus did we order all things, and offer sacrifices to the gods throughout\rthe city; but three days afterwards the Epeans came in a body, many\rin number, they and their chariots, in full array, and with them the\rtwo Moliones in their armour, though they were still lads and unused\rto fighting. Now there is a certain town, Thryoessa, perched upon\ra rock on the river Alpheus, the border city Pylus; this they would\rdestroy, and pitched their camp about it, but when they had crossed\rtheir whole plain, Minerva darted down by night from Olympus and bade\rus set ourselves in array; and she found willing soldiers in Pylos,\rfor the men meant fighting. Neleus would not let me arm, and hid my\rhorses, for he said that as yet I could know nothing about war; nevertheless\rMinerva so ordered the fight that, all on foot as I was, I fought\ramong our mounted forces and vied with the foremost of them. There\ris a river Minyeius that falls into the sea near Arene, and there\rthey that were mounted (and I with them) waited till morning, when\rthe companies of foot soldiers came up with us in force. Thence in\rfull panoply and equipment we came towards noon to the sacred waters\rof the Alpheus, and there we offered victims to almighty Jove, with\ra bull to Alpheus, another to Neptune, and a herd-heifer to Minerva.\rAfter this we took supper in our companies, and laid us down to rest\reach in his armour by the river. \r\r"The Epeans were beleaguering the city and were determined to take\rit, but ere this might be there was a desperate fight in store for\rthem. When the sun\'s rays began to fall upon the earth we joined battle,\rpraying to Jove and to Minerva, and when the fight had begun, I was\rthe first to kill my man and take his horses- to wit the warrior Mulius.\rHe was son-in-law to Augeas, having married his eldest daughter, golden-haired\rAgamede, who knew the virtues of every herb which grows upon the face\rof the earth. I speared him as he was coming towards me, and when\rhe fell headlong in the dust, I sprang upon his chariot and took my\rplace in the front ranks. The Epeans fled in all directions when they\rsaw the captain of their horsemen (the best man they had) laid low,\rand I swept down on them like a whirlwind, taking fifty chariots-\rand in each of them two men bit the dust, slain by my spear. I should\rhave even killed the two Moliones sons of Actor, unless their real\rfather, Neptune lord of the earthquake, had hidden them in a thick\rmist and borne them out of the fight. Thereon Jove vouchsafed the\rPylians a great victory, for we chased them far over the plain, killing\rthe men and bringing in their armour, till we had brought our horses\rto Buprasium rich in wheat and to the Olenian rock, with the hill\rthat is called Alision, at which point Minerva turned the people back.\rThere I slew the last man and left him; then the Achaeans drove their\rhorses back from Buprasium to Pylos and gave thanks to Jove among\rthe gods, and among mortal men to Nestor. \r\r"Such was I among my peers, as surely as ever was, but Achilles is\rfor keeping all his valour for himself; bitterly will he rue it hereafter\rwhen the host is being cut to pieces. My good friend, did not Menoetius\rcharge you thus, on the day when he sent you from Phthia to Agamemnon?\rUlysses and I were in the house, inside, and heard all that he said\rto you; for we came to the fair house of Peleus while beating up recruits\rthroughout all Achaea, and when we got there we found Menoetius and\ryourself, and Achilles with you. The old knight Peleus was in the\router court, roasting the fat thigh-bones of a heifer to Jove the\rlord of thunder; and he held a gold chalice in his hand from which\rhe poured drink-offerings of wine over the burning sacrifice. You\rtwo were busy cutting up the heifer, and at that moment we stood at\rthe gates, whereon Achilles sprang to his feet, led us by the hand\rinto the house, placed us at table, and set before us such hospitable\rentertainment as guests expect. When we had satisfied ourselves with\rmeat and drink, I said my say and urged both of you to join us. You\rwere ready enough to do so, and the two old men charged you much and\rstraitly. Old Peleus bade his son Achilles fight ever among the foremost\rand outvie his peers, while Menoetius the son of Actor spoke thus\rto you: \'My son,\' said he, \'Achilles is of nobler birth than you are,\rbut you are older than he, though he is far the better man of the\rtwo. Counsel him wisely, guide him in the right way, and he will follow\ryou to his own profit.\' Thus did your father charge you, but you have\rforgotten; nevertheless, even now, say all this to Achilles if he\rwill listen to you. Who knows but with heaven\'s help you may talk\rhim over, for it is good to take a friend\'s advice. If, however, he\ris fearful about some oracle, or if his mother has told him something\rfrom Jove, then let him send you, and let the rest of the Myrmidons\rfollow with you, if perchance you may bring light and saving to the\rDanaans. And let him send you into battle clad in his own armour,\rthat the Trojans may mistake you for him and leave off fighting; the\rsons of the Achaeans may thus have time to get their breath, for they\rare hard pressed and there is little breathing time in battle. You,\rwho are fresh, might easily drive a tired enemy back to his walls\rand away from the tents and ships." \r\rWith these words he moved the heart of Patroclus, who set off running\rby the line of the ships to Achilles, descendant of Aeacus. When he\rhad got as far as the ships of Ulysses, where was their place of assembly\rand court of justice, with their altars dedicated to the gods, Eurypylus\rson of Euaemon met him, wounded in the thigh with an arrow, and limping\rout of the fight. Sweat rained from his head and shoulders, and black\rblood welled from his cruel wound, but his mind did not wander. The\rson of Menoetius when he saw him had compassion upon him and spoke\rpiteously saying, "O unhappy princes and counsellors of the Danaans,\rare you then doomed to feed the hounds of Troy with your fat, far\rfrom your friends and your native land? say, noble Eurypylus, will\rthe Achaeans be able to hold great Hector in check, or will they fall\rnow before his spear?" \r\rWounded Eurypylus made answer, "Noble Patroclus, there is no hope\rleft for the Achaeans but they will perish at their ships. All they\rthat were princes among us are lying struck down and wounded at the\rhands of the Trojans, who are waxing stronger and stronger. But save\rme and take me to your ship; cut out the arrow from my thigh; wash\rthe black blood from off it with warm water, and lay upon it those\rgracious herbs which, so they say, have been shown you by Achilles,\rwho was himself shown them by Chiron, most righteous of all the centaurs.\rFor of the physicians Podalirius and Machaon, I hear that the one\ris lying wounded in his tent and is himself in need of healing, while\rthe other is fighting the Trojans upon the plain." \r\r"Hero Eurypylus," replied the brave son of Menoetius, "how may these\rthings be? What can I do? I am on my way to bear a message to noble\rAchilles from Nestor of Gerene, bulwark of the Achaeans, but even\rso I will not be unmindful your distress." \r\rWith this he clasped him round the middle and led him into the tent,\rand a servant, when he saw him, spread bullock-skins on the ground\rfor him to lie on. He laid him at full length and cut out the sharp\rarrow from his thigh; he washed the black blood from the wound with\rwarm water; he then crushed a bitter herb, rubbing it between his\rhands, and spread it upon the wound; this was a virtuous herb which\rkilled all pain; so the wound presently dried and the blood left off\rflowing. \r\r----------------------------------------------------------------------\r\rBOOK XII\r\rSo the son of Menoetius was attending to the hurt of Eurypylus within\rthe tent, but the Argives and Trojans still fought desperately, nor\rwere the trench and the high wall above it, to keep the Trojans in\rcheck longer. They had built it to protect their ships, and had dug\rthe trench all round it that it might safeguard both the ships and\rthe rich spoils which they had taken, but they had not offered hecatombs\rto the gods. It had been built without the consent of the immortals,\rand therefore it did not last. So long as Hector lived and Achilles\rnursed his anger, and so long as the city of Priam remained untaken,\rthe great wall of the Achaeans stood firm; but when the bravest of\rthe Trojans were no more, and many also of the Argives, though some\rwere yet left alive when, moreover, the city was sacked in the tenth\ryear, and the Argives had gone back with their ships to their own\rcountry- then Neptune and Apollo took counsel to destroy the wall,\rand they turned on to it the streams of all the rivers from Mount\rIda into the sea, Rhesus, Heptaporus, Caresus, Rhodius, Grenicus,\rAesopus, and goodly Scamander, with Simois, where many a shield and\rhelm had fallen, and many a hero of the race of demigods had bitten\rthe dust. Phoebus Apollo turned the mouths of all these rivers together\rand made them flow for nine days against the wall, while Jove rained\rthe whole time that he might wash it sooner into the sea. Neptune\rhimself, trident in hand, surveyed the work and threw into the sea\rall the foundations of beams and stones which the Achaeans had laid\rwith so much toil; he made all level by the mighty stream of the Hellespont,\rand then when he had swept the wall away he spread a great beach of\rsand over the place where it had been. This done he turned the rivers\rback into their old courses. \r\rThis was what Neptune and Apollo were to do in after time; but as\ryet battle and turmoil were still raging round the wall till its timbers\rrang under the blows that rained upon them. The Argives, cowed by\rthe scourge of Jove, were hemmed in at their ships in fear of Hector\rthe mighty minister of Rout, who as heretofore fought with the force\rand fury of a whirlwind. As a lion or wild boar turns fiercely on\rthe dogs and men that attack him, while these form solid wall and\rshower their javelins as they face him- his courage is all undaunted,\rbut his high spirit will be the death of him; many a time does he\rcharge at his pursuers to scatter them, and they fall back as often\ras he does so- even so did Hector go about among the host exhorting\rhis men, and cheering them on to cross the trench. \r\rBut the horses dared not do so, and stood neighing upon its brink,\rfor the width frightened them. They could neither jump it nor cross\rit, for it had overhanging banks all round upon either side, above\rwhich there were the sharp stakes that the sons of the Achaeans had\rplanted so close and strong as a defence against all who would assail\rit; a horse, therefore, could not get into it and draw his chariot\rafter him, but those who were on foot kept trying their very utmost.\rThen Polydamas went up to Hector and said, "Hector, and you other\rcaptains of the Trojans and allies, it is madness for us to try and\rdrive our horses across the trench; it will be very hard to cross,\rfor it is full of sharp stakes, and beyond these there is the wall.\rOur horses therefore cannot get down into it, and would be of no use\rif they did; moreover it is a narrow place and we should come to harm.\rIf, indeed, great Jove is minded to help the Trojans, and in his anger\rwill utterly destroy the Achaeans, I would myself gladly see them\rperish now and here far from Argos; but if they should rally and we\rare driven back from the ships pell-mell into the trench there will\rbe not so much as a man get back to the city to tell the tale. Now,\rtherefore, let us all do as I say; let our squires hold our horses\rby the trench, but let us follow Hector in a body on foot, clad in\rfull armour, and if the day of their doom is at hand the Achaeans\rwill not be able to withstand us." \r\rThus spoke Polydamas and his saying pleased Hector, who sprang in\rfull armour to the ground, and all the other Trojans, when they saw\rhim do so, also left their chariots. Each man then gave his horses\rover to his charioteer in charge to hold them ready for him at the\rtrench. Then they formed themselves into companies, made themselves\rready, and in five bodies followed their leaders. Those that went\rwith Hector and Polydamas were the bravest and most in number, and\rthe most determined to break through the wall and fight at the ships.\rCebriones was also joined with them as third in command, for Hector\rhad left his chariot in charge of a less valiant soldier. The next\rcompany was led by Paris, Alcathous, and Agenor; the third by Helenus\rand Deiphobus, two sons of Priam, and with them was the hero Asius-\rAsius the son of Hyrtacus, whose great black horses of the breed that\rcomes from the river Selleis had brought him from Arisbe. Aeneas the\rvaliant son of Anchises led the fourth; he and the two sons of Antenor,\rArchelochus and Acamas, men well versed in all the arts of war. Sarpedon\rwas captain over the allies, and took with him Glaucus and Asteropaeus\rwhom he deemed most valiant after himself- for he was far the best\rman of them all. These helped to array one another in their ox-hide\rshields, and then charged straight at the Danaans, for they felt sure\rthat they would not hold out longer and that they should themselves\rnow fall upon the ships. \r\rThe rest of the Trojans and their allies now followed the counsel\rof Polydamas but Asius son of Hyrtacus would not leave his horses\rand his esquire behind him; in his foolhardiness he took them on with\rhim towards the ships, nor did he fail to come by his end in consequence.\rNevermore was he to return to wind-beaten Ilius, exulting in his chariot\rand his horses; ere he could do so, death of ill-omened name had overshadowed\rhim and he had fallen by the spear of Idomeneus the noble son of Deucalion.\rHe had driven towards the left wing of the ships, by which way the\rAchaeans used to return with their chariots and horses from the plain.\rHither he drove and found the gates with their doors opened wide,\rand the great bar down- for the gatemen kept them open so as to let\rthose of their comrades enter who might be flying towards the ships.\rHither of set purpose did he direct his horses, and his men followed\rhim with a loud cry, for they felt sure that the Achaeans would not\rhold out longer, and that they should now fall upon the ships. Little\rdid they know that at the gates they should find two of the bravest\rchieftains, proud sons of the fighting Lapithae- the one, Polypoetes,\rmighty son of Pirithous, and the other Leonteus, peer of murderous\rMars. These stood before the gates like two high oak trees upon the\rmountains, that tower from their wide-spreading roots, and year after\ryear battle with wind and rain- even so did these two men await the\ronset of great Asius confidently and without flinching. The Trojans\rled by him and by Iamenus, Orestes, Adamas the son of Asius, Thoon\rand Oenomaus, raised a loud cry of battle and made straight for the\rwall, holding their shields of dry ox-hide above their heads; for\ra while the two defenders remained inside and cheered the Achaeans\ron to stand firm in the defence of their ships; when, however, they\rsaw that the Trojans were attacking the wall, while the Danaans were\rcrying out for help and being routed, they rushed outside and fought\rin front of the gates like two wild boars upon the mountains that\rabide the attack of men and dogs, and charging on either side break\rdown the wood all round them tearing it up by the roots, and one can\rhear the clattering of their tusks, till some one hits them and makes\ran end of them- even so did the gleaming bronze rattle about their\rbreasts, as the weapons fell upon them; for they fought with great\rfury, trusting to their own prowess and to those who were on the wall\rabove them. These threw great stones at their assailants in defence\rof themselves their tents and their ships. The stones fell thick as\rthe flakes of snow which some fierce blast drives from the dark clouds\rand showers down in sheets upon the earth- even so fell the weapons\rfrom the hands alike of Trojans and Achaeans. Helmet and shield rang\rout as the great stones rained upon them, and Asius the son of Hyrtacus\rin his dismay cried aloud and smote his two thighs. "Father Jove,"\rhe cried, "of a truth you too are altogether given to lying. I made\rsure the Argive heroes could not withstand us, whereas like slim-waisted\rwasps, or bees that have their nests in the rocks by the wayside-\rthey leave not the holes wherein they have built undefended, but fight\rfor their little ones against all who would take them- even so these\rmen, though they be but two, will not be driven from the gates, but\rstand firm either to slay or be slain." \r\rHe spoke, but moved not the mind of Jove, whose counsel it then was\rto give glory to Hector. Meanwhile the rest of the Trojans were fighting\rabout the other gates; I, however, am no god to be able to tell about\rall these things, for the battle raged everywhere about the stone\rwall as it were a fiery furnace. The Argives, discomfited though they\rwere, were forced to defend their ships, and all the gods who were\rdefending the Achaeans were vexed in spirit; but the Lapithae kept\ron fighting with might and main. \r\rThereon Polypoetes, mighty son of Pirithous, hit Damasus with a spear\rupon his cheek-pierced helmet. The helmet did not protect him, for\rthe point of the spear went through it, and broke the bone, so that\rthe brain inside was scattered about, and he died fighting. He then\rslew Pylon and Ormenus. Leonteus, of the race of Mars, killed Hippomachus\rthe son of Antimachus by striking him with his spear upon the girdle.\rHe then drew his sword and sprang first upon Antiphates whom he killed\rin combat, and who fell face upwards on the earth. After him he killed\rMenon, Iamenus, and Orestes, and laid them low one after the other.\r\rWhile they were busy stripping the armour from these heroes, the youths\rwho were led on by Polydamas and Hector (and these were the greater\rpart and the most valiant of those that were trying to break through\rthe wall and fire the ships) were still standing by the trench, uncertain\rwhat they should do; for they had seen a sign from heaven when they\rhad essayed to cross it- a soaring eagle that flew skirting the left\rwing of their host, with a monstrous blood-red snake in its talons\rstill alive and struggling to escape. The snake was still bent on\rrevenge, wriggling and twisting itself backwards till it struck the\rbird that held it, on the neck and breast; whereon the bird being\rin pain, let it fall, dropping it into the middle of the host, and\rthen flew down the wind with a sharp cry. The Trojans were struck\rwith terror when they saw the snake, portent of aegis-bearing Jove,\rwrithing in the midst of them, and Polydamas went up to Hector and\rsaid, "Hector, at our councils of war you are ever given to rebuke\rme, even when I speak wisely, as though it were not well, forsooth,\rthat one of the people should cross your will either in the field\ror at the council board; you would have them support you always: nevertheless\rI will say what I think will be best; let us not now go on to fight\rthe Danaans at their ships, for I know what will happen if this soaring\reagle which skirted the left wing of our with a monstrous blood-red\rsnake in its talons (the snake being still alive) was really sent\ras an omen to the Trojans on their essaying to cross the trench. The\reagle let go her hold; she did not succeed in taking it home to her\rlittle ones, and so will it be- with ourselves; even though by a mighty\reffort we break through the gates and wall of the Achaeans, and they\rgive way before us, still we shall not return in good order by the\rway we came, but shall leave many a man behind us whom the Achaeans\rwill do to death in defence of their ships. Thus would any seer who\rwas expert in these matters, and was trusted by the people, read the\rportent." \r\rHector looked fiercely at him and said, "Polydamas, I like not of\ryour reading. You can find a better saying than this if you will.\rIf, however, you have spoken in good earnest, then indeed has heaven\rrobbed you of your reason. You would have me pay no heed to the counsels\rof Jove, nor to the promises he made me- and he bowed his head in\rconfirmation; you bid me be ruled rather by the flight of wild-fowl.\rWhat care I whether they fly towards dawn or dark, and whether they\rbe on my right hand or on my left? Let us put our trust rather in\rthe counsel of great Jove, king of mortals and immortals. There is\rone omen, and one only- that a man should fight for his country. Why\rare you so fearful? Though we be all of us slain at the ships of the\rArgives you are not likely to be killed yourself, for you are not\rsteadfast nor courageous. If you will. not fight, or would talk others\rover from doing so, you shall fall forthwith before my spear."\r\rWith these words he led the way, and the others followed after with\ra cry that rent the air. Then Jove the lord of thunder sent the blast\rof a mighty wind from the mountains of Ida, that bore the dust down\rtowards the ships; he thus lulled the Achaeans into security, and\rgave victory to Hector and to the Trojans, who, trusting to their\rown might and to the signs he had shown them, essayed to break through\rthe great wall of the Achaeans. They tore down the breastworks from\rthe walls, and overthrew the battlements; they upheaved the buttresses,\rwhich the Achaeans had set in front of the wall in order to support\rit; when they had pulled these down they made sure of breaking through\rthe wall, but the Danaans still showed no sign of giving ground; they\rstill fenced the battlements with their shields of ox-hide, and hurled\rtheir missiles down upon the foe as soon as any came below the wall.\r\rThe two Ajaxes went about everywhere on the walls cheering on the\rAchaeans, giving fair words to some while they spoke sharply to any\rone whom they saw to be remiss. "My friends," they cried, "Argives\rone and all- good bad and indifferent, for there was never fight yet,\rin which all were of equal prowess- there is now work enough, as you\rvery well know, for all of you. See that you none of you turn in flight\rtowards the ships, daunted by the shouting of the foe, but press forward\rand keep one another in heart, if it may so be that Olympian Jove\rthe lord of lightning will vouchsafe us to repel our foes, and drive\rthem back towards the city." \r\rThus did the two go about shouting and cheering the Achaeans on. As\rthe flakes that fall thick upon a winter\'s day, when Jove is minded\rto snow and to display these his arrows to mankind- he lulls the wind\rto rest, and snows hour after hour till he has buried the tops of\rthe high mountains, the headlands that jut into the sea, the grassy\rplains, and the tilled fields of men; the snow lies deep upon the\rforelands, and havens of the grey sea, but the waves as they come\rrolling in stay it that it can come no further, though all else is\rwrapped as with a mantle so heavy are the heavens with snow- even\rthus thickly did the stones fall on one side and on the other, some\rthrown at the Trojans, and some by the Trojans at the Achaeans; and\rthe whole wall was in an uproar. \r\rStill the Trojans and brave Hector would not yet have broken down\rthe gates and the great bar, had not Jove turned his son Sarpedon\ragainst the Argives as a lion against a herd of horned cattle. Before\rhim he held his shield of hammered bronze, that the smith had beaten\rso fair and round, and had lined with ox hides which he had made fast\rwith rivets of gold all round the shield; this he held in front of\rhim, and brandishing his two spears came on like some lion of the\rwilderness, who has been long famished for want of meat and will dare\rbreak even into a well-fenced homestead to try and get at the sheep.\rHe may find the shepherds keeping watch over their flocks with dogs\rand spears, but he is in no mind to be driven from the fold till he\rhas had a try for it; he will either spring on a sheep and carry it\roff, or be hit by a spear from strong hand- even so was Sarpedon fain\rto attack the wall and break down its battlements. Then he said to\rGlaucus son of Hippolochus, "Glaucus, why in Lycia do we receive especial\rhonour as regards our place at table? Why are the choicest portions\rserved us and our cups kept brimming, and why do men look up to us\ras though we were gods? Moreover we hold a large estate by the banks\rof the river Xanthus, fair with orchard lawns and wheat-growing land;\rit becomes us, therefore, to take our stand at the head of all the\rLycians and bear the brunt of the fight, that one may say to another,\rOur princes in Lycia eat the fat of the land and drink best of wine,\rbut they are fine fellows; they fight well and are ever at the front\rin battle.\' My good friend, if, when we were once out of this fight,\rwe could escape old age and death thenceforward and for ever, I should\rneither press forward myself nor bid you do so, but death in ten thousand\rshapes hangs ever over our heads, and no man can elude him; therefore\rlet us go forward and either win glory for ourselves, or yield it\rto another." \r\rGlaucus heeded his saying, and the pair forthwith led on the host\rof Lycians. Menestheus son of Peteos was dismayed when he saw them,\rfor it was against his part of the wall that they came- bringing destruction\rwith them; he looked along the wall for some chieftain to support\rhis comrades and saw the two Ajaxes, men ever eager for the fray,\rand Teucer, who had just come from his tent, standing near them; but\rhe could not make his voice heard by shouting to them, so great an\ruproar was there from crashing shields and helmets and the battering\rof gates with a din which reached the skies. For all the gates had\rbeen closed, and the Trojans were hammering at them to try and break\rtheir way through them. Menestheus, therefore, sent Thootes with a\rmessage to Ajax. "Run, good Thootes," said and call Ajax, or better\rstill bid both come, for it will be all over with us here directly;\rthe leaders of the Lycians are upon us, men who have ever fought desperately\rheretofore. But if the have too much on their hands to let them come,\rat any rate let Ajax son of Telamon do so, and let Teucer the famous\rbowman come with him." \r\rThe messenger did as he was told, and set off running along the wall\rof the Achaeans. When he reached the Ajaxes he said to them, "Sirs,\rprinces of the Argives, the son of noble Peteos bids you come to him\rfor a while and help him. You had better both come if you can, or\rit will be all over with him directly; the leaders of the Lycians\rare upon him, men who have ever fought desperately heretofore; if\ryou have too much on your hands to let both come, at any rate let\rAjax son of Telamon do so, and let Teucer the famous bowman come with\rhim." \r\rGreat Ajax, son of Telamon, heeded the message, and at once spoke\rto the son of Oileus. "Ajax," said he, "do you two, yourself and brave\rLycomedes, stay here and keep the Danaans in heart to fight their\rhardest. I will go over yonder, and bear my part in the fray, but\rI will come back here at once as soon as I have given them the help\rthey need." \r\rWith this, Ajax son of Telamon set off, and Teucer his brother by\rthe same father went also, with Pandion to carry Teucer\'s bow. They\rwent along inside the wall, and when they came to the tower where\rMenestheus was (and hard pressed indeed did they find him) the brave\rcaptains and leaders of the Lycians were storming the battlements\ras it were a thick dark cloud, fighting in close quarters, and raising\rthe battle-cry aloud. \r\rFirst, Ajax son of Telamon killed brave Epicles, a comrade of Sarpedon,\rhitting him with a jagged stone that lay by the battlements at the\rvery top of the wall. As men now are, even one who is in the bloom\rof youth could hardly lift it with his two hands, but Ajax raised\rit high aloft and flung it down, smashing Epicles\' four-crested helmet\rso that the bones of his head were crushed to pieces, and he fell\rfrom the high wall as though he were diving, with no more life left\rin him. Then Teucer wounded Glaucus the brave son of Hippolochus as\rhe was coming on to attack the wall. He saw his shoulder bare and\raimed an arrow at it, which made Glaucus leave off fighting. Thereon\rhe sprang covertly down for fear some of the Achaeans might see that\rhe was wounded and taunt him. Sarpedon was stung with grief when he\rsaw Glaucus leave him, still he did not leave off fighting, but aimed\rhis spear at Alcmaon the son of Thestor and hit him. He drew his spear\rback again Alcmaon came down headlong after it with his bronzed armour\rrattling round him. Then Sarpedon seized the battlement in his strong\rhands, and tugged at it till it an gave way together, and a breach\rwas made through which many might pass. \r\rAjax and Teucer then both of them attacked him. Teucer hit him with\ran arrow on the band that bore the shield which covered his body,\rbut Jove saved his son from destruction that he might not fall by\rthe ships\' sterns. Meanwhile Ajax sprang on him and pierced his shield,\rbut the spear did not go clean through, though it hustled him back\rthat he could come on no further. He therefore retired a little space\rfrom the battlement, yet without losing all his ground, for he still\rthought to cover himself with glory. Then he turned round and shouted\rto the brave Lycians saying, "Lycians, why do you thus fail me? For\rall my prowess I cannot break through the wall and open a way to the\rships single-handed. Come close on behind me, for the more there are\rof us the better." \r\rThe Lycians, shamed by his rebuke, pressed closer round him who was\rtheir counsellor their king. The Argives on their part got their men\rin fighting order within the wall, and there was a deadly struggle\rbetween them. The Lycians could not break through the wall and force\rtheir way to the ships, nor could the Danaans drive the Lycians from\rthe wall now that they had once reached it. As two men, measuring-rods\rin hand, quarrel about their boundaries in a field that they own in\rcommon, and stickle for their rights though they be but in a mere\rstrip, even so did the battlements now serve as a bone of contention,\rand they beat one another\'s round shields for their possession. Many\ra man\'s body was wounded with the pitiless bronze, as he turned round\rand bared his back to the foe, and many were struck clean through\rtheir shields; the wall and battlements were everywhere deluged with\rthe blood alike of Trojans and of Achaeans. But even so the Trojans\rcould not rout the Achaeans, who still held on; and as some honest\rhard-working woman weighs wool in her balance and sees that the scales\rbe true, for she would gain some pitiful earnings for her little ones,\reven so was the fight balanced evenly between them till the time came\rwhen Jove gave the greater glory to Hector son of Priam, who was first\rto spring towards the wall of the Achaeans. As he did so, he cried\raloud to the Trojans, "Up, Trojans, break the wall of the Argives,\rand fling fire upon their ships." \r\rThus did he hound them on, and in one body they rushed straight at\rthe wall as he had bidden them, and scaled the battlements with sharp\rspears in their hands. Hector laid hold of a stone that lay just outside\rthe gates and was thick at one end but pointed at the other; two of\rthe best men in a town, as men now are, could hardly raise it from\rthe ground and put it on to a waggon, but Hector lifted it quite easily\rby himself, for the son of scheming Saturn made it light for him.\rAs a shepherd picks up a ram\'s fleece with one hand and finds it no\rburden, so easily did Hector lift the great stone and drive it right\rat the doors that closed the gates so strong and so firmly set. These\rdoors were double and high, and were kept closed by two cross-bars\rto which there was but one key. When he had got close up to them,\rHector strode towards them that his blow might gain in force and struck\rthem in the middle, leaning his whole weight against them. He broke\rboth hinges, and the stone fell inside by reason of its great weight.\rThe portals re-echoed with the sound, the bars held no longer, and\rthe doors flew open, one one way, and the other the other, through\rthe force of the blow. Then brave Hector leaped inside with a face\ras dark as that of flying night. The gleaming bronze flashed fiercely\rabout his body and he had tow spears in his hand. None but a god could\rhave withstood him as he flung himself into the gateway, and his eyes\rglared like fire. Then he turned round towards the Trojans and called\ron them to scale the wall, and they did as he bade them- some of them\rat once climbing over the wall, while others passed through the gates.\rThe Danaans then fled panic-stricken towards their ships, and all\rwas uproar and confusion. \r\r----------------------------------------------------------------------\r\rBOOK XIII\r\rNow when Jove had thus brought Hector and the Trojans to the ships,\rhe left them to their never-ending toil, and turned his keen eyes\raway, looking elsewhither towards the horse-breeders of Thrace, the\rMysians, fighters at close quarters, the noble Hippemolgi, who live\ron milk, and the Abians, justest of mankind. He no longer turned so\rmuch as a glance towards Troy, for he did not think that any of the\rimmortals would go and help either Trojans or Danaans. \r\rBut King Neptune had kept no blind look-out; he had been looking admiringly\ron the battle from his seat on the topmost crests of wooded Samothrace,\rwhence he could see all Ida, with the city of Priam and the ships\rof the Achaeans. He had come from under the sea and taken his place\rhere, for he pitied the Achaeans who were being overcome by the Trojans;\rand he was furiously angry with Jove. \r\rPresently he came down from his post on the mountain top, and as he\rstrode swiftly onwards the high hills and the forest quaked beneath\rthe tread of his immortal feet. Three strides he took, and with the\rfourth he reached his goal- Aegae, where is his glittering golden\rpalace, imperishable, in the depths of the sea. When he got there,\rhe yoked his fleet brazen-footed steeds with their manes of gold all\rflying in the wind; he clothed himself in raiment of gold, grasped\rhis gold whip, and took his stand upon his chariot. As he went his\rway over the waves the sea-monsters left their lairs, for they knew\rtheir lord, and came gambolling round him from every quarter of the\rdeep, while the sea in her gladness opened a path before his chariot.\rSo lightly did the horses fly that the bronze axle of the car was\rnot even wet beneath it; and thus his bounding steeds took him to\rthe ships of the Achaeans. \r\rNow there is a certain huge cavern in the depths of the sea midway\rbetween Tenedos and rocky Imbrus; here Neptune lord of the earthquake\rstayed his horses, unyoked them, and set before them their ambrosial\rforage. He hobbled their feet with hobbles of gold which none could\reither unloose or break, so that they might stay there in that place\runtil their lord should return. This done he went his way to the host\rof the Achaeans. \r\rNow the Trojans followed Hector son of Priam in close array like a\rstorm-cloud or flame of fire, fighting with might and main and raising\rthe cry battle; for they deemed that they should take the ships of\rthe Achaeans and kill all their chiefest heroes then and there. Meanwhile\rearth-encircling Neptune lord of the earthquake cheered on the Argives,\rfor he had come up out of the sea and had assumed the form and voice\rof Calchas. \r\rFirst he spoke to the two Ajaxes, who were doing their best already,\rand said, "Ajaxes, you two can be the saving of the Achaeans if you\rwill put out all your strength and not let yourselves be daunted.\rI am not afraid that the Trojans, who have got over the wall in force,\rwill be victorious in any other part, for the Achaeans can hold all\rof them in check, but I much fear that some evil will befall us here\rwhere furious Hector, who boasts himself the son of great Jove himself,\ris leading them on like a pillar of flame. May some god, then, put\rit into your hearts to make a firm stand here, and to incite others\rto do the like. In this case you will drive him from the ships even\rthough he be inspired by Jove himself." \r\rAs he spoke the earth-encircling lord of the earthquake struck both\rof them with his sceptre and filled their hearts with daring. He made\rtheir legs light and active, as also their hands and their feet. Then,\ras the soaring falcon poises on the wing high above some sheer rock,\rand presently swoops down to chase some bird over the plain, even\rso did Neptune lord of the earthquake wing his flight into the air\rand leave them. Of the two, swift Ajax son of Oileus was the first\rto know who it was that had been speaking with them, and said to Ajax\rson of Telamon, "Ajax, this is one of the gods that dwell on Olympus,\rwho in the likeness of the prophet is bidding us fight hard by our\rships. It was not Calchas the seer and diviner of omens; I knew him\rat once by his feet and knees as he turned away, for the gods are\rsoon recognised. Moreover I feel the lust of battle burn more fiercely\rwithin me, while my hands and my feet under me are more eager for\rthe fray." \r\rAnd Ajax son of Telamon answered, "I too feel my hands grasp my spear\rmore firmly; my strength is greater, and my feet more nimble; I long,\rmoreover, to meet furious Hector son of Priam, even in single combat."\r\rThus did they converse, exulting in the hunger after battle with which\rthe god had filled them. Meanwhile the earth-encircler roused the\rAchaeans, who were resting in the rear by the ships overcome at once\rby hard fighting and by grief at seeing that the Trojans had got over\rthe wall in force. Tears began falling from their eyes as they beheld\rthem, for they made sure that they should not escape destruction;\rbut the lord of the earthquake passed lightly about among them and\rurged their battalions to the front. \r\rFirst he went up to Teucer and Leitus, the hero Peneleos, and Thoas\rand Deipyrus; Meriones also and Antilochus, valiant warriors; all\rdid he exhort. "Shame on you young Argives," he cried, "it was on\ryour prowess I relied for the saving of our ships; if you fight not\rwith might and main, this very day will see us overcome by the Trojans.\rOf a truth my eyes behold a great and terrible portent which I had\rnever thought to see- the Trojans at our ships- they, who were heretofore\rlike panic-stricken hinds, the prey of jackals and wolves in a forest,\rwith no strength but in flight for they cannot defend themselves.\rHitherto the Trojans dared not for one moment face the attack of the\rAchaeans, but now they have sallied far from their city and are fighting\rat our very ships through the cowardice of our leader and the disaffection\rof the people themselves, who in their discontent care not to fight\rin defence of the ships but are being slaughtered near them. True,\rKing Agamemnon son of Atreus is the cause of our disaster by having\rinsulted the son of Peleus, still this is no reason why we should\rleave off fighting. Let us be quick to heal, for the hearts of the\rbrave heal quickly. You do ill to be thus remiss, you, who are the\rfinest soldiers in our whole army. I blame no man for keeping out\rof battle if he is a weakling, but I am indignant with such men as\ryou are. My good friends, matters will soon become even worse through\rthis slackness; think, each one of you, of his own honour and credit,\rfor the hazard of the fight is extreme. Great Hector is now fighting\rat our ships; he has broken through the gates and the strong bolt\rthat held them." \r\rThus did the earth-encircler address the Achaeans and urge them on.\rThereon round the two Ajaxes there gathered strong bands of men, of\rwhom not even Mars nor Minerva, marshaller of hosts could make light\rif they went among them, for they were the picked men of all those\rwho were now awaiting the onset of Hector and the Trojans. They made\ra living fence, spear to spear, shield to shield, buckler to buckler,\rhelmet to helmet, and man to man. The horse-hair crests on their gleaming\rhelmets touched one another as they nodded forward, so closely seffied\rwere they; the spears they brandished in their strong hands were interlaced,\rand their hearts were set on battle. \r\rThe Trojans advanced in a dense body, with Hector at their head pressing\rright on as a rock that comes thundering down the side of some mountain\rfrom whose brow the winter torrents have torn it; the foundations\rof the dull thing have been loosened by floods of rain, and as it\rbounds headlong on its way it sets the whole forest in an uproar;\rit swerves neither to right nor left till it reaches level ground,\rbut then for all its fury it can go no further- even so easily did\rHector for a while seem as though he would career through the tents\rand ships of the Achaeans till he had reached the sea in his murderous\rcourse; but the closely serried battalions stayed him when he reached\rthem, for the sons of the Achaeans thrust at him with swords and spears\rpointed at both ends, and drove him from them so that he staggered\rand gave ground; thereon he shouted to the Trojans, "Trojans, Lycians,\rand Dardanians, fighters in close combat, stand firm: the Achaeans\rhave set themselves as a wall against me, but they will not check\rme for long; they will give ground before me if the mightiest of the\rgods, the thundering spouse of Juno, has indeed inspired my onset."\r\rWith these words he put heart and soul into them all. Deiphobus son\rof Priam went about among them intent on deeds of daring with his\rround shield before him, under cover of which he strode quickly forward.\rMeriones took aim at him with a spear, nor did he fail to hit the\rbroad orb of ox-hide; but he was far from piercing it for the spear\rbroke in two pieces long ere he could do so; moreover Deiphobus had\rseen it coming and had held his shield well away from him. Meriones\rdrew back under cover of his comrades, angry alike at having failed\rto vanquish Deiphobus, and having broken his spear. He turned therefore\rtowards the ships and tents to fetch a spear which he had left behind\rin his tent. \r\rThe others continued fighting, and the cry of battle rose up into\rthe heavens. Teucer son of Telamon was the first to kill his man,\rto wit, the warrior Imbrius son of Mentor rich in horses. Until the\rAchaeans came he had lived in Pedaeum, and had married Medesicaste\ra bastard daughter of Priam; but on the arrival of the Danaan fleet\rhe had gone back to Ilius, and was a great man among the Trojans,\rdwelling near Priam himself, who gave him like honour with his own\rsons. The son of Telamon now struck him under the ear with a spear\rwhich he then drew back again, and Imbrius fell headlong as an ash-tree\rwhen it is felled on the crest of some high mountain beacon, and its\rdelicate green foliage comes toppling down to the ground. Thus did\rhe fall with his bronze-dight armour ringing harshly round him, and\rTeucer sprang forward with intent to strip him of his armour; but\ras he was doing so, Hector took aim at him with a spear. Teucer saw\rthe spear coming and swerved aside, whereon it hit Amphimachus, son\rof Cteatus son of Actor, in the chest as he was coming into battle,\rand his armour rang rattling round him as he fell heavily to the ground.\rHector sprang forward to take Amphimachus\'s helmet from off his temples,\rand in a moment Ajax threw a spear at him, but did not wound him,\rfor he was encased all over in his terrible armour; nevertheless the\rspear struck the boss of his shield with such force as to drive him\rback from the two corpses, which the Achaeans then drew off. Stichius\rand Menestheus, captains of the Athenians, bore away Amphimachus to\rthe host of the Achaeans, while the two brave and impetuous Ajaxes\rdid the like by Imbrius. As two lions snatch a goat from the hounds\rthat have it in their fangs, and bear it through thick brushwood high\rabove the ground in their jaws, thus did the Ajaxes bear aloft the\rbody of Imbrius, and strip it of its armour. Then the son of Oileus\rsevered the head from the neck in revenge for the death of Amphimachus,\rand sent it whirling over the crowd as though it had been a ball,\rtill fell in the dust at Hector\'s feet. \r\rNeptune was exceedingly angry that his grandson Amphimachus should\rhave fallen; he therefore went to the tents and ships of the Achaeans\rto urge the Danaans still further, and to devise evil for the Trojans.\rIdomeneus met him, as he was taking leave of a comrade, who had just\rcome to him from the fight, wounded in the knee. His fellow-soldiers\rbore him off the field, and Idomeneus having given orders to the physicians\rwent on to his tent, for he was still thirsting for battle. Neptune\rspoke in the likeness and with the voice of Thoas son of Andraemon\rwho ruled the Aetolians of all Pleuron and high Calydon, and was honoured\ramong his people as though he were a god. "Idomeneus," said he, "lawgiver\rto the Cretans, what has now become of the threats with which the\rsons of the Achaeans used to threaten the Trojans?" \r\rAnd Idomeneus chief among the Cretans answered, "Thoas, no one, so\rfar as I know, is in fault, for we can all fight. None are held back\rneither by fear nor slackness, but it seems to be the of almighty\rJove that the Achaeans should perish ingloriously here far from Argos:\ryou, Thoas, have been always staunch, and you keep others in heart\rif you see any fail in duty; be not then remiss now, but exhort all\rto do their utmost." \r\rTo this Neptune lord of the earthquake made answer, "Idomeneus, may\rhe never return from Troy, but remain here for dogs to batten upon,\rwho is this day wilfully slack in fighting. Get your armour and go,\rwe must make all haste together if we may be of any use, though we\rare only two. Even cowards gain courage from companionship, and we\rtwo can hold our own with the bravest." \r\rTherewith the god went back into the thick of the fight, and Idomeneus\rwhen he had reached his tent donned his armour, grasped his two spears,\rand sallied forth. As the lightning which the son of Saturn brandishes\rfrom bright Olympus when he would show a sign to mortals, and its\rgleam flashes far and wide- even so did his armour gleam about him\ras he ran. Meriones his sturdy squire met him while he was still near\rhis tent (for he was going to fetch his spear) and Idomeneus said\r\r"Meriones, fleet son of Molus, best of comrades, why have you left\rthe field? Are you wounded, and is the point of the weapon hurting\ryou? or have you been sent to fetch me? I want no fetching; I had\rfar rather fight than stay in my tent." \r\r"Idomeneus," answered Meriones, "I come for a spear, if I can find\rone in my tent; I have broken the one I had, in throwing it at the\rshield of Deiphobus." \r\rAnd Idomeneus captain of the Cretans answered, "You will find one\rspear, or twenty if you so please, standing up against the end wall\rof my tent. I have taken them from Trojans whom I have killed, for\rI am not one to keep my enemy at arm\'s length; therefore I have spears,\rbossed shields, helmets, and burnished corslets." \r\rThen Meriones said, "I too in my tent and at my ship have spoils taken\rfrom the Trojans, but they are not at hand. I have been at all times\rvalorous, and wherever there has been hard fighting have held my own\ramong the foremost. There may be those among the Achaeans who do not\rknow how I fight, but you know it well enough yourself."\r\rIdomeneus answered, "I know you for a brave man: you need not tell\rme. If the best men at the ships were being chosen to go on an ambush-\rand there is nothing like this for showing what a man is made of;\rit comes out then who is cowardly and who brave; the coward will change\rcolour at every touch and turn; he is full of fears, and keeps shifting\rhis weight first on one knee and then on the other; his heart beats\rfast as he thinks of death, and one can hear the chattering of his\rteeth; whereas the brave man will not change colour nor be on finding\rhimself in ambush, but is all the time longing to go into action-\rif the best men were being chosen for such a service, no one could\rmake light of your courage nor feats of arms. If you were struck by\ra dart or smitten in close combat, it would not be from behind, in\ryour neck nor back, but the weapon would hit you in the chest or belly\ras you were pressing forward to a place in the front ranks. But let\rus no longer stay here talking like children, lest we be ill spoken\rof; go, fetch your spear from the tent at once." \r\rOn this Meriones, peer of Mars, went to the tent and got himself a\rspear of bronze. He then followed after Idomeneus, big with great\rdeeds of valour. As when baneful Mars sallies forth to battle, and\rhis son Panic so strong and dauntless goes with him, to strike terror\reven into the heart of a hero- the pair have gone from Thrace to arm\rthemselves among the Ephyri or the brave Phlegyans, but they will\rnot listen to both the contending hosts, and will give victory to\rone side or to the other- even so did Meriones and Idomeneus, captains\rof men, go out to battle clad in their bronze armour. Meriones was\rfirst to speak. "Son of Deucalion," said he, "where would you have\rus begin fighting? On the right wing of the host, in the centre, or\ron the left wing, where I take it the Achaeans will be weakest?"\r\rIdomeneus answered, "There are others to defend the centre- the two\rAjaxes and Teucer, who is the finest archer of all the Achaeans, and\ris good also in a hand-to-hand fight. These will give Hector son of\rPriam enough to do; fight as he may, he will find it hard to vanquish\rtheir indomitable fury, and fire the ships, unless the son of Saturn\rfling a firebrand upon them with his own hand. Great Ajax son of Telamon\rwill yield to no man who is in mortal mould and eats the grain of\rCeres, if bronze and great stones can overthrow him. He would not\ryield even to Achilles in hand-to-hand fight, and in fleetness of\rfoot there is none to beat him; let us turn therefore towards the\rleft wing, that we may know forthwith whether we are to give glory\rto some other, or he to us." \r\rMeriones, peer of fleet Mars, then led the way till they came to the\rpart of the host which Idomeneus had named. \r\rNow when the Trojans saw Idomeneus coming on like a flame of fire,\rhim and his squire clad in their richly wrought armour, they shouted\rand made towards him all in a body, and a furious hand-to-hand fight\rraged under the ships\' sterns. Fierce as the shrill winds that whistle\rupon a day when dust lies deep on the roads, and the gusts raise it\rinto a thick cloud- even such was the fury of the combat, and might\rand main did they hack at each other with spear and sword throughout\rthe host. The field bristled with the long and deadly spears which\rthey bore. Dazzling was the sheen of their gleaming helmets, their\rfresh-burnished breastplates, and glittering shields as they joined\rbattle with one another. Iron indeed must be his courage who could\rtake pleasure in the sight of such a turmoil, and look on it without\rbeing dismayed. \r\rThus did the two mighty sons of Saturn devise evil for mortal heroes.\rJove was minded to give victory to the Trojans and to Hector, so as\rto do honour to fleet Achilles, nevertheless he did not mean to utterly\roverthrow the Achaean host before Ilius, and only wanted to glorify\rThetis and her valiant son. Neptune on the other hand went about among\rthe Argives to incite them, having come up from the grey sea in secret,\rfor he was grieved at seeing them vanquished by the Trojans, and was\rfuriously angry with Jove. Both were of the same race and country,\rbut Jove was elder born and knew more, therefore Neptune feared to\rdefend the Argives openly, but in the likeness of man, he kept on\rencouraging them throughout their host. Thus, then, did these two\rdevise a knot of war and battle, that none could unloose or break,\rand set both sides tugging at it, to the failing of men\'s knees beneath\rthem. \r\rAnd now Idomeneus, though his hair was already flecked with grey,\rcalled loud on the Danaans and spread panic among the Trojans as he\rleaped in among them. He slew Othryoneus from Cabesus, a sojourner,\rwho had but lately come to take part in the war. He sought Cassandra\rthe fairest of Priam\'s daughters in marriage, but offered no gifts\rof wooing, for he promised a great thing, to wit, that he would drive\rthe sons of the Achaeans willy nilly from Troy; old King Priam had\rgiven his consent and promised her to him, whereon he fought on the\rstrength of the promises thus made to him. Idomeneus aimed a spear,\rand hit him as he came striding on. His cuirass of bronze did not\rprotect him, and the spear stuck in his belly, so that he fell heavily\rto the ground. Then Idomeneus vaunted over him saying, "Othryoneus,\rthere is no one in the world whom I shall admire more than I do you,\rif you indeed perform what you have promised Priam son of Dardanus\rin return for his daughter. We too will make you an offer; we will\rgive you the loveliest daughter of the son of Atreus, and will bring\rher from Argos for you to marry, if you will sack the goodly city\rof Ilius in company with ourselves; so come along with me, that we\rmay make a covenant at the ships about the marriage, and we will not\rbe hard upon you about gifts of wooing." \r\rWith this Idomeneus began dragging him by the foot through the thick\rof the fight, but Asius came up to protect the body, on foot, in front\rof his horses which his esquire drove so close behind him that he\rcould feel their \'breath upon his shoulder. He was longing to strike\rdown Idomeneus, but ere he could do so Idomeneus smote him with his\rspear in the throat under the chin, and the bronze point went clean\rthrough it. He fell as an oak, or poplar, or pine which shipwrights\rhave felled for ship\'s timber upon the mountains with whetted axes-\reven thus did he lie full length in front of his chariot and horses,\rgrinding his teeth and clutching at the bloodstained just. His charioteer\rwas struck with panic and did not dare turn his horses round and escape:\rthereupon Antilochus hit him in the middle of his body with a spear;\rhis cuirass of bronze did not protect him, and the spear stuck in\rhis belly. He fell gasping from his chariot and Antilochus great Nestor\'s\rson, drove his horses from the Trojans to the Achaeans. \r\rDeiphobus then came close up to Idomeneus to avenge Asius, and took\raim at him with a spear, but Idomeneus was on the look-out and avoided\rit, for he was covered by the round shield he always bore- a shield\rof oxhide and bronze with two arm-rods on the inside. He crouched\runder cover of this, and the spear flew over him, but the shield rang\rout as the spear grazed it, and the weapon sped not in vain from the\rstrong hand of Deiphobus, for it struck Hypsenor son of Hippasus,\rshepherd of his people, in the liver under the midriff, and his limbs\rfailed beneath him. Deiphobus vaunted over him and cried with a loud\rvoice saying, "Of a truth Asius has not fallen unavenied; he will\rbe glad even while passing into the house of Hades, strong warden\rof the gate, that I have sent some one to escort him." \r\rThus did he vaunt, and the Argives were stung by his saying. Noble\rAntilochus was more angry than any one, but grief did not make him\rforget his friend and comrade. He ran up to him, bestrode him, and\rcovered him with his shield; then two of his staunch comrades, Mecisteus\rson of Echius, and Alastor stooped down, and bore him away groaning\rheavily to the ships. But Idomeneus ceased not his fury. He kept on\rstriving continually either to enshroud some Trojan in the darkness\rof death, or himself to fall while warding off the evil day from the\rAchaeans. Then fell Alcathous son of noble Aesyetes: he was son-in-law\rto Anchises, having married his eldest daughter Hippodameia who was\rthe darling of her father and mother, and excelled all her generation\rin beauty, accomplishments, and understanding, wherefore the bravest\rman in all Troy had taken her to wife- him did Neptune lay low by\rthe hand of Idomeneus, blinding his bright eyes and binding his strong\rlimbs in fetters so that he could neither go back nor to one side,\rbut stood stock still like pillar or lofty tree when Idomeneus struck\rhim with a spear in the middle of his chest. The coat of mail that\rhad hitherto protected his body was now broken, and rang harshly as\rthe spear tore through it. He fell heavily to the ground, and the\rspear stuck in his heart, which still beat, and made the butt-end\rof the spear quiver till dread Mars put an end to his life. Idomeneus\rvaunted over him and cried with a loud voice saying, "Deiphobus, since\ryou are in a mood to vaunt, shall we cry quits now that we have killed\rthree men to your one? Nay, sir, stand in fight with me yourself,\rthat you may learn what manner of Jove-begotten man am I that have\rcome hither. Jove first begot Minos chief ruler in Crete, and Minos\rin his turn begot a son, noble Deucalion; Deucalion begot me to be\ra ruler over many men in Crete, and my ships have now brought me hither,\rto be the bane of yourself, your father, and the Trojans."\r\rThus did he speak, and Deiphobus was in two minds, whether to go back\rand fetch some other Trojan to help him, or to take up the challenge\rsingle-handed. In the end, he deemed it best to go and fetch Aeneas,\rwhom he found standing in the rear, for he had long been aggrieved\rwith Priam because in spite his brave deeds he did not give him his\rdue share of honour. Deiphobus went up to him and said, "Aeneas, prince\ramong the Trojans, if you know any ties of kinship, help me now to\rdefend the body of your sister\'s husband; come with me to the rescue\rof Alcathous, who being husband to your sister brought you up when\ryou were a child in his house, and now Idomeneus has slain him."\r\rWith these words he moved the heart of Aeneas, and he went in pursuit\rof Idomeneus, big with great deeds of valour; but Idomeneus was not\rto be thus daunted as though he were a mere child; he held his ground\ras a wild boar at bay upon the mountains, who abides the coming of\ra great crowd of men in some lonely place- the bristles stand upright\ron his back, his eyes flash fire, and he whets his tusks in his eagerness\rto defend himself against hounds and men- even so did famed Idomeneus\rhold his ground and budge not at the coming of Aeneas. He cried aloud\rto his comrades looking towards Ascalaphus, Aphareus, Deipyrus, Meriones,\rand Antilochus, all of them brave soldiers- "Hither my friends," he\rcried, "and leave me not single-handed- I go in great fear by fleet\rAeneas, who is coming against me, and is a redoubtable dispenser of\rdeath battle. Moreover he is in the flower of youth when a man\'s strength\ris greatest; if I was of the same age as he is and in my present mind,\reither he or I should soon bear away the prize of victory\r\rOn this, all of them as one man stood near him, shield on shoulder.\rAeneas on the other side called to his comrades, looking towards Deiphobus,\rParis, and Agenor, who were leaders of the Trojans along with himself,\rand the people followed them as sheep follow the ram when they go\rdown to drink after they have been feeding, and the heart of the shepherd\ris glad- even so was the heart of Aeneas gladdened when he saw his\rpeople follow him. \r\rThen they fought furiously in close combat about the body of Alcathous,\rwielding their long spears; and the bronze armour about their bodies\rrang fearfully as they took aim at one another in the press of the\rfight, while the two heroes Aeneas and Idomeneus, peers of Mars, outxied\revery one in their desire to hack at each other with sword and spear.\rAeneas took aim first, but Idomeneus was on the lookout and avoided\rthe spear, so that it sped from Aeneas\' strong hand in vain, and fell\rquivering in the ground. Idomeneus meanwhile smote Oenomaus in the\rmiddle of his belly, and broke the plate of his corslet, whereon his\rbowels came gushing out and he clutched the earth in the palms of\rhis hands as he fell sprawling in the dust. Idomeneus drew his spear\rout of the body, but could not strip him of the rest of his armour\rfor the rain of darts that were showered upon him: moreover his strength\rwas now beginning to fail him so that he could no longer charge, and\rcould neither spring forward to recover his own weapon nor swerve\raside to avoid one that was aimed at him; therefore, though he still\rdefended himself in hand-to-hand fight, his heavy feet could not bear\rhim swiftly out of the battle. Deiphobus aimed a spear at him as he\rwas retreating slowly from the field, for his bitterness against him\rwas as fierce as ever, but again he missed him, and hit Ascalaphus,\rthe son of Mars; the spear went through his shoulder, and he clutched\rthe earth in the palms of his hands as he fell sprawling in the dust.\r\rGrim Mars of awful voice did not yet know that his son had fallen,\rfor he was sitting on the summits of Olympus under the golden clouds,\rby command of Jove, where the other gods were also sitting, forbidden\rto take part in the battle. Meanwhile men fought furiously about the\rbody. Deiphobus tore the helmet from off his head, but Meriones sprang\rupon him, and struck him on the arm with a spear so that the visored\rhelmet fell from his hand and came ringing down upon the ground. Thereon\rMeriones sprang upon him like a vulture, drew the spear from his shoulder,\rand fell back under cover of his men. Then Polites, own brother of\rDeiphobus passed his arms around his waist, and bore him away from\rthe battle till he got to his horses that were standing in the rear\rof the fight with the chariot and their driver. These took him towards\rthe city groaning and in great pain, with the blood flowing from his\rarm. \r\rThe others still fought on, and the battle-cry rose to heaven without\rceasing. Aeneas sprang on Aphareus son of Caletor, and struck him\rwith a spear in his throat which was turned towards him; his head\rfell on one side, his helmet and shield came down along with him,\rand death, life\'s foe, was shed around him. Antilochus spied his chance,\rflew forward towards Thoon, and wounded him as he was turning round.\rHe laid open the vein that runs all the way up the back to the neck;\rhe cut this vein clean away throughout its whole course, and Thoon\rfell in the dust face upwards, stretching out his hands imploringly\rtowards his comrades. Antilochus sprang upon him and stripped the\rarmour from his shoulders, glaring round him fearfully as he did so.\rThe Trojans came about him on every side and struck his broad and\rgleaming shield, but could not wound his body, for Neptune stood guard\rover the son of Nestor, though the darts fell thickly round him. He\rwas never clear of the foe, but was always in the thick of the fight;\rhis spear was never idle; he poised and aimed it in every direction,\rso eager was he to hit some one from a distance or to fight him hand\rto hand. \r\rAs he was thus aiming among the crowd, he was seen by Adamas son of\rAsius, who rushed towards him and struck him with a spear in the middle\rof his shield, but Neptune made its point without effect, for he grudged\rhim the life of Antilochus. One half, therefore, of the spear stuck\rfast like a charred stake in Antilochus\'s shield, while the other\rlay on the ground. Adamas then sought shelter under cover of his men,\rbut Meriones followed after and hit him with a spear midway between\rthe private parts and the navel, where a wound is particualrly painful\rto wretched mortals. There did Meriones transfix him, and he writhed\rconvulsively about the spear as some bull whom mountain herdsmen have\rbound with ropes of withes and are taking away perforce. Even so did\rhe move convulsively for a while, but not for very long, till Meriones\rcame up and drew the spear out of his body, and his eyes were veiled\rin darkness. \r\rHelenus then struck Deipyrus with a great Thracian sword, hitting\rhim on the temple in close combat and tearing the helmet from his\rhead; the helmet fell to the ground, and one of those who were fighting\ron the Achaean side took charge of it as it rolled at his feet, but\rthe eyes of Deipyrus were closed in the darkness of death.\r\rOn this Menelaus was grieved, and made menacingly towards Helenus,\rbrandishing his spear; but Helenus drew his bow, and the two attacked\rone another at one and the same moment, the one with his spear, and\rthe other with his bow and arrow. The son of Priam hit the breastplate\rof Menelaus\'s corslet, but the arrow glanced from off it. As black\rbeans or pulse come pattering down on to a threshing-floor from the\rbroad winnowing-shovel, blown by shrill winds and shaken by the shovel-\reven so did the arrow glance off and recoil from the shield of Menelaus,\rwho in his turn wounded the hand with which Helenus carried his bow;\rthe spear went right through his hand and stuck in the bow itself,\rso that to his life he retreated under cover of his men, with his\rhand dragging by his side- for the spear weighed it down till Agenor\rdrew it out and bound the hand carefully up in a woollen sling which\rhis esquire had with him. \r\rPisander then made straight at Menelaus- his evil destiny luring him\ron to his doom, for he was to fall in fight with you, O Menelaus.\rWhen the two were hard by one another the spear of the son of Atreus\rturned aside and he missed his aim; Pisander then struck the shield\rof brave Menelaus but could not pierce it, for the shield stayed the\rspear and broke the shaft; nevertheless he was glad and made sure\rof victory; forthwith, however, the son of Atreus drew his sword and\rsprang upon him. Pisander then seized the bronze battle-axe, with\rits long and polished handle of olive wood that hung by his side under\rhis shield, and the two made at one another. Pisander struck the peak\rof Menelaus\'s crested helmet just under the crest itself, and Menelaus\rhit Pisander as he was coming towards him, on the forehead, just at\rthe rise of his nose; the bones cracked and his two gore-bedrabbled\reyes fell by his feet in the dust. He fell backwards to the ground,\rand Menelaus set his heel upon him, stripped him of his armour, and\rvaunted over him saying, "Even thus shall you Trojans leave the ships\rof the Achaeans, proud and insatiate of battle though you be: nor\rshall you lack any of the disgrace and shame which you have heaped\rupon myself. Cowardly she-wolves that you are, you feared not the\ranger of dread Jove, avenger of violated hospitality, who will one\rday destroy your city; you stole my wedded wife and wickedly carried\roff much treasure when you were her guest, and now you would fling\rfire upon our ships, and kill our heroes. A day will come when, rage\ras you may, you shall be stayed. O father Jove, you, who they say\rart above all both gods and men in wisdom, and from whom all things\rthat befall us do proceed, how can you thus favour the Trojans- men\rso proud and overweening, that they are never tired of fighting? All\rthings pall after a while- sleep, love, sweet song, and stately dance-\rstill these are things of which a man would surely have his fill rather\rthan of battle, whereas it is of battle that the Trojans are insatiate."\r\rSo saying Menelaus stripped the blood-stained armour from the body\rof Pisander, and handed it over to his men; then he again ranged himself\ramong those who were in the front of the fight. \r\rHarpalion son of King Pylaemenes then sprang upon him; he had come\rto fight at Troy along with his father, but he did not go home again.\rHe struck the middle of Menelaus\'s shield with his spear but could\rnot pierce it, and to save his life drew back under cover of his men,\rlooking round him on every side lest he should be wounded. But Meriones\raimed a bronze-tipped arrow at him as he was leaving the field, and\rhit him on the right buttock; the arrow pierced the bone through and\rthrough, and penetrated the bladder, so he sat down where he was and\rbreathed his last in the arms of his comrades, stretched like a worm\rupon the ground and watering the earth with the blood that flowed\rfrom his wound. The brave Paphlagonians tended him with all due care;\rthey raised him into his chariot, and bore him sadly off to the city\rof Troy; his father went also with him weeping bitterly, but there\rwas no ransom that could bring his dead son to life again.\r\rParis was deeply grieved by the death of Harpalion, who was his host\rwhen he went among the Paphlagonians; he aimed an arrow, therefore,\rin order to avenge him. Now there was a certain man named Euchenor,\rson of Polyidus the prophet, a brave man and wealthy, whose home was\rin Corinth. This Euchenor had set sail for Troy well knowing that\rit would be the death of him, for his good old father Polyidus had\roften told him that he must either stay at home and die of a terrible\rdisease, or go with the Achaeans and perish at the hands of the Trojans;\rhe chose, therefore, to avoid incurring the heavy fine the Achaeans\rwould have laid upon him, and at the same time to escape the pain\rand suffering of disease. Paris now smote him on the jaw under his\rear, whereon the life went out of him and he was enshrouded in the\rdarkness of death. \r\rThus then did they fight as it were a flaming fire. But Hector had\rnot yet heard, and did not know that the Argives were making havoc\rof his men on the left wing of the battle, where the Achaeans ere\rlong would have triumphed over them, so vigorously did Neptune cheer\rthem on and help them. He therefore held on at the point where he\rhad first forced his way through the gates and the wall, after breaking\rthrough the serried ranks of Danaan warriors. It was here that the\rships of Ajax and Protesilaus were drawn up by the sea-shore; here\rthe wall was at its lowest, and the fight both of man and horse raged\rmost fiercely.
