The Boeotians and the Ionians with their long tunics,\rthe Locrians, the men of Phthia, and the famous force of the Epeans\rcould hardly stay Hector as he rushed on towards the ships, nor could\rthey drive him from them, for he was as a wall of fire. The chosen\rmen of the Athenians were in the van, led by Menestheus son of Peteos,\rwith whom were also Pheidas, Stichius, and stalwart Bias: Meges son\rof Phyleus, Amphion, and Dracius commanded the Epeans, while Medon\rand staunch Podarces led the men of Phthia. Of these, Medon was bastard\rson to Oileus and brother of Ajax, but he lived in Phylace away from\rhis own country, for he had killed the brother of his stepmother Eriopis,\rthe wife of Oileus; the other, Podarces, was the son of Iphiclus son\rof Phylacus. These two stood in the van of the Phthians, and defended\rthe ships along with the Boeotians. \r\rAjax son of Oileus never for a moment left the side of Ajax son of\rTelamon, but as two swart oxen both strain their utmost at the plough\rwhich they are drawing in a fallow field, and the sweat steams upwards\rfrom about the roots of their horns- nothing but the yoke divides\rthem as they break up the ground till they reach the end of the field-\reven so did the two Ajaxes stand shoulder to shoulder by one another.\rMany and brave comrades followed the son of Telamon, to relieve him\rof his shield when he was overcome with sweat and toil, but the Locrians\rdid not follow so close after the son of Oileus, for they could not\rhold their own in a hand-to-hand fight. They had no bronze helmets\rwith plumes of horse-hair, neither had they shields nor ashen spears,\rbut they had come to Troy armed with bows, and with slings of twisted\rwool from which they showered their missiles to break the ranks of\rthe Trojans. The others, therefore, with their heavy armour bore the\rbrunt of the fight with the Trojans and with Hector, while the Locrians\rshot from behind, under their cover; and thus the Trojans began to\rlose heart, for the arrows threw them into confusion. \r\rThe Trojans would now have been driven in sorry plight from the ships\rand tents back to windy Ilius, had not Polydamas presently said to\rHector, "Hector, there is no persuading you to take advice. Because\rheaven has so richly endowed you with the arts of war, you think that\ryou must therefore excel others in counsel; but you cannot thus claim\rpreeminence in all things. Heaven has made one man an excellent soldier;\rof another it has made a dancer or a singer and player on the lyre;\rwhile yet in another Jove has implanted a wise understanding of which\rmen reap fruit to the saving of many, and he himself knows more about\rit than any one; therefore I will say what I think will be best. The\rfight has hemmed you in as with a circle of fire, and even now that\rthe Trojans are within the wall some of them stand aloof in full armour,\rwhile others are fighting scattered and outnumbered near the ships.\rDraw back, therefore, and call your chieftains round you, that we\rmay advise together whether to fall now upon the ships in the hope\rthat heaven may vouchsafe us victory, or to beat a retreat while we\rcan yet safely do so. I greatly fear that the Achaeans will pay us\rtheir debt of yesterday in full, for there is one abiding at their\rships who is never weary of battle, and who will not hold aloof much\rlonger." \r\rThus spoke Polydamas, and his words pleased Hector well. He sprang\rin full armour from his chariot and said, "Polydamas, gather the chieftains\rhere; I will go yonder into the fight, but will return at once when\rI have given them their orders." \r\rHe then sped onward, towering like a snowy mountain, and with a loud\rcry flew through the ranks of the Trojans and their allies. When they\rheard his voice they all hastened to gather round Polydamas the excellent\rson of Panthous, but Hector kept on among the foremost, looking everywhere\rto find Deiphobus and prince Helenus, Adamas son of Asius, and Asius\rson of Hyrtacus; living, indeed, and scatheless he could no longer\rfind them, for the two last were lying by the sterns of the Achaean\rships, slain by the Argives, while the others had been also stricken\rand wounded by them; but upon the left wing of the dread battle he\rfound Alexandrus, husband of lovely Helen, cheering his men and urging\rthem on to fight. He went up to him and upbraided him. "Paris," said\rhe, "evil-hearted Paris, fair to see but woman-mad and false of tongue,\rwhere are Deiphobus and King Helenus? Where are Adamas son of Asius,\rand Asius son of Hyrtacus? Where too is Othryoneus? Ilius is undone\rand will now surely fall!" \r\rAlexandrus answered, "Hector, why find fault when there is no one\rto find fault with? I should hold aloof from battle on any day rather\rthan this, for my mother bore me with nothing of the coward about\rme. From the moment when you set our men fighting about the ships\rwe have been staying here and doing battle with the Danaans. Our comrades\rabout whom you ask me are dead; Deiphobus and King Helenus alone have\rleft the field, wounded both of them in the hand, but the son of Saturn\rsaved them alive. Now, therefore, lead on where you would have us\rgo, and we will follow with right goodwill; you shall not find us\rfail you in so far as our strength holds out, but no man can do more\rthan in him lies, no matter how willing he may be." \r\rWith these words he satisfied his brother, and the two went towards\rthe part of the battle where the fight was thickest, about Cebriones,\rbrave Polydamas, Phalces, Orthaeus, godlike Polyphetes, Palmys, Ascanius,\rand Morys son of Hippotion, who had come from fertile Ascania on the\rpreceding day to relieve other troops. Then Jove urged them on to\rfight. They flew forth like the blasts of some fierce wind that strike\rearth in the van of a thunderstorm- they buffet the salt sea into\ran uproar; many and mighty are the great waves that come crashing\rin one after the other upon the shore with their arching heads all\rcrested with foam- even so did rank behind rank of Trojans arrayed\rin gleaming armour follow their leaders onward. The way was led by\rHector son of Priam, peer of murderous Mars, with his round shield\rbefore him- his shield of ox-hides covered with plates of bronze-\rand his gleaming helmet upon his temples. He kept stepping forward\runder cover of his shield in every direction, making trial of the\rranks to see if they would give way be him, but he could not daunt\rthe courage of the Achaeans. Ajax was the first to stride out and\rchallenge him. "Sir," he cried, "draw near; why do you think thus\rvainly to dismay the Argives? We Achaeans are excellent soldiers,\rbut the scourge of Jove has fallen heavily upon us. Your heart, forsooth,\ris set on destroying our ships, but we too have bands that can keep\ryou at bay, and your own fair town shall be sooner taken and sacked\rby ourselves. The time is near when you shall pray Jove and all the\rgods in your flight, that your steeds may be swifter than hawks as\rthey raise the dust on the plain and bear you back to your city."\r\rAs he was thus speaking a bird flew by upon his right hand, and the\rhost of the Achaeans shouted, for they took heart at the omen. But\rHector answered, "Ajax, braggart and false of tongue, would that I\rwere as sure of being son for evermore to aegis-bearing Jove, with\rQueen Juno for my mother, and of being held in like honour with Minerva\rand Apollo, as I am that this day is big with the destruction of the\rAchaeans; and you shall fall among them if you dare abide my spear;\rit shall rend your fair body and bid you glut our hounds and birds\rof prey with your fat and your flesh, as you fall by the ships of\rthe Achaeans." \r\rWith these words he led the way and the others followed after with\ra cry that rent the air, while the host shouted behind them. The Argives\ron their part raised a shout likewise, nor did they forget their prowess,\rbut stood firm against the onslaught of the Trojan chieftains, and\rthe cry from both the hosts rose up to heaven and to the brightness\rof Jove\'s presence. \r\r----------------------------------------------------------------------\r\rBOOK XIV\r\rNestor was sitting over his wine, but the cry of battle did not escape\rhim, and he said to the son of Aesculapius, "What, noble Machaon,\ris the meaning of all this? The shouts of men fighting by our ships\rgrow stronger and stronger; stay here, therefore, and sit over your\rwine, while fair Hecamede heats you a bath and washes the clotted\rblood from off you. I will go at once to the look-out station and\rsee what it is all about." \r\rAs he spoke he took up the shield of his son Thrasymedes that was\rlying in his tent, all gleaming with bronze, for Thrasymedes had taken\rhis father\'s shield; he grasped his redoubtable bronze-shod spear,\rand as soon as he was outside saw the disastrous rout of the Achaeans\rwho, now that their wall was overthrown, were flying pell-mell before\rthe Trojans. As when there is a heavy swell upon the sea, but the\rwaves are dumb- they keep their eyes on the watch for the quarter\rwhence the fierce winds may spring upon them, but they stay where\rthey are and set neither this way nor that, till some particular wind\rsweeps down from heaven to determine them- even so did the old man\rponder whether to make for the crowd of Danaans, or go in search of\rAgamemnon. In the end he deemed it best to go to the son of Atreus;\rbut meanwhile the hosts were fighting and killing one another, and\rthe hard bronze rattled on their bodies, as they thrust at one another\rwith their swords and spears. \r\rThe wounded kings, the son of Tydeus, Ulysses, and Agamemnon son of\rAtreus, fell in Nestor as they were coming up from their ships- for\rtheirs were drawn up some way from where the fighting was going on,\rbeing on the shore itself inasmuch as they had been beached first,\rwhile the wall had been built behind the hindermost. The stretch of\rthe shore, wide though it was, did not afford room for all the ships,\rand the host was cramped for space, therefore they had placed the\rships in rows one behind the other, and had filled the whole opening\rof the bay between the two points that formed it. There is nothing wrong in flying\rruin even by night. See how Promachus\rnow sleeps, vanquished by my spear; payment for my brother\'s blood\rhas not long delayed; a man, therefore, may well be thankful if he\rleaves a kinsman in his house behind him to avenge his fall."\r\rHis taunts infuriated the Argives, and Peneleos was more enraged than\rany of them. Ilioneus was his only son, and Peneleos now\rwounded him in the eye under his eyebrows, tearing the eye-ball from\rits socket: the spear went right through the eye into the nape of\rthe neck, and he fell, stretching out both hands before him. Peneleos\rthen drew his sword and smote him on the neck, so that both head and\rhelmet came tumbling down to the ground with the spear still sticking\rin the eye; he then held up the head, as though it had been a poppy-head,\rand showed it to the Trojans, vaunting over them as he did so. "Trojans,"\rhe cried, "bid the father and mother of noble Ilioneus make moan for\rhim in their house, for the wife also of Promachus son of Alegenor\rwill never be gladdened by the coming of her dear husband- when we\rArgives return with our ships from Troy." \r\rAs he spoke fear fell upon them, and every man looked round about\rto see whither he might fly for safety. \r\rTell me now, O Muses that dwell on Olympus, who was the first of the\rArgives to bear away blood-stained spoils after Neptune lord of the\rearthquake had turned the fortune of war. Ajax son of Telamon was\rfirst to wound Hyrtius son of Gyrtius, captain of the staunch Mysians.\rAntilochus killed Phalces and Mermerus, while Meriones slew Morys\rand Hippotion, Teucer also killed Prothoon and Periphetes. Ajax son\rof Oileus killed more than any other, for there was no man so fleet\ras he to pursue flying foes when Jove had spread panic among them.\r\r----------------------------------------------------------------------\r\rBOOK XV\r\rBut when their flight had taken them past the trench and the set\rstakes, and many had fallen by the hands of the Danaans, the Trojans\rmade a halt on reaching their chariots, routed and pale with fear.\rJove now woke on the crests of Ida, where he was lying with golden-throned\rJuno by his side, and starting to his feet he saw the Trojans and\rAchaeans, the one thrown into confusion, and the others driving them\rpell-mell before them with King Neptune in their midst. He saw Hector\rlying on the ground with his comrades gathered round him, gasping\rfor breath, wandering in mind and vomiting blood, for it was not the\rfeeblest of the Achaeans who struck him. \r\rThe sire of gods and men had pity on him, and looked fiercely on Juno.\r"I see, Juno," said he, "you mischief- making trickster, that your\rcunning has stayed Hector from fighting and has caused the rout of\rhis host. I am in half a mind to thrash you, in which case you will\rbe the first to reap the fruits of your scurvy knavery. Do you not\rremember how once upon a time I had you hanged? I fastened two anvils\ron to your feet, and bound your hands in a chain of gold which none\rmight break, and you hung in mid-air among the clouds. All the gods\rin Olympus were in a fury, but they could not reach you to set you\rfree; when I caught any one of them I gripped him and hurled him from\rthe heavenly threshold till he came fainting down to earth; yet even\rthis did not relieve my mind from the incessant anxiety which I felt\rabout noble Hercules whom you and Boreas had spitefully conveyed beyond\rthe seas to Cos, after suborning the tempests; but I rescued him,\rand notwithstanding all his mighty labours I brought him back again\rto Argos. I would remind you of this that you may learn to leave off\rbeing so deceitful, and discover how much you are likely to gain by\rthe embraces out of which you have come here to trick me."\r\rJuno trembled as he spoke, and said, "May heaven above and earth below\rbe my witnesses, with the waters of the river Styx- and this is the\rmost solemn oath that a blessed god can take- nay, I swear also by\ryour own almighty head and by our bridal bed- things over which I\rcould never possibly perjure myself- that Neptune is not punishing\rHector and the Trojans and helping the Achaeans through any doing\rof mine; it is all of his own mere motion because he was sorry to\rsee the Achaeans hard pressed at their ships: if I were advising him,\rI should tell him to do as you bid him." \r\rThe sire of gods and men smiled and answered, "If you, Juno, were\ralways to support me when we sit in council of the gods, Neptune,\rlike it or no, would soon come round to your and my way of thinking.\rIf, then, you are speaking the truth and mean what you say, go among\rthe rank and file of the gods, and tell Iris and Apollo lord of the\rbow, that I want them- Iris, that she may go to the Achaean host and\rtell Neptune to leave off fighting and go home, and Apollo, that he\rmay send Hector again into battle and give him fresh strength; he\rwill thus forget his present sufferings, and drive the Achaeans back\rin confusion till they fall among the ships of Achilles son of Peleus.\rAchilles will then send his comrade Patroclus into battle, and Hector\rwill kill him in front of Ilius after he has slain many warriors,\rand among them my own noble son Sarpedon. Achilles will kill Hector\rto avenge Patroclus, and from that time I will bring it about that\rthe Achaeans shall persistently drive the Trojans back till they fulfil\rthe counsels of Minerva and take Ilius. But I will not stay my anger,\rnor permit any god to help the Danaans till I have accomplished the\rdesire of the son of Peleus, according to the promise I made by bowing\rmy head on the day when Thetis touched my knees and besought me to\rgive him honour." \r\rJuno heeded his words and went from the heights of Ida to great Olympus.\rSwift as the thought of one whose fancy carries him over vast continents,\rand he says to himself, "Now I will be here, or there," and he would\rhave all manner of things- even so swiftly did Juno wing her way till\rshe came to high Olympus and went in among the gods who were gathered\rin the house of Jove. When they saw her they all of them came up to\rher, and held out their cups to her by way of greeting. She let the\rothers be, but took the cup offered her by lovely Themis, who was\rfirst to come running up to her. "Juno," said she, "why are you here?\rAnd you seem troubled- has your husband the son of Saturn been frightening\ryou?" \r\rAnd Juno answered, "Themis, do not ask me about it. You know what\ra proud and cruel disposition my husband has. Lead the gods to table,\rwhere you and all the immortals can hear the wicked designs which\rhe has avowed. Many a one, mortal and immortal, will be angered by\rthem, however peaceably he may be feasting now." \r\rOn this Juno sat down, and the gods were troubled throughout the house\rof Jove. Laughter sat on her lips but her brow was furrowed with care,\rand she spoke up in a rage. "Fools that we are," she cried, "to be\rthus madly angry with Jove; we keep on wanting to go up to him and\rstay him by force or by persuasion, but he sits aloof and cares for\rnobody, for he knows that he is much stronger than any other of the\rimmortals. Make the best, therefore, of whatever ills he may choose\rto send each one of you; Mars, I take it, has had a taste of them\ralready, for his son Ascalaphus has fallen in battle- the man whom\rof all others he loved most dearly and whose father he owns himself\rto be." \r\rWhen he heard this Mars smote his two sturdy thighs with the flat\rof his hands, and said in anger, "Do not blame me, you gods that dwell\rin heaven, if I go to the ships of the Achaeans and avenge the death\rof my son, even though it end in my being struck by Jove\'s lightning\rand lying in blood and dust among the corpses." \r\rAs he spoke he gave orders to yoke his horses Panic and Rout, while\rhe put on his armour. Therefore lay aside your anger for\rthe death of your son; better men than he have either been killed\ralready or will fall hereafter, and one cannot protect every one\'s\rwhole family." \r\rWith these words she took Mars back to his seat. Bid him leave off fighting,\rand either join the company of the gods, or go down into the sea.\rIf he takes no heed and disobeys me, let him consider well whether\rhe is strong enough to hold his own against me if I attack him. I\ram older and much stronger than he is; yet he is not afraid to set\rhimself up as on a level with myself, of whom all the other gods stand\rin awe." \r\rIris, fleet as the wind, obeyed him, and as the cold hail or snowflakes\rthat fly from out the clouds before the blast of Boreas, even so did\rshe wing her way till she came close up to the great shaker of the\rearth. He would have you keep\rout of his reach, for he is older and much stronger than you are,\rand yet you are not afraid to set yourself up as on a level with himself,\rof whom all the other gods stand in awe." \r\rNeptune was very angry and said, "Great heavens! strong as Jove may\rbe, he has said more than he can do if he has threatened violence\ragainst me, who am of like honour with himself. We were three brothers\rwhom Rhea bore to Saturn- Jove, myself, and Hades who rules the world\rbelow. Heaven and earth were divided into three parts, and each of\rus was to have an equal share. When we cast lots, it fell to me to\rhave my dwelling in the sea for evermore; Hades took the darkness\rof the realms under the earth, while air and sky and clouds were the\rportion that fell to Jove; but earth and great Olympus are the common\rproperty of all. Sensible people are open to argument, and you know that\rthe Erinyes always range themselves on the side of the older person."\r\rNeptune answered, "Goddess Iris, your words have been spoken in season.\rIt is well when a messenger shows so much discretion. From that point I will think\rit well over, how the Achaeans may have a respite from their troubles."\r\rApollo obeyed his father\'s saying, and left the crests of Ida, flying\rlike a falcon, bane of doves and swiftest of all birds. Has any mishap befallen you?" \r\rHector in a weak voice answered, "And which, kind sir, of the gods\rare you, who now ask me thus? Now, therefore, order your\rhorsemen to drive their chariots to the ships in great multitudes.\rI will go before your horses to smooth the way for them, and will\rturn the Achaeans in flight." \r\rAs he spoke he infused great strength into the shepherd of his people.\rAnd as a horse, stabled and full-fed, breaks loose and gallops gloriously\rover the plain to the place where he is wont to take his bath in the\rriver- he tosses his head, and his mane streams over his shoulders\ras in all the pride of his strength he flies full speed to the pastures\rwhere the mares are feeding- even so Hector, when he heard what the\rgod said, urged his horsemen on, and sped forward as fast as his limbs\rcould take him. As country peasants set their hounds on to a homed\rstag or wild goat- he has taken shelter under rock or thicket, and\rthey cannot find him, but, lo, a bearded lion whom their shouts have\rroused stands in their path, and they are in no further humour for\rthe chase- even so the Achaeans were still charging on in a body,\rusing their swords and spears pointed at both ends, but when they\rsaw Hector going about among his men they were afraid, and their hearts\rfell down into their feet. \r\rThen spoke Thoas son of Andraemon, leader of the Aetolians, a man\rwho could throw a good throw, and who was staunch also in close fight,\rwhile few could surpass him in debate when opinions were divided.\rHe then with all sincerity and goodwill addressed them thus: "What,\rin heaven\'s name, do I now see? He has killed\rmany of us Danaans already, and I take it will yet do so, for the\rhand of Jove must be with him or he would never dare show himself\rso masterful in the forefront of the battle. Those who were\rabout Ajax and King Idomeneus, the followers moreover of Teucer, Meriones,\rand Meges peer of Mars called all their best men about them and sustained\rthe fight against Hector and the Trojans, but the main body fell back\rupon the ships of the Achaeans. \r\rThe Trojans pressed forward in a dense body, with Hector striding\ron at their head. "Eurypylus,"\rsaid he in his dismay, "I know you want me badly, but I cannot stay\rwith you any longer, for there is hard fighting going on; a servant\rshall take care of you now, for I must make all speed to Achilles,\rand induce him to fight if I can; who knows but with heaven\'s help\rI may persuade him. A man does well to listen to the advice of a friend."\r\rWhen he had thus spoken he went his way. The Achaeans stood firm and\rresisted the attack of the Trojans, yet though these were fewer in\rnumber, they could not drive them back from the ships, neither could\rthe Trojans break the Achaean ranks and make their way in among the\rtents and ships. Therefore swarm round the ships and\rfight. Ajax on the\rother side exhorted his comrades saying, "Shame on you Argives, we\rare now utterly undone, unless we can save ourselves by driving the\renemy from our ships. Do you think, if Hector takes them, that you\rwill be able to get home by land? Can you not hear him cheering on\rhis whole host to fire our fleet, and bidding them remember that they\rare not at a dance but in battle? Our only course is to fight them\rwith might and main; we had better chance it, life or death, once\rfor all, than fight long and without issue hemmed in at our ships\rby worse men than ourselves." \r\rWith these words he put life and soul into them all. Hector then killed\rSchedius son of Perimedes, leader of the Phoceans, and Ajax killed\rLaodamas captain of foot soldiers and son to Antenor. Polydamas killed\rOtus of Cyllene a comrade of the son of Phyleus and chief of the proud\rEpeans. At that moment the valiant soldier Dolops son of Lampus\rsprang upon Lampus was son of Laomedon and for his valour, while his\rson Dolops was versed in all the ways of war. He then struck the middle\rof the son of Phyleus\' shield with his spear, setting on him at close\rquarters, but his good corslet made with plates of metal saved him;\rPhyleus had brought it from Ephyra and the river Selleis, where his\rhost, King Euphetes, had given it him to wear in battle and protect\rhim. It now served to save the life of his son. Then Meges struck\rthe topmost crest of Dolops\'s bronze helmet with his spear and tore\raway its plume of horse-hair, so that all newly dyed with scarlet\ras it was it tumbled down into the dust. While he was still fighting\rand confident of victory, Menelaus came up to help Meges, and got\rby the side of Dolops unperceived; he then speared him in the shoulder,\rfrom behind, and the point, driven so furiously, went through into\rhis chest, whereon he fell headlong. The two then made towards him\rto strip him of his armour, but Hector called on all his brothers\rfor help, and he especially upbraided brave Melanippus son of Hiketaon,\rwho erewhile used to pasture his herds of cattle in Percote before\rthe war broke out; but when the ships of the Danaans came, he went\rback to Ilius, where he was eminent among the Trojans, and lived near\rPriam who treated him as one of his own sons. Hector now rebuked him\rand said, "Why, Melanippus, are we thus remiss? do you take no note\rof the death of your kinsman, and do you not see how they are trying\rto take Dolops\'s armour? Follow me; there must be no fighting the\rArgives from a distance now, but we must do so in close combat till\reither we kill them or they take the high wall of Ilius and slay her\rpeople." \r\rHe led on as he spoke, and the hero Melanippus followed after. Meanwhile\rAjax son of Telamon was cheering on the Argives. "My friends," he\rcried, "be men, and fear dishonour; quit yourselves in battle so as\rto win respect from one another. Men who respect each other\'s good\ropinion are less likely to be killed than those who do not, but in\rflight there is neither gain nor glory." \r\rThus did he exhort men who were already bent upon driving back the\rTrojans. They laid his words to heart and hedged the ships as with\ra wall of bronze, while Jove urged on the Trojans. Menelaus of the\rloud battle-cry urged Antilochus on. "Antilochus," said he, "you are\ryoung and there is none of the Achaeans more fleet of foot or more\rvaliant than you are. See if you cannot spring upon some Trojan and\rkill him." \r\rHe hurried away when he had thus spurred Antilochus, who at once darted\rout from the front ranks and aimed a spear, after looking carefully\rround him. The Trojans fell back as he threw, and the dart did not\rspeed from his hand without effect, for it struck Melanippus the proud\rson of Hiketaon in the breast by the nipple as he was coming forward,\rand his armour rang rattling round him as he fell heavily to the ground.\rAntilochus sprang upon him as a dog springs on a fawn which a hunter\rhas hit as it was breaking away from its covert, and killed it. Even\rso, O Melanippus, did stalwart Antilochus spring upon you to strip\ryou of your armour; but noble Hector marked him, and came running\rup to him through the thick of the battle. Antilochus, brave soldier\rthough he was, would not stay to face him, but fled like some savage\rcreature which knows it has done wrong, and flies, when it has killed\ra dog or a man who is herding his cattle, before a body of men can\rbe gathered to attack it. Even so did the son of Nestor fly, and the\rTrojans and Hector with a cry that rent the air showered their weapons\rafter him; nor did he turn round and stay his flight till he had reached\rhis comrades. \r\rThe Trojans, fierce as lions, were still rushing on towards the ships\rin fulfilment of the behests of Jove who kept spurring them on to\rnew deeds of daring, while he deadened the courage of the Argives\rand defeated them by encouraging the Trojans. For he meant giving\rglory to Hector son of Priam, and letting him throw fire upon the\rships, till he had fulfilled the unrighteous prayer that Thetis had\rmade him; Jove, therefore, bided his time till he should see the glare\rof a blazing ship. From that hour he was about so to order that the\rTrojans should be driven back from the ships and to vouchsafe glory\rto the Achaeans. With this purpose he inspired Hector son of Priam,\rwho was cager enough already, to assail the ships. His fury was as\rthat of Mars, or as when a fire is raging in the glades of some dense\rforest upon the mountains; he foamed at the mouth, his eyes glared\runder his terrible eye-brows, and his helmet quivered on his temples\rby reason of the fury with which he fought. Jove from heaven was with\rhim, and though he was but one against many, vouchsafed him victory\rand glory; for he was doomed to an early death, and already Pallas\rMinerva was hurrying on the hour of his destruction at the hands of\rthe son of Peleus. Now, however, he kept trying to break the ranks\rof the enemy wherever he could see them thickest, and in the goodliest\rarmour; but do what he might he could not break through them, for\rthey stood as a tower foursquare, or as some high cliff rising from\rthe grey sea that braves the anger of the gale, and of the waves that\rthunder up against it. He fell upon them like flames of fire from\revery quarter. As when a wave, raised mountain high by wind and storm,\rbreaks over a ship and covers it deep in foam, the fierce winds roar\ragainst the mast, the hearts of the sailors fail them for fear, and\rthey are saved but by a very little from destruction- even so were\rthe hearts of the Achaeans fainting within them. Or as a savage lion\rattacking a herd of cows while they are feeding by thousands in the\rlow-lying meadows by some wide-watered shore- the herdsman is at his\rwit\'s end how to protect his herd and keeps going about now in the\rvan and now in the rear of his cattle, while the lion springs into\rthe thick of them and fastens on a cow so that they all tremble for\rfear- even so were the Achaeans utterly panic-stricken by Hector and\rfather Jove. Nevertheless Hector only killed Periphetes of Mycenae;\rhe was son of Copreus who was wont to take the orders of King Eurystheus\rto mighty Hercules, but the son was a far better man than the father\rin every way; he was fleet of foot, a valiant warrior, and in understanding\rranked among the foremost men of Mycenae. He it was who then afforded\rHector a triumph, for as he was turning back he stumbled against the\rrim of his shield which reached his feet, and served to keep the javelins\roff him. He tripped against this and fell face upward, his helmet\rringing loudly about his head as he did so. Hector saw him fall and\rran up to him; he then thrust a spear into his chest, and killed him\rclose to his own comrades. These, for all their sorrow, could not\rhelp him for they were themselves terribly afraid of Hector.\r\rThey had now reached the ships and the prows of those that had been\rdrawn up first were on every side of them, but the Trojans came pouring\rafter them. The Argives were driven back from the first row of ships,\rbut they made a stand by their tents without being broken up and scattered;\rshame and fear restrained them. They kept shouting incessantly to\rone another, and Nestor of Gerene, tower of strength to the Achaeans,\rwas loudest in imploring every man by his parents, and beseeching\rhim to stand firm. \r\r"Be men, my friends," he cried, "and respect one another\'s good opinion.\rThink, all of you, on your children, your wives, your property, and\ryour parents whether these be alive or dead. On their behalf though\rthey are not here, I implore you to stand firm, and not to turn in\rflight." \r\rWith these words he put heart and soul into them all. Minerva lifted\rthe thick veil of darkness from their eyes, and much light fell upon\rthem, alike on the side of the ships and on that where the fight was\rraging. They could see Hector and all his men, both those in the rear\rwho were taking no part in the battle, and those who were fighting\rby the ships. \r\rAjax could not bring himself to retreat along with the rest, but strode\rfrom deck to deck with a great sea-pike in his hands twelve cubits\rlong and jointed with rings. As a man skilled in feats of horsemanship\rcouples four horses together and comes tearing full speed along the\rpublic way from the country into some large town- many both men and\rwomen marvel as they see him for he keeps all the time changing his\rhorse, springing from one to another without ever missing his feet\rwhile the horses are at a gallop- even so did Ajax go striding from\rone ship\'s deck to another, and his voice went up into the heavens.\rHe kept on shouting his orders to the Danaans and exhorting them to\rdefend their ships and tents; neither did Hector remain within the\rmain body of the Trojan warriors, but as a dun eagle swoops down upon\ra flock of wild-fowl feeding near a river-geese, it may be, or cranes,\ror long-necked swans- even so did Hector make straight for a dark-prowed\rship, rushing right towards it; for Jove with his mighty hand impelled\rhim forward, and roused his people to follow him. \r\rAnd now the battle again raged furiously at the ships. You would have\rthought the men were coming on fresh and unwearied, so fiercely did\rthey fight; and this was the mind in which they were- the Achaeans\rdid not believe they should escape destruction but thought themselves\rdoomed, while there was not a Trojan but his heart beat high with\rthe hope of firing the ships and putting the Achaean heroes to the\rsword. \r\rThus were the two sides minded. Then Hector seized the stern of the\rgood ship that had brought Protesilaus to Troy, but never bore him\rback to his native land. Round this ship there raged a close hand-to-hand\rfight between Danaans and Trojans. They did not fight at a distance\rwith bows and javelins, but with one mind hacked at one another in\rclose combat with their mighty swords and spears pointed at both ends;\rthey fought moreover with keen battle-axes and with hatchets. Many\ra good stout blade hilted and scabbarded with iron, fell from hand\ror shoulder as they fought, and the earth ran red with blood. Hector,\rwhen he had seized the ship, would not loose his hold but held on\rto its curved stern and shouted to the Trojans, "Bring fire, and raise\rthe battle-cry all of you with a single voice. Therefore\rhe left the raised deck at the stern, and stepped back on to the seven-foot\rbench of the oarsmen. Here he stood on the look-out, and with his\rspear held back Trojan whom he saw bringing fire to the ships. All\rthe time he kept on shouting at the top of his voice and exhorting\rthe Danaans. "My friends," he cried, "Danaan heroes, servants of Mars,\rbe men my friends, and fight with might and with main. Can we hope\rto find helpers hereafter, or a wall to shield us more surely than\rthe one we have? There is no strong city within reach, whence we may\rdraw fresh forces to turn the scales in our favour. Then Patroclus\rdrew near to Achilles with tears welling from his eyes, as from some\rspring whose crystal stream falls over the ledges of a high precipice.\rWhen Achilles saw him thus weeping he was sorry for him and said,\r"Why, Patroclus, do you stand there weeping like some silly child\rthat comes running to her mother, and begs to be taken up and carried-\rshe catches hold of her mother\'s dress to stay her though she is in\ra hurry, and looks tearfully up until her mother carries her- even\rsuch tears, Patroclus, are you now shedding. Have you anything to\rsay to the Myrmidons or to myself? or have you had news from Phthia\rwhich you alone know? They tell me Menoetius son of Actor is still\ralive, as also Peleus son of Aeacus, among the Myrmidons- men whose\rloss we two should bitterly deplore; or are you grieving about the\rArgives and the way in which they are being killed at the ships, throu\rtheir own high-handed doings? Do not hide anything from me but tell\rme that both of us may know about it." \r\rThen, O knight Patroclus, with a deep sigh you answered, "Achilles,\rson of Peleus, foremost champion of the Achaeans, do not be angry,\rbut I weep for the disaster that has now befallen the Argives. All\rthose who have been their champions so far are lying at the ships,\rwounded by sword or spear. Brave Diomed son of Tydeus has been hit\rwith a spear, while famed Ulysses and Agamemnon have received sword-wounds;\rEurypylus again has been struck with an arrow in the thigh; skilled\rapothecaries are attending to these heroes, and healing them of their\rwounds; are you still, O Achilles, so inexorable? May it never be\rmy lot to nurse such a passion as you have done, to the baning of\ryour own good name. Who in future story will speak well of you unless\ryou now save the Argives from ruin? You know no pity; knight Peleus\rwas not your father nor Thetis your mother, but the grey sea bore\ryou and the sheer cliffs begot you, so cruel and remorseless are you.\rIf however you are kept back through knowledge of some oracle, or\rif your mother Thetis has told you something from the mouth of Jove,\rat least send me and the Myrmidons with me, if I may bring deliverance\rto the Danaans. Let me moreover wear your armour; the Trojans may\rthus mistake me for you and quit the field, so that the hard-pressed\rsons of the Achaeans may have breathing time- which while they are\rfighting may hardly be. We who are fresh might soon drive tired men\rback from our ships and tents to their own city." \r\rHe knew not what he was asking, nor that he was suing for his own\rdestruction. Achilles was deeply moved and answered, "What, noble\rPatroclus, are you saying? I know no prophesyings which I am heeding,\rnor has my mother told me anything from the mouth of Jove, but I am\rcut to the very heart that one of my own rank should dare to rob me\rbecause he is more powerful than I am. This, after all that I have\rgone through, is more than I can endure. The girl whom the sons of\rthe Achaeans chose for me, whom I won as the fruit of my spear on\rhaving sacked a city- her has King Agamemnon taken from me as though\rI were some common vagrant. Still, let bygones be bygones: no man\rmay keep his anger for ever; I said I would not relent till battle\rand the cry of war had reached my own ships; nevertheless, now gird\rmy armour about your shoulders, and lead the Myrmidons to battle,\rfor the dark cloud of Trojans has burst furiously over our fleet;\rthe Argives are driven back on to the beach, cooped within a narrow\rspace, and the whole people of Troy has taken heart to sally out against\rthem, because they see not the visor of my helmet gleaming near them.\rHad they seen this, there would not have been a creek nor grip that\rhad not been filled with their dead as they fled back again. And so\rit would have been, if only King Agamemnon had dealt fairly by me.\rAs it is the Trojans have beset our host. Diomed son of Tydeus no\rlonger wields his spear to defend the Danaans, neither have I heard\rthe voice of the son of Atreus coming from his hated head, whereas\rthat of murderous Hector rings in my cars as he gives orders to the\rTrojans, who triumph over the Achaeans and fill the whole plain with\rtheir cry of battle. When you have driven\rthe Trojans from the ships, come back again. But Ajax could no longer hold his ground for\rthe shower of darts that rained upon him; the will of Jove and the\rjavelins of the Trojans were too much for him; the helmet that gleamed\rabout his temples rang with the continuous clatter of the missiles\rthat kept pouring on to it and on to the cheek-pieces that protected\rhis face. Hector came close\rup and let drive with his great sword at the ashen spear of Ajax.\rHe cut it clean in two just behind where the point was fastened on\rto the shaft of the spear. Ajax knew the hand of heaven in this,\rand was dismayed at seeing that Jove had now left him utterly defenceless\rand was willing victory for the Trojans. Therefore he drew back, and\rthe Trojans flung fire upon the ship which was at once wrapped in\rflame. \r\rThe fire was now flaring about the ship\'s stern, whereon Achilles\rsmote his two thighs and said to Patroclus, "Up, noble knight, for\rI see the glare of hostile fire at our fleet; up, lest they destroy\rour ships, and there be no way by which we may retreat. First he greaved his legs\rwith greaves of good make, and fitted with ancle-clasps of silver;\rafter this he donned the cuirass of the son of Aeacus, richly inlaid\rand studded. Even as fierce ravening wolves\rthat are feasting upon a homed stag which they have killed upon the\rmountains, and their jaws are red with blood- they go in a pack to\rlap water from the clear spring with their long thin tongues; and\rthey reek of blood and slaughter; they know not what fear is, for\rit is hunger drives them- even so did the leaders and counsellors\rof the Myrmidons gather round the good squire of the fleet descendant\rof Aeacus, and among them stood Achilles himself cheering on both\rmen and horses. \r\rFifty ships had noble Achilles brought to Troy, and in each there\rwas a crew of fifty oarsmen. Patroclus called\rout to his men at the top of his voice, "Myrmidons, followers of Achilles\rson of Peleus, be men my friends, fight with might and with main,\rthat we may win glory for the son of Peleus, who is far the foremost\rman at the ships of the Argives- he, and his close fighting followers.\rThe son of Atreus King Agamemnon will thus learn his folly in showing\rno respect to the bravest of the Achaeans." \r\rWith these words he put heart and soul into them all, and they fell\rin a body upon the Trojans. The ships rang again with the cry which\rthe Achaeans raised, and when the Trojans saw the brave son of Menoetius\rand his squire all gleaming in their armour, they were daunted and\rtheir battalions were thrown into confusion, for they thought the\rfleet son of Peleus must now have put aside his anger, and have been\rreconciled to Agamemnon; every one, therefore, looked round about\rto see whither he might fly for safety. \r\rPatroclus first aimed a spear into the middle of the press where men\rwere packed most closely, by the stern of the ship of Protesilaus.\rHe hit Pyraechmes who had led his Paeonian horsemen from the Amydon\rand the broad waters of the river Axius; the spear struck him on the\rright shoulder, and with a groan he fell backwards in the dust; on\rthis his men were thrown into confusion, for by killing their leader,\rwho was the finest soldier among them, Patroclus struck panic into\rthem all. He thus drove them from the ship and quenched the fire that\rwas then blazing- leaving the half-burnt ship to lie where it was.\rThe Trojans were now driven back with a shout that rent the skies,\rwhile the Danaans poured after them from their ships, shouting also\rwithout ceasing. As when Jove, gatherer of the thunder-cloud, spreads\ra dense canopy on the top of some lofty mountain, and all the peaks,\rthe jutting headlands, and forest glades show out in the great light\rthat flashes from the bursting heavens, even so when the Danaans had\rnow driven back the fire from their ships, they took breath for a\rlittle while; but the fury of the fight was not yet over, for the\rTrojans were not driven back in utter rout, but still gave battle,\rand were ousted from their ground only by sheer fighting.\r\rThe fight then became more scattered, and the chieftains killed one\ranother when and how they could. The valiant son of Menoetius first\rdrove his spear into the thigh of Areilycus just as he was turning\rround; the point went clean through, and broke the bone so that he\rfell forward. Meanwhile Menelaus struck Thoas in the chest, where\rit was exposed near the rim of his shield, and he fell dead. The son\rof Phyleus saw Amphiclus about to attack him, and ere he could do\rso took aim at the upper part of his thigh, where the muscles are\rthicker than in any other part; the spear tore through all the sinews\rof the leg, and his eyes were closed in darkness. Of the sons of Nestor\rone, Antilochus, speared Atymnius, driving the point of the spear\rthrough his throat, and down he fell. Maris then sprang on Antilochus\rin hand-to-hand fight to avenge his brother, and bestrode the body\rspear in hand; but valiant Thrasymedes was too quick for him, and\rin a moment had struck him in the shoulder ere he could deal his blow;\rhis aim was true, and the spear severed all the muscles at the root\rof his arm, and tore them right down to the bone, so he fell heavily\rto the ground and his eyes were closed in darkness. Thus did these\rtwo noble comrades of Sarpedon go down to Erebus slain by the two\rsons of Nestor; they were the warrior sons of Amisodorus, who had\rreared the invincible Chimaera, to the bane of many. Ajax son of Oileus\rsprang on Cleobulus and took him alive as he was entangled in the\rcrush; but he killed him then and there by a sword-blow on the neck.\rThe sword reeked with his blood, while dark death and the strong hand\rof fate gripped him and closed his eyes. \r\rPeneleos and Lycon now met in close fight, for they had missed each\rother with their spears. They had both thrown without effect, so now\rthey drew their swords. Lycon struck the plumed crest of Peneleos\'\rhelmet but his sword broke at the hilt, while Peneleos smote Lycon\ron the neck under the ear. The blade sank so deep that the head was\rheld on by nothing but the skin, and there was no more life left in\rhim. Meriones gave chase to Acamas on foot and caught him up just\ras he was about to mount his chariot; he drove a spear through his\rright shoulder so that he fell headlong from the car, and his eyes\rwere closed in darkness. Idomeneus speared Erymas in the mouth; the\rbronze point of the spear went clean through it beneath the brain,\rcrashing in among the white bones and smashing them up. His teeth\rwere all of them knocked out and the blood came gushing in a stream\rfrom both his eyes; it also came gurgling up from his mouth and nostrils,\rand the darkness of death enfolded him round about. \r\rThus did these chieftains of the Danaans each of them kill his man.\rAs ravening wolves seize on kids or lambs, fastening on them when\rthey are alone on the hillsides and have strayed from the main flock\rthrough the carelessness of the shepherd- and when the wolves see\rthis they pounce upon them at once because they cannot defend themselves-\reven so did the Danaans now fall on the Trojans, who fled with ill-omened\rcries in their panic and had no more fight left in them.\r\rMeanwhile great Ajax kept on trying to drive a spear into Hector,\rbut Hector was so skilful that he held his broad shoulders well under\rcover of his ox-hide shield, ever on the look-out for the whizzing\rof the arrows and the heavy thud of the spears. He well knew that\rthe fortunes of the day had changed, but still stood his ground and\rtried to protect his comrades. \r\rAs when a cloud goes up into heaven from Olympus, rising out of a\rclear sky when Jove is brewing a gale- even with such panic stricken\rrout did the Trojans now fly, and there was no order in their going.\rHector\'s fleet horses bore him and his armour out of the fight, and\rhe left the Trojan host penned in by the deep trench against their\rwill. Many a yoke of horses snapped the pole of their chariots in\rthe trench and left their master\'s car behind them. Patroclus gave\rchase, calling impetuously on the Danaans and full of fury against\rthe Trojans, who, being now no longer in a body, filled all the ways\rwith their cries of panic and rout; the air was darkened with the\rclouds of dust they raised, and the horses strained every nerve in\rtheir flight from the tents and ships towards the city. \r\rPatroclus kept on heading his horses wherever he saw most men flying\rin confusion, cheering on his men the while. Chariots were being smashed\rin all directions, and many a man came tumbling down from his own\rcar to fall beneath the wheels of that of Patroclus, whose immortal\rsteeds, given by the gods to Peleus, sprang over the trench at a bound\ras they sped onward. He was intent on trying to get near Hector, for\rhe had set his heart on spearing him, but Hector\'s horses were now\rhurrying him away. As the whole dark earth bows before some tempest\ron an autumn day when Jove rains his hardest to punish men for giving\rcrooked judgement in their courts, and arriving justice therefrom\rwithout heed to the decrees of heaven- all the rivers run full and\rthe torrents tear many a new channel as they roar headlong from the\rmountains to the dark sea, and it fares ill with the works of men-\reven such was the stress and strain of the Trojan horses in their\rflight. \r\rPatroclus now cut off the battalions that were nearest to him and\rdrove them back to the ships. They were doing their best to reach\rthe city, but he would not Yet them, and bore down on them between\rthe river and the ships and wall. Many a fallen comrade did he then\ravenge. First he hit Pronous with a spear on the chest where it was\rexposed near the rim of his shield, and he fell heavily to the ground.\rNext he sprang on Thestor son of Enops, who was sitting all huddled\rup in his chariot, for he had lost his head and the reins had been\rtorn out of his hands. Patroclus went up to him and drove a spear\rinto his right jaw; he thus hooked him by the teeth and the spear\rpulled him over the rim of his car, as one who sits at the end of\rsome jutting rock and draws a strong fish out of the sea with a hook\rand a line- even so with his spear did he pull Thestor all gaping\rfrom his chariot; he then threw him down on his face and he died while\rfalling. On this, as Erylaus was on to attack him, he struck him full\ron the head with a stone, and his brains were all battered inside\rhis helmet, whereon he fell headlong to the ground and the pangs of\rdeath took hold upon him. Then he laid low, one after the other, Erymas,\rAmphoterus, Epaltes, Tlepolemus, Echius son of Damastor, Pyris, lpheus,\rEuippus and Polymelus son of Argeas. \r\rNow when Sarpedon saw his comrades, men who wore ungirdled tunics,\rbeing overcome by Patroclus son of Menoetius, he rebuked the Lycians\rsaying. "Shame on you, where are you flying to? Show your mettle;\rI will myself meet this man in fight and learn who it is that is so\rmasterful; he has done us much hurt, and has stretched many a brave\rman upon the ground." \r\rHe sprang from his chariot as he spoke, and Patroclus, when he saw\rthis, leaped on to the ground also. The two then rushed at one another\rwith loud cries like eagle-beaked crook-taloned vultures that scream\rand tear at one another in some high mountain fastness. \r\rThe son of scheming Saturn looked down upon them in pity and said\rto Juno who was his wife and sister, "Alas, that it should be the\rlot of Sarpedon whom I love so dearly to perish by the hand of Patroclus.\rI am in two minds whether to catch him up out of the fight and set\rhim down safe and sound in the fertile land of Lycia, or to let him\rnow fall by the hand of the son of Menoetius." \r\rAnd Juno answered, "Most dread son of Saturn, what is this that you\rare saying? Would you snatch a mortal man, whose doom has long been\rfated, out of the jaws of death? Do as you will, but we shall not\rall of us be of your mind. I say further, and lay my saying to your\rheart, that if you send Sarpedon safely to his own home, some other\rof the gods will be also wanting to escort his son out of battle,\rfor there are many sons of gods fighting round the city of Troy, and\ryou will make every one jealous. If, however, you are fond of him\rand pity him, let him indeed fall by the hand of Patroclus, but as\rsoon as the life is gone out of him, send Death and sweet Sleep to\rbear him off the field and take him to the broad lands of Lycia, where\rhis brothers and his kinsmen will bury him with mound and pillar,\rin due honour to the dead." \r\rThe sire of gods and men assented, but he shed a rain of blood upon\rthe earth in honour of his son whom Patroclus was about to kill on\rthe rich plain of Troy far from his home. \r\rWhen they were now come close to one another Patroclus struck Thrasydemus,\rthe brave squire of Sarpedon, in the lower part of the belly, and\rkilled him. Sarpedon then aimed a spear at Patroclus and missed him,\rbut he struck the horse Pedasus in the right shoulder, and it screamed\raloud as it lay, groaning in the dust until the life went out of it.\rThe other two horses began to plunge; the pole of the chariot cracked\rand they got entangled in the reins through the fall of the horse\rthat was yoked along with them; but Automedon knew what to do; without\rthe loss of a moment he drew the keen blade that hung by his sturdy\rthigh and cut the third horse adrift; whereon the other two righted\rthemselves, and pulling hard at the reins again went together into\rbattle. \r\rSarpedon now took a second aim at Patroclus, and again missed him,\rthe point of the spear passed over his left shoulder without hitting\rhim. Patroclus then aimed in his turn, and the spear sped not from\rhis hand in vain, for he hit Sarpedon just where the midriff surrounds\rthe ever-beating heart. He fell like some oak or silver poplar or\rtall pine to which woodmen have laid their axes upon the mountains\rto make timber for ship-building- even so did he lie stretched at\rfull length in front of his chariot and horses, moaning and clutching\rat the blood-stained dust. As when a lion springs with a bound upon\ra herd of cattle and fastens on a great black bull which dies bellowing\rin its clutches- even so did the leader of the Lycian warriors struggle\rin death as he fell by the hand of Patroclus. He called on his trusty\rcomrade and said, "Glaucus, my brother, hero among heroes, put forth\rall your strength, fight with might and main, now if ever quit yourself\rlike a valiant soldier. First go about among the Lycian captains and\rbid them fight for Sarpedon; then yourself also do battle to save\rmy armour from being taken. My name will haunt you henceforth and\rfor ever if the Achaeans rob me of my armour now that I have fallen\rat their ships. Do your very utmost and call all my people together."\r\rDeath closed his eyes as he spoke. Patroclus planted his heel on his\rbreast and drew the spear from his body, whereon his senses came out\ralong with it, and he drew out both spear-point and Sarpedon\'s soul\rat the same time. Hard by the Myrmidons held his snorting steeds,\rwho were wild with panic at finding themselves deserted by their lords.\r\rGlaucus was overcome with grief when he heard what Sarpedon said,\rfor he could not help him. He had to support his arm with his other\rhand, being in great pain through the wound which Teucer\'s arrow had\rgiven him when Teucer was defending the wall as he, Glaucus, was assailing\rit. Therefore he prayed to far-darting Apollo saying, "Hear me O king\rfrom your seat, may be in the rich land of Lycia, or may be in Troy,\rfor in all places you can hear the prayer of one who is in distress,\ras I now am. I have a grievous wound; my hand is aching with pain,\rthere is no staunching the blood, and my whole arm drags by reason\rof my hurt, so that I cannot grasp my sword nor go among my foes and\rfight them, thou our prince, Jove\'s son Sarpedon, is slain. Jove defended\rnot his son, do you, therefore, O king, heal me of my wound, ease\rmy pain and grant me strength both to cheer on the Lycians and to\rfight along with them round the body of him who has fallen."\r\rThus did he pray, and Apollo heard his prayer. He eased his pain,\rstaunched the black blood from the wound, and gave him new strength.\rGlaucus perceived this, and was thankful that the mighty god had answered\rhis prayer; forthwith, therefore, he went among the Lycian captains,\rand bade them come to fight about the body of Sarpedon. From these\rhe strode on among the Trojans to Polydamas son of Panthous and Agenor;\rhe then went in search of Aeneas and Hector, and when he had found\rthem he said, "Hector, you have utterly forgotten your allies, who\rlanguish here for your sake far from friends and home while you do\rnothing to support them. Sarpedon leader of the Lycian warriors has\rfallen- he who was at once the right and might of Lycia; Mars has\rlaid him low by the spear of Patroclus. Stand by him, my friends,\rand suffer not the Myrmidons to strip him of his armour, nor to treat\rhis body with contumely in revenge for all the Danaans whom we have\rspeared at the ships." \r\rAs he spoke the Trojans were plunged in extreme and ungovernable grief;\rfor Sarpedon, alien though he was, had been one of the main stays\rof their city, both as having much people with him, and himself the\rforemost among them all. Led by Hector, who was infuriated by the\rfall of Sarpedon, they made instantly for the Danaans with all their\rmight, while the undaunted spirit of Patroclus son of Menoetius cheered\ron the Achaeans. First he spoke to the two Ajaxes, men who needed\rno bidding. "Ajaxes," said he, "may it now please you to show youselves\rthe men you have always been, or even better- Sarpedon is fallen-\rhe who was first to overleap the wall of the Achaeans; let us take\rthe body and outrage it; let us strip the armour from his shoulders,\rand kill his comrades if they try to rescue his body." \r\rHe spoke to men who of themselves were full eager; both sides, therefore,\rthe Trojans and Lycians on the one hand, and the Myrmidons and Achaeans\ron the other, strengthened their battalions, and fought desperately\rabout the body of Sarpedon, shouting fiercely the while. Mighty was\rthe din of their armour as they came together, and Jove shed a thick\rdarkness over the fight, to increase the of the battle over the body\rof his son. \r\rAt first the Trojans made some headway against the Achaeans, for one\rof the best men among the Myrmidons was killed, Epeigeus, son of noble\rAgacles who had erewhile been king in the good city of Budeum; but\rpresently, having killed a valiant kinsman of his own, he took refuge\rwith Peleus and Thetis, who sent him to Ilius the land of noble steeds\rto fight the Trojans under Achilles. Hector now struck him on the\rhead with a stone just as he had caught hold of the body, and his\rbrains inside his helmet were all battered in, so that he fell face\rforemost upon the body of Sarpedon, and there died. Patroclus was\renraged by the death of his comrade, and sped through the front ranks\ras swiftly as a hawk that swoops down on a flock of daws or starlings.\rEven so swiftly, O noble knight Patroclus, did you make straight for\rthe Lycians and Trojans to avenge your comrade. Forthwith he struck\rSthenelaus the son of Ithaemenes on the neck with a stone, and broke\rthe tendons that join it to the head and spine. On this Hector and\rthe front rank of his men gave ground. As far as a man can throw a\rjavelin when competing for some prize, or even in battle- so far did\rthe Trojans now retreat before the Achaeans. Glaucus, captain of the\rLycians, was the first to rally them, by killing Bathycles son of\rChalcon who lived in Hellas and was the richest man among the Myrmidons.\rGlaucus turned round suddenly, just as Bathycles who was pursuing\rhim was about to lay hold of him, and drove his spear right into the\rmiddle of his chest, whereon he fell heavily to the ground, and the\rfall of so good a man filled the Achaeans with dismay, while the Trojans\rwere exultant, and came up in a body round the corpse. Nevertheless\rthe Achaeans, mindful of their prowess, bore straight down upon them.\r\rMeriones then killed a helmed warrior of the Trojans, Laogonus son\rof Onetor, who was priest of Jove of Mt. Ida, and was honoured by\rthe people as though he were a god. Meriones struck him under the\rjaw and ear, so that life went out of him and the darkness of death\rlaid hold upon him. Aeneas then aimed a spear at Meriones, hoping\rto hit him under the shield as he was advancing, but Meriones saw\rit coming and stooped forward to avoid it, whereon the spear flew\rpast him and the point stuck in the ground, while the butt-end went\ron quivering till Mars robbed it of its force. The spear, therefore,\rsped from Aeneas\'s hand in vain and fell quivering to the ground.\rAeneas was angry and said, "Meriones, you are a good dancer, but if\rI had hit you my spear would soon have made an end of you."\r\rAnd Meriones answered, "Aeneas, for all your bravery, you will not\rbe able to make an end of every one who comes against you. You are\ronly a mortal like myself, and if I were to hit you in the middle\rof your shield with my spear, however strong and self-confident you\rmay be, I should soon vanquish you, and you would yield your life\rto Hades of the noble steeds." \r\rOn this the son of Menoetius rebuked him and said, "Meriones, hero\rthough you be, you should not speak thus; taunting speeches, my good\rfriend, will not make the Trojans draw away from the dead body; some\rof them must go under ground first; blows for battle, and words for\rcouncil; fight, therefore, and say nothing." \r\rHe led the way as he spoke and the hero went forward with him. As\rthe sound of woodcutters in some forest glade upon the mountains-\rand the thud of their axes is heard afar- even such a din now rose\rfrom earth-clash of bronze armour and of good ox-hide shields, as\rmen smote each other with their swords and spears pointed at both\rends. A man had need of good eyesight now to know Sarpedon, so covered\rwas he from head to foot with spears and blood and dust. Men swarmed\rabout the body, as flies that buzz round the full milk-pails in spring\rwhen they are brimming with milk- even so did they gather round Sarpedon;\rnor did Jove turn his keen eyes away for one moment from the fight,\rbut kept looking at it all the time, for he was settling how best\rto kill Patroclus, and considering whether Hector should be allowed\rto end him now in the fight round the body of Sarpedon, and strip\rhim of his armour, or whether he should let him give yet further trouble\rto the Trojans. In the end, he deemed it best that the brave squire\rof Achilles son of Peleus should drive Hector and the Trojans back\rtowards the city and take the lives of many. First, therefore, he\rmade Hector turn fainthearted, whereon he mounted his chariot and\rfled, bidding the other Trojans fly also, for he saw that the scales\rof Jove had turned against him. Neither would the brave Lycians stand\rfirm; they were dismayed when they saw their king lying struck to\rthe heart amid a heap of corpses- for when the son of Saturn made\rthe fight wax hot many had fallen above him. The Achaeans, therefore\rstripped the gleaming armour from his shoulders and the brave son\rof Menoetius gave it to his men to take to the ships. Then Jove lord\rof the storm-cloud said to Apollo, "Dear Phoebus, go, I pray you,\rand take Sarpedon out of range of the weapons; cleanse the black blood\rfrom off him, and then bear him a long way off where you may wash\rhim in the river, anoint him with ambrosia, and clothe him in immortal\rraiment; this done, commit him to the arms of the two fleet messengers,\rDeath, and Sleep, who will carry him straightway to the rich land\rof Lycia, where his brothers and kinsmen will inter him, and will\rraise both mound and pillar to his memory, in due honour to the dead."\r\rThus he spoke. Apollo obeyed his father\'s saying, and came down from\rthe heights of Ida into the thick of the fight; forthwith he took\rSarpedon out of range of the weapons, and then bore him a long way\roff, where he washed him in the river, anointed him with ambrosia\rand clothed him in immortal raiment; this done, he committed him to\rthe arms of the two fleet messengers, Death, and Sleep, who presently\rset him down in the rich land of Lycia. \r\rMeanwhile Patroclus, with many a shout to his horses and to Automedon,\rpursued the Trojans and Lycians in the pride and foolishness of his\rheart. Had he but obeyed the bidding of the son of Peleus, he would\rhave, escaped death and have been scatheless; but the counsels of\rJove pass man\'s understanding; he will put even a brave man to flight\rand snatch victory from his grasp, or again he will set him on to\rfight, as he now did when he put a high spirit into the heart of Patroclus.\r\rWho then first, and who last, was slain by you, O Patroclus, when\rthe gods had now called you to meet your doom? First Adrestus, Autonous,\rEcheclus, Perimus the son of Megas, Epistor and Melanippus; after\rthese he killed Elasus, Mulius, and Pylartes. These he slew, but the\rrest saved themselves by flight. \r\rThe sons of the Achaeans would now have taken Troy by the hands of\rPatroclus, for his spear flew in all directions, had not Phoebus Apollo\rtaken his stand upon the wall to defeat his purpose and to aid the\rTrojans. Thrice did Patroclus charge at an angle of the high wall,\rand thrice did Apollo beat him back, striking his shield with his\rown immortal hands. When Patroclus was coming on like a god for yet\ra fourth time, Apollo shouted to him with an awful voice and said,\r"Draw back, noble Patroclus, it is not your lot to sack the city of\rthe Trojan chieftains, nor yet will it be that of Achilles who is\ra far better man than you are." On hearing this, Patroclus withdrew\rto some distance and avoided the anger of Apollo. \r\rMeanwhile Hector was waiting with his horses inside the Scaean gates,\rin doubt whether to drive out again and go on fighting, or to call\rthe army inside the gates. As he was thus doubting Phoebus Apollo\rdrew near him in the likeness of a young and lusty warrior Asius,\rwho was Hector\'s uncle, being own brother to Hecuba, and son of Dymas\rwho lived in Phrygia by the waters of the river Sangarius; in his\rlikeness Jove\'s son Apollo now spoke to Hector saying, "Hector, why\rhave you left off fighting? It is ill done of you. If I were as much\rbetter a man than you, as I am worse, you should soon rue your slackness.\rDrive straight towards Patroclus, if so be that Apollo may grant you\ra triumph over him, and you may rull him." \r\rWith this the god went back into the hurly-burly, and Hector bade\rCebriones drive again into the fight. Apollo passed in among them,\rand struck panic into the Argives, while he gave triumph to Hector\rand the Trojans. Hector let the other Danaans alone and killed no\rman, but drove straight at Patroclus. Patroclus then sprang from his\rchariot to the ground, with a spear in his left hand, and in his right\ra jagged stone as large as his hand could hold. He stood still and\rthrew it, nor did it go far without hitting some one; the cast was\rnot in vain, for the stone struck Cebriones, Hector\'s charioteer,\ra bastard son of Priam, as he held the reins in his hands. The stone\rhit him on the forehead and drove his brows into his head for the\rbone was smashed, and his eyes fell to the ground at his feet. He\rdropped dead from his chariot as though he were diving, and there\rwas no more life left in him. Over him did you then vaunt, O knight\rPatroclus, saying, "Bless my heart, how active he is, and how well\rhe dives. If we had been at sea this fellow would have dived from\rthe ship\'s side and brought up as many oysters as the whole crew could\rstomach, even in rough water, for he has dived beautifully off his\rchariot on to the ground. It seems, then, that there are divers also\ramong the Trojans." \r\rAs he spoke he flung himself on Cebriones with the spring, as it were,\rof a lion that while attacking a stockyard is himself struck in the\rchest, and his courage is his own bane- even so furiously, O Patroclus,\rdid you then spring upon Cebriones. Hector sprang also from his chariot\rto the ground. The pair then fought over the body of Cebriones. As\rtwo lions fight fiercely on some high mountain over the body of a\rstag that they have killed, even so did these two mighty warriors,\rPatroclus son of Menoetius and brave Hector, hack and hew at one another\rover the corpse of Cebriones. Hector would not let him go when he\rhad once got him by the head, while Patroclus kept fast hold of his\rfeet, and a fierce fight raged between the other Danaans and Trojans.\rAs the east and south wind buffet one another when they beat upon\rsome dense forest on the mountains- there is beech and ash and spreading\rcornel; the to of the trees roar as they beat on one another, and\rone can hear the boughs cracking and breaking- even so did the Trojans\rand Achaeans spring upon one another and lay about each other, and\rneither side would give way. Many a pointed spear fell to ground and\rmany a winged arrow sped from its bow-string about the body of Cebriones;\rmany a great stone, moreover, beat on many a shield as they fought\raround his body, but there he lay in the whirling clouds of dust,\rall huge and hugely, heedless of his driving now. \r\rSo long as the sun was still high in mid-heaven the weapons of either\rside were alike deadly, and the people fell; but when he went down\rtowards the time when men loose their oxen, the Achaeans proved to\rbe beyond all forecast stronger, so that they drew Cebriones out of\rrange of the darts and tumult of the Trojans, and stripped the armour\rfrom his shoulders. Then Patroclus sprang like Mars with fierce intent\rand a terrific shout upon the Trojans, and thrice did he kill nine\rmen; but as he was coming on like a god for a time, then, O Patroclus,\rwas the hour of your end approaching, for Phoebus fought you in fell\rearnest. Patroclus did not see him as he moved about in the crush,\rfor he was enshrouded in thick darkness, and the god struck him from\rbehind on his back and his broad shoulders with the flat of his hand,\rso that his eyes turned dizzy. Phoebus Apollo beat the helmet from\roff his head, and it rolled rattling off under the horses\' feet, where\rits horse-hair plumes were all begrimed with dust and blood. Never\rindeed had that helmet fared so before, for it had served to protect\rthe head and comely forehead of the godlike hero Achilles. Now, however,\rZeus delivered it over to be worn by Hector. Nevertheless the end\rof Hector also was near. The bronze-shod spear, so great and so strong,\rwas broken in the hand of Patroclus, while his shield that covered\rhim from head to foot fell to the ground as did also the band that\rheld it, and Apollo undid the fastenings of his corslet.\r\rOn this his mind became clouded; his limbs failed him, and he stood\ras one dazed; whereon Euphorbus son of Panthous a Dardanian, the best\rspearman of his time, as also the finest horseman and fleetest runner,\rcame behind him and struck him in the back with a spear, midway between\rthe shoulders. This man as soon as ever he had come up with his chariot\rhad dismounted twenty men, so proficient was he in all the arts of\rwar- he it was, O knight Patroclus, that first drove a weapon into\ryou, but he did not quite overpower you. Euphorbus then ran back into\rthe crowd, after drawing his ashen spear out of the wound; he would\rnot stand firm and wait for Patroclus, unarmed though he now was,\rto attack him; but Patroclus unnerved, alike by the blow the god had\rgiven him and by the spear-wound, drew back under cover of his men\rin fear for his life. As when a lion has fought some\rfierce wild-boar and worsted him- the two fight furiously upon the\rmountains over some little fountain at which they would both drink,\rand the lion has beaten the boar till he can hardly breathe- even\rso did Hector son of Priam take the life of the brave son of Menoetius\rwho had killed so many, striking him from close at hand, and vaunting\rover him the while. "Patroclus," said he, "you deemed that you should\rsack our city, rob our Trojan women of their freedom, and carry them\roff in your ships to your own country. Fool; Hector and his fleet\rhorses were ever straining their utmost to defend them. I am foremost\rof all the Trojan warriors to stave the day of bondage from off them;\ras for you, vultures shall devour you here. Poor wretch, Achilles\rwith all his bravery availed you nothing; and yet I ween when you\rleft him he charged you straitly saying, \'Come not back to the ships,\rknight Patroclus, till you have rent the bloodstained shirt of murderous\rHector about his body. Thus I ween did he charge you, and your fool\'s\rheart answered him \'yea\' within you." \r\rThen, as the life ebbed out of you, you answered, O knight Patroclus:\r"Hector, vaunt as you will, for Jove the son of Saturn and Apollo\rhave vouchsafed you victory; it is they who have vanquished me so\reasily, and they who have stripped the armour from my shoulders; had\rtwenty such men as you attacked me, all of them would have fallen\rbefore my spear. Fate and the son of Leto have overpowered me, and\ramong mortal men Euphorbus; you are yourself third only in the killing\rof me. I say further, and lay my saying to your heart, you too shall\rlive but for a little season; death and the day of your doom are close\rupon you, and they will lay you low by the hand of Achilles son of\rAeacus." \r\rWhen he had thus spoken his eyes were closed in death, his soul left\rhis body and flitted down to the house of Hades, mourning its sad\rfate and bidding farewell to the youth and vigor of its manhood. Dead\rthough he was, Hector still spoke to him saying, "Patroclus, why should\ryou thus foretell my doom? Who knows but Achilles, son of lovely Thetis,\rmay be smitten by my spear and die before me?" \r\rAs he spoke he drew the bronze spear from the wound, planting his\rfoot upon the body, which he thrust off and let lie on its back. He\rthen went spear in hand after Automedon, squire of the fleet descendant\rof Aeacus, for he longed to lay him low, but the immortal steeds which\rthe gods had given as a rich gift to Peleus bore him swiftly from\rthe field. \r\r----------------------------------------------------------------------\r\rBOOK XVII\r\rBrave Menelaus son of Atreus now came to know that Patroclus had\rfallen, and made his way through the front ranks clad in full armour\rto bestride him. As a cow stands lowing over her first calf, even\rso did yellow-haired Menelaus bestride Patroclus. He held his round\rshield and his spear in front of him, resolute to kill any who should\rdare face him. But the son of Panthous had also noted the body, and\rcame up to Menelaus saying, "Menelaus, son of Atreus, draw back, leave\rthe body, and let the bloodstained spoils be. I was first of the Trojans\rand their brave allies to drive my spear into Patroclus, let me, therefore,\rhave my full glory among the Trojans, or I will take aim and kill\ryou." \r\rTo this Menelaus answered in great anger "By father Jove, boasting\ris an ill thing. The pard is not more bold, nor the lion nor savage\rwild-boar, which is fiercest and most dauntless of all creatures,\rthan are the proud sons of Panthous. Yet Hyperenor did not see out\rthe days of his youth when he made light of me and withstood me, deeming\rme the meanest soldier among the Danaans. His own feet never bore\rhim back to gladden his wife and parents. Even so shall I make an\rend of you too, if you withstand me; get you back into the crowd and\rdo not face me, or it shall be worse for you. Even a fool may be wise\rafter the event." \r\rEuphorbus would not listen, and said, "Now indeed, Menelaus, shall\ryou pay for the death of my brother over whom you vaunted, and whose\rwife you widowed in her bridal chamber, while you brought grief unspeakable\ron his parents. I shall comfort these poor people if I bring your\rhead and armour and place them in the hands of Panthous and noble\rPhrontis. The time is come when this matter shall be fought out and\rsettled, for me or against me." \r\rAs he spoke he struck Menelaus full on the shield, but the spear did\rnot go through, for the shield turned its point. Menelaus then took\raim, praying to father Jove as he did so; Euphorbus was drawing back,\rand Menelaus struck him about the roots of his throat, leaning his\rwhole weight on the spear, so as to drive it home. The point went\rclean through his neck, and his armour rang rattling round him as\rhe fell heavily to the ground. His hair which was like that of the\rGraces, and his locks so deftly bound in bands of silver and gold,\rwere all bedrabbled with blood. As one who has grown a fine young\rolive tree in a clear space where there is abundance of water- the\rplant is full of promise, and though the winds beat upon it from every\rquarter it puts forth its white blossoms till the blasts of some fierce\rhurricane sweep down upon it and level it with the ground- even so\rdid Menelaus strip the fair youth Euphorbus of his armour after he\rhad slain him. Or as some fierce lion upon the mountains in the pride\rof his strength fastens on the finest heifer in a herd as it is feeding-\rfirst he breaks her neck with his strong jaws, and then gorges on\rher blood and entrails; dogs and shepherds raise a hue and cry against\rhim, but they stand aloof and will not come close to him, for they\rare pale with fear- even so no one had the courage to face valiant\rMenelaus. The son of Atreus would have then carried off the armour\rof the son of Panthous with ease, had not Phoebus Apollo been angry,\rand in the guise of Mentes chief of the Cicons incited Hector to attack\rhim. "Hector," said he, "you are now going after the horses of the\rnoble son of Aeacus, but you will not take them; they cannot be kept\rin hand and driven by mortal man, save only by Achilles, who is son\rto an immortal mother. Meanwhile Menelaus son of Atreus has bestridden\rthe body of Patroclus and killed the noblest of the Trojans, Euphorbus\rson of Panthous, so that he can fight no more." \r\rThe god then went back into the toil and turmoil, but the soul of\rHector was darkened with a cloud of grief; he looked along the ranks\rand saw Euphorbus lying on the ground with the blood still flowing\rfrom his wound, and Menelaus stripping him of his armour. When the son of Atreus heard\rhim, he said to himself in his dismay, "Alas! what shall I do? I may\rnot let the Trojans take the armour of Patroclus who has fallen fighting\ron my behalf, lest some Danaan who sees me should cry shame upon me.\rStill if for my honour\'s sake I fight Hector and the Trojans single-handed,\rthey will prove too many for me, for Hector is bringing them up in\rforce. Meanwhile Achilles did not know that\rhe had fallen, for the fight was under the wall of Troy a long way\roff the ships. The horses of the descendant of Aeacus stood out of the fight\rand wept when they heard that their driver had been laid low by the\rhand of murderous Hector.
