When Hector saw him he said\rto Aeneas who was near him, "Aeneas, counsellor of the mail-clad Trojans,\rI see the steeds of the fleet son of Aeacus come into battle with\rweak hands to drive them. I am sure, if you think well, that we might\rtake them; they will not dare face us if we both attack them."\r\rThe valiant son of Anchises was of the same mind, and the pair went\rright on, with their shoulders covered under shields of tough dry\rox-hide, overlaid with much bronze. Chromius and Aretus went also\rwith them, and their hearts beat high with hope that they might kill\rthe men and capture the horses- fools that they were, for they were\rnot to return scatheless from their meeting with Automedon, who prayed\rto father Jove and was forthwith filled with courage and strength\rabounding. Nevertheless the issue lies\ron the lap of heaven, I will therefore hurl my spear and leave the\rrest to Jove." \r\rHe poised and hurled as he spoke, whereon the spear struck the round\rshield of Aretus, and went right through it for the shield stayed\rit not, so that it was driven through his belt into the lower part\rof his belly. He will\rgo back when he has given his horses their fill of being driven all\rwhithers under our walls, and will be in no mind to try and force\rhis way into the city. Neither will he ever sack it, dogs shall devour\rhim ere he do so." \r\rHector looked fiercely at him and answered, "Polydamas, your words\rare not to my liking in that you bid us go back and be pent within\rthe city. Have you not had enough of being cooped up behind walls?\rIn the old-days the city of Priam was famous the whole world over\rfor its wealth of gold and bronze, but our treasures are wasted out\rof our houses, and much goods have been sold away to Phrygia and fair\rMeonia, for the hand of Jove has been laid heavily upon us. Now, therefore,\rthat the son of scheming Saturn has vouchsafed me to win glory here\rand to hem the Achaeans in at their ships, prate no more in this fool\'s\rwise among the people. You will have no man with you; it shall not\rbe; do all of you as I now say;- take your suppers in your companies\rthroughout the host, and keep your watches and be wakeful every man\rof you. If any Trojan is uneasy about his possessions, let him gather\rthem and give them out among the people. Better let these, rather\rthan the Achaeans, have them. At daybreak we will arm and fight about\rthe ships; granted that Achilles has again come forward to defend\rthem, let it be as he will, but it shall go hard with him. I shall\rnot shun him, but will fight him, to fall or conquer. The god of war\rdeals out like measure to all, and the slayer may yet be slain."\r\rThus spoke Hector; and the Trojans, fools that they were, shouted\rin applause, for Pallas Minerva had robbed them of their understanding.\rThey gave ear to Hector with his evil counsel, but the wise words\rof Polydamas no man would heed. They took their supper throughout\rthe host, and meanwhile through the whole night the Achaeans mourned\rPatroclus, and the son of Peleus led them in their lament. He laid\rhis murderous hands upon the breast of his comrade, groaning again\rand again as a bearded lion when a man who was chasing deer has robbed\rhim of his young in some dense forest; when the lion comes back he\ris furious, and searches dingle and dell to track the hunter if he\rcan find him, for he is mad with rage- even so with many a sigh did\rAchilles speak among the Myrmidons saying, "Alas! vain were the words\rwith which I cheered the hero Menoetius in his own house; I said that\rI would bring his brave son back again to Opoeis after he had sacked\rIlius and taken his share of the spoils- but Jove does not give all\rmen their heart\'s desire. The same soil shall be reddened here at\rTroy by the blood of us both, for I too shall never be welcomed home\rby the old knight Peleus, nor by my mother Thetis, but even in this\rplace shall the earth cover me. Thereon they\rset a tripod full of bath water on to a clear fire: they threw sticks\ron to it to make it blaze, and the water became hot as the flame played\rabout the belly of the tripod. When the water in the cauldron was\rboiling they washed the body, anointed it with oil, and closed its\rwounds with ointment that had been kept nine years. Then they laid\rit on a bier and covered it with a linen cloth from head to foot,\rand over this they laid a fair white robe. Thus all night long did\rthe Myrmidons gather round Achilles to mourn Patroclus. \r\rThen Jove said to Juno his sister-wife, "So, Queen Juno, you have\rgained your end, and have roused fleet Achilles. One would think that\rthe Achaeans were of your own flesh and blood." \r\rAnd Juno answered, "Dread son of Saturn, why should you say this thing?\rMay not a man though he be only mortal and knows less than we do,\rdo what he can for another person? And shall not I- foremost of all\rgoddesses both by descent and as wife to you who reign in heaven-\rdevise evil for the Trojans if I am angry with them?" \r\rThus did they converse. Meanwhile Thetis came to the house of Vulcan,\rimperishable, star-bespangled, fairest of the abodes in heaven, a\rhouse of bronze wrought by the lame god\'s own hands. She found him\rbusy with his bellows, sweating and hard at work, for he was making\rtwenty tripods that were to stand by the wall of his house, and he\rset wheels of gold under them all that they might go of their own\rselves to the assemblies of the gods, and come back again- marvels\rindeed to see. They were finished all but the ears of cunning workmanship\rwhich yet remained to be fixed to them: these he was now fixing, and\rhe was hammering at the rivets. While he was thus at work silver-footed\rThetis came to the house. Charis, of graceful head-dress, wife to\rthe far-famed lame god, came towards her as soon as she saw her, and\rtook her hand in her own, saying, "Why have you come to our house,\rThetis, honoured and ever welcome- for you do not visit us often?\rCome inside and let me set refreshment before you." \r\rThe goddess led the way as she spoke, and bade Thetis sit on a richly\rdecorated seat inlaid with silver; there was a footstool also under\rher feet. It would have gone hardly with me had not Eurynome,\rdaughter of the ever-encircling waters of Oceanus, and Thetis, taken\rme to their bosom. Nine years did I stay with them, and many beautiful\rworks in bronze, brooches, spiral armlets, cups, and chains, did I\rmake for them in their cave, with the roaring waters of Oceanus foaming\ras they rushed ever past it; and no one knew, neither of gods nor\rmen, save only Thetis and Eurynome who took care of me. If, then,\rThetis has come to my house I must make her due requital for having\rsaved me; entertain her, therefore, with all hospitality, while I\rput by my bellows and all my tools." \r\rOn this the mighty monster hobbled off from his anvil, his thin legs\rplying lustily under him. He set the bellows away from the fire, and\rgathered his tools into a silver chest. Then he took a sponge and\rwashed his face and hands, his shaggy chest and brawny neck; he donned\rhis shirt, grasped his strong staff, and limped towards the door.\rThere were golden handmaids also who worked for him, and were like\rreal young women, with sense and reason, voice also and strength,\rand all the learning of the immortals; these busied themselves as\rthe king bade them, while he drew near to Thetis, seated her upon\ra goodly seat, and took her hand in his own, saying, "Why have you\rcome to our house, Thetis honoured and ever welcome- for you do not\rvisit us often? Say what you want, and I will do it for you at once\rif I can, and if it can be done at all." \r\rThetis wept and answered, "Vulcan, is there another goddess in Olympus\rwhom the son of Saturn has been pleased to try with so much affliction\ras he has me? Me alone of the marine goddesses did he make subject\rto a mortal husband, Peleus son of Aeacus, and sorely against my will\rdid I submit to the embraces of one who was but mortal, and who now\rstays at home worn out with age. Twenty bellows\rblew upon the melting-pots, and they blew blasts of every kind, some\rfierce to help him when he had need of them, and others less strong\ras Vulcan willed it in the course of his work. He made the shield in five thicknesses,\rand with many a wonder did his cunning hand enrich it. \r\rHe wrought the earth, the heavens, and the sea; the moon also at her\rfull and the untiring sun, with all the signs that glorify the face\rof heaven- the Pleiads, the Hyads, huge Orion, and the Bear, which\rmen also call the Wain and which turns round ever in one place, facing.\rOrion, and alone never dips into the stream of Oceanus. \r\rHe wrought also two cities, fair to see and busy with the hum of men.\rIn the one were weddings and wedding-feasts, and they were going about\rthe city with brides whom they were escorting by torchlight from their\rchambers. Loud rose the cry of Hymen, and the youths danced to the\rmusic of flute and lyre, while the women stood each at her house door\rto see them. \r\rMeanwhile the people were gathered in assembly, for there was a quarrel,\rand two men were wrangling about the blood-money for a man who had\rbeen killed, the one saying before the people that he had paid damages\rin full, and the other that he had not been paid. Each was trying\rto make his own case good, and the people took sides, each man backing\rthe side that he had taken; but the heralds kept them back, and the\relders sate on their seats of stone in a solemn circle, holding the\rstaves which the heralds had put into their hands. Then they rose\rand each in his turn gave judgement, and there were two talents laid\rdown, to be given to him whose judgement should be deemed the fairest.\r\rAbout the other city there lay encamped two hosts in gleaming armour,\rand they were divided whether to sack it, or to spare it and accept\rthe half of what it contained. But the men of the city would not yet\rconsent, and armed themselves for a surprise; their wives and little\rchildren kept guard upon the walls, and with them were the men who\rwere past fighting through age; but the others sallied forth with\rMars and Pallas Minerva at their head- both of them wrought in gold\rand clad in golden raiment, great and fair with their armour as befitting\rgods, while they that followed were smaller. When they reached the\rplace where they would lay their ambush, it was on a riverbed to which\rlive stock of all kinds would come from far and near to water; here,\rthen, they lay concealed, clad in full armour. Some way off them there\rwere two scouts who were on the look-out for the coming of sheep or\rcattle, which presently came, followed by two shepherds who were playing\ron their pipes, and had not so much as a thought of danger. When those\rwho were in ambush saw this, they cut off the flocks and herds and\rkilled the shepherds. With them were Strife and\rRiot, and fell Fate who was dragging three men after her, one with\ra fresh wound, and the other unwounded, while the third was dead,\rand she was dragging him along by his heel: and her robe was bedrabbled\rin men\'s blood. Each time that they turned on reaching\rthe headland a man would come up to them and give them a cup of wine,\rand they would go back to their furrows looking forward to the time\rwhen they should again reach the headland. The part that they had\rploughed was dark behind them, so that the field, though it was of\rgold, still looked as if it were being ploughed- very curious to behold.\r\rHe wrought also a field of harvest corn, and the reapers were reaping\rwith sharp sickles in their hands. The bunches overhead were black, but the\rvines were trained on poles of silver. The\rmaidens wore robes of light linen, and the youths well woven shirts\rthat were slightly oiled. The girls were crowned with garlands, while\rthe young men had daggers of gold that hung by silver baldrics; sometimes\rthey would dance deftly in a ring with merry twinkling feet, as it\rwere a potter sitting at his work and making trial of his wheel to\rsee whether it will run, and sometimes they would go all in line with\rone another, and much people was gathered joyously about the green.\rThere was a bard also to sing to them and play his lyre, while two\rtumblers went about performing in the midst of them when the man struck\rup with his tune. \r\rAll round the outermost rim of the shield he set the mighty stream\rof the river Oceanus. \r\rThen when he had fashioned the shield so great and strong, he made\ra breastplate also that shone brighter than fire. He made helmet,\rclose fitting to the brow, and richly worked, with a golden plume\roverhanging it; and he made greaves also of beaten tin. \r\rLastly, when the famed lame god had made all the armour, he took it\rand set it before the mother of Achilles; whereon she darted like\ra falcon from the snowy summits of Olympus and bore away the gleaming\rarmour from the house of Vulcan. \r\r----------------------------------------------------------------------\r\rBOOK XIX\r\rNow when Dawn in robe of saffron was hasting from the streams of\rOceanus, to bring light to mortals and immortals, Thetis reached the\rships with the armour that the god had given her. She found her son\rfallen about the body of Patroclus and weeping bitterly. Many also\rof his followers were weeping round him, but when the goddess came\ramong them she clasped his hand in her own, saying, "My son, grieve\ras we may we must let this man lie, for it is by heaven\'s will that\rhe has fallen; now, therefore, accept from Vulcan this rich and goodly\rarmour, which no man has ever yet borne upon his shoulders."\r\rAs she spoke she set the armour before Achilles, and it rang out bravely\ras she did so. Then, as soon as he had satisfied himself with looking at\rit, he said to his mother, "Mother, the god has given me armour, meet\rhandiwork for an immortal and such as no living could have fashioned;\rI will now arm, but I much fear that flies will settle upon the son\rof Menoetius and breed worms about his wounds, so that his body, now\rhe is dead, will be disfigured and the flesh will rot." \r\rSilver-footed Thetis answered, "My son, be not disquieted about this\rmatter. I will find means to protect him from the swarms of noisome\rflies that prey on the bodies of men who have been killed in battle.\rHe may lie for a whole year, and his flesh shall still be as sound\ras ever, or even sounder. Call, therefore, the Achaean heroes in assembly;\runsay your anger against Agamemnon; arm at once, and fight with might\rand main." \r\rAs she spoke she put strength and courage into his heart, and she\rthen dropped ambrosia and red nectar into the wounds of Patroclus,\rthat his body might suffer no change. \r\rThen Achilles went out upon the seashore, and with a loud cry called\ron the Achaean heroes. On this even those who as yet had stayed always\rat the ships, the pilots and helmsmen, and even the stewards who were\rabout the ships and served out rations, all came to the place of assembly\rbecause Achilles had shown himself after having held aloof so long\rfrom fighting. Two sons of Mars, Ulysses and the son of Tydeus, came\rlimping, for their wounds still pained them; nevertheless they came,\rand took their seats in the front row of the assembly. Last of all\rcame Agamemnon, king of men, he too wounded, for Coon son of Antenor\rhad struck him with a spear in battle. \r\rWhen the Achaeans were got together Achilles rose and said, "Son of\rAtreus, surely it would have been better alike for both you and me,\rwhen we two were in such high anger about Briseis, surely it would\rhave been better, had Diana\'s arrow slain her at the ships on the\rday when I took her after having sacked Lyrnessus. For so, many an\rAchaean the less would have bitten dust before the foe in the days\rof my anger. It has been well for Hector and the Trojans, but the\rAchaeans will long indeed remember our quarrel. Now, however, let\rit be, for it is over. If we have been angry, necessity has schooled\rour anger. I put it from me: I dare not nurse it for ever; therefore,\rbid the Achaeans arm forthwith that I may go out against the Trojans,\rand learn whether they will be in a mind to sleep by the ships or\rno. Glad, I ween, will he be to rest his knees who may fly my spear\rwhen I wield it." \r\rThus did he speak, and the Achaeans rejoiced in that he had put away\rhis anger. \r\rThen Agamemnon spoke, rising in his place, and not going into the\rmiddle of the assembly. "Danaan heroes," said he, "servants of Mars,\rit is well to listen when a man stands up to speak, and it is not\rseemly to interrupt him, or it will go hard even with a practised\rspeaker. Who can either hear or speak in an uproar? Even the finest\rorator will be disconcerted by it. I will expound to the son of Peleus,\rand do you other Achaeans heed me and mark me well. Often have the\rAchaeans spoken to me of this matter and upbraided me, but it was\rnot I that did it: Jove, and Fate, and Erinys that walks in darkness\rstruck me mad when we were assembled on the day that I took from Achilles\rthe meed that had been awarded to him. What could I do? All things\rare in the hand of heaven, and Folly, eldest of Jove\'s daughters,\rshuts men\'s eyes to their destruction. She walks delicately, not on\rthe solid earth, but hovers over the heads of men to make them stumble\ror to ensnare them. \r\r"Time was when she fooled Jove himself, who they say is greatest whether\rof gods or men; for Juno, woman though she was, beguiled him on the\rday when Alcmena was to bring forth mighty Hercules in the fair city\rof Thebes. He told it out among the gods saying, \'Hear me all gods\rand goddesses, that I may speak even as I am minded; this day shall\ran Ilithuia, helper of women who are in labour, bring a man child\rinto the world who shall be lord over all that dwell about him who\rare of my blood and lineage.\' Then said Juno all crafty and full of\rguile, \'You will play false, and will not hold to your word. For Juno darted down from the high summit\rof Olympus, and went in haste to Achaean Argos where she knew that\rthe noble wife of Sthenelus son of Perseus then was. Even so did I grieve when mighty\rHector was killing the Argives at their ships, and all the time I\rkept thinking of Folly who had so baned me. I was blind, and Jove\rrobbed me of my reason; I will now make atonement, and will add much\rtreasure by way of amends. Go, therefore, into battle, you and your\rpeople with you. No man can do battle\rthe livelong day to the going down of the sun if he is without food;\rhowever much he may want to fight his strength will fail him before\rhe knows it; hunger and thirst will find him out, and his limbs will\rgrow weary under him. I will swear as you would\rhave me do; I do so of my own free will, neither shall I take the\rname of heaven in vain. Therefore I can take thought of nothing save\ronly slaughter and blood and the rattle in the throat of the dying."\r\rUlysses answered, "Achilles, son of Peleus, mightiest of all the Achaeans,\rin battle you are better than I, and that more than a little, but\rin counsel I am much before you, for I am older and of greater knowledge.\rTherefore be patient under my words. Fighting is a thing of which\rmen soon surfeit, and when Jove, who is wars steward, weighs the upshot,\rit may well prove that the straw which our sickles have reaped is\rfar heavier than the grain. It may not be that the Achaeans should\rmourn the dead with their bellies; day by day men fall thick and threefold\rcontinually; when should we have respite from our sorrow? Let us mourn\rour dead for a day and bury them out of sight and mind, but let those\rof us who are left eat and drink that we may arm and fight our foes\rmore fiercely. In that hour let no man hold back, waiting for a second\rsummons; such summons shall bode ill for him who is found lagging\rbehind at our ships; let us rather sally as one man and loose the\rfury of war upon the Trojans." \r\rWhen he had thus spoken he took with him the sons of Nestor, with\rMeges son of Phyleus, Thoas, Meriones, Lycomedes son of Creontes,\rand Melanippus, and went to the tent of Agamemnon son of Atreus. The\rword was not sooner said than the deed was done: they brought out\rthe seven tripods which Agamemnon had promised, with the twenty metal\rcauldrons and the twelve horses; they also brought the women skilled\rin useful arts, seven in number, with Briseis, which made eight. The son of Atreus drew the knife which\rhe wore by the scabbard of his mighty sword, and began by cutting\roff some bristles from the boar, lifting up his hands in prayer as\rhe did so. The other Achaeans sat where they were all silent and orderly\rto hear the king, and Agamemnon looked into the vault of heaven and\rprayed saying, "I call Jove the first and mightiest of all gods to\rwitness, I call also Earth and Sun and the Erinyes who dwell below\rand take vengeance on him who shall swear falsely, that I have laid\rno hand upon the girl Briseis, neither to take her to my bed nor otherwise,\rbut that she has remained in my tents inviolate. If I swear falsely\rmay heaven visit me with all the penalties which it metes out to those\rwho perjure themselves." \r\rHe cut the boar\'s throat as he spoke, whereon Talthybius whirled it\rround his head, and flung it into the wide sea to feed the fishes.\rThen Achilles also rose and said to the Argives, "Father Jove, of\ra truth you blind men\'s eyes and bane them. The son of Atreus had\rnot else stirred me to so fierce an anger, nor so stubbornly taken\rBriseis from me against my will. Surely Jove must have counselled\rthe destruction of many an Argive. Go, now, and take your food that\rwe may begin fighting." \r\rOn this he broke up the assembly, and every man went back to his own\rship. The Myrmidons attended to the presents and took them away to\rthe ship of Achilles. They placed them in his tents, while the stable-men\rdrove the horses in among the others. \r\rBriseis, fair as Venus, when she saw the mangled body of Patroclus,\rflung herself upon it and cried aloud, tearing her breast, her neck,\rand her lovely face with both her hands. Beautiful as a goddess she\rwept and said, "Patroclus, dearest friend, when I went hence I left\ryou living; I return, O prince, to find you dead; thus do fresh sorrows\rmultiply upon me one after the other. I saw him to whom my father\rand mother married me, cut down before our city, and my three own\rdear brothers perished with him on the self-same day; but you, Patroclus,\reven when Achilles slew my husband and sacked the city of noble Mynes,\rtold me that I was not to weep, for you said you would make Achilles\rmarry me, and take me back with him to Phthia, we should have a wedding\rfeast among the Myrmidons. You were always kind to me and I shall\rnever cease to grieve for you." \r\rShe wept as she spoke, and the women joined in her lament-making as\rthough their tears were for Patroclus, but in truth each was weeping\rfor her own sorrows. The elders of the Achaeans gathered round Achilles\rand prayed him to take food, but he groaned and would not do so. "I\rpray you," said he, "if any comrade will hear me, bid me neither eat\rnor drink, for I am in great heaviness, and will stay fasting even\rto the going down of the sun." \r\rOn this he sent the other princes away, save only the two sons of\rAtreus and Ulysses, Nestor, Idomeneus, and the knight Phoenix, who\rstayed behind and tried to comfort him in the bitterness of his sorrow:\rbut he would not be comforted till he should have flung himself into\rthe jaws of battle, and he fetched sigh on sigh, thinking ever of\rPatroclus. Then he said- \r\r"Hapless and dearest comrade, you it was who would get a good dinner\rready for me at once and without delay when the Achaeans were hasting\rto fight the Trojans; now, therefore, though I have meat and drink\rin my tents, yet will I fast for sorrow. Grief greater than this I\rcould not know, not even though I were to hear of the death of my\rfather, who is now in Phthia weeping for the loss of me his son, who\ram here fighting the Trojans in a strange land for the accursed sake\rof Helen, nor yet though I should hear that my son is no more- he\rwho is being brought up in Scyros- if indeed Neoptolemus is still\rliving. The son of Saturn looked down\rwith pity upon them, and said presently to Minerva, "My child, you\rhave quite deserted your hero; is he then gone so clean out of your\rrecollection? He slung the silver-studded\rsword of bronze about his shoulders, and then took up the shield so\rgreat and strong that shone afar with a splendour as of the moon.\rAs the light seen by sailors from out at sea, when men have lit a\rfire in their homestead high up among the mountains, but the sailors\rare carried out to sea by wind and storm far from the haven where\rthey would be- even so did the gleam of Achilles\' wondrous shield\rstrike up into the heavens. Pelion, which Chiron had once given to Peleus,\rfraught with the death of heroes. You need not do so, for I well know that I am to\rfall here, far from my dear father and mother; none the more, however,\rshall I stay my hand till I have given the Trojans their fill of fighting."\r\rSo saying, with a loud cry he drove his horses to the front.\r\r----------------------------------------------------------------------\r\rBOOK XX\r\rThus, then, did the Achaeans arm by their ships round you, O son\rof Peleus, who were hungering for battle; while the Trojans over against\rthem armed upon the rise of the plain. \r\rMeanwhile Jove from the top of many-delled Olympus, bade Themis gather\rthe gods in council, whereon she went about and called them to the\rhouse of Jove. There was not a river absent except Oceanus, nor a\rsingle one of the nymphs that haunt fair groves, or springs of rivers\rand meadows of green grass. When they reached the house of cloud-compelling\rJove, they took their seats in the arcades of polished marble which\rVulcan with his consummate skill had made for father Jove.\r\rIn such wise, therefore, did they gather in the house of Jove. Neptune\ralso, lord of the earthquake, obeyed the call of the goddess, and\rcame up out of the sea to join them. There, sitting in the midst of\rthem, he asked what Jove\'s purpose might be. Are you considering\rsome matter that concerns the Trojans and Achaeans- for the blaze\rof battle is on the point of being kindled between them?"\r\rAnd Jove answered, "You know my purpose, shaker of earth, and wherefore\rI have called you hither. I take thought for them even in their destruction.\rFor my own part I shall stay here seated on Mt. Olympus and look on\rin peace, but do you others go about among Trojans and Achaeans, and\rhelp either side as you may be severally disposed. If Achilles fights\rthe Trojans without hindrance they will make no stand against him;\rthey have ever trembled at the sight of him, and now that he is roused\rto such fury about his comrade, he will override fate itself and storm\rtheir city." \r\rThus spoke Jove and gave the word for war, whereon the gods took their\rseveral sides and went into battle. Juno, Pallas Minerva, earth-encircling\rNeptune, Mercury bringer of good luck and excellent in all cunning-\rall these joined the host that came from the ships; with them also\rcame Vulcan in all his glory, limping, but yet with his thin legs\rplying lustily under him. Mars of gleaming helmet joined the Trojans,\rand with him Apollo of locks unshorn, and the archer goddess Diana,\rLeto, Xanthus, and laughter-loving Venus. \r\rSo long as the gods held themselves aloof from mortal warriors the\rAchaeans were triumphant, for Achilles who had long refused to fight\rwas now with them. There was not a Trojan but his limbs failed him\rfor fear as he beheld the fleet son of Peleus all glorious in his\rarmour, and looking like Mars himself. When, however, the Olympians\rcame to take their part among men, forthwith uprose strong Strife,\rrouser of hosts, and Minerva raised her loud voice, now standing by\rthe deep trench that ran outside the wall, and now shouting with all\rher might upon the shore of the sounding sea. Mars also bellowed out\rupon the other side, dark as some black thunder-cloud, and called\ron the Trojans at the top of his voice, now from the acropolis, and\rnow speeding up the side of the river Simois till he came to the hill\rCallicolone. \r\rThus did the gods spur on both hosts to fight, and rouse fierce contention\ralso among themselves. The sire of gods and men thundered from heaven\rabove, while from beneath Neptune shook the vast earth, and bade the\rhigh hills tremble. The spurs and crests of many-fountained Ida quaked,\ras also the city of the Trojans and the ships of the Achaeans. Such was\rthe uproar as the gods came together in battle. Apollo with his arrows\rtook his stand to face King Neptune, while Minerva took hers against\rthe god of war; the archer-goddess Diana with her golden arrows, sister\rof far-darting Apollo, stood to face Juno; Mercury the lusty bringer\rof good luck faced Leto, while the mighty eddying river whom men can\rScamander, but gods Xanthus, matched himself against Vulcan.\r\rThe gods, then, were thus ranged against one another. But the heart\rof Achilles was set on meeting Hector son of Priam, for it was with\rhis blood that he longed above all things else to glut the stubborn\rlord of battle. In his likeness therefore, he said to Aeneas,\r"Aeneas, counsellor of the Trojans, where are now the brave words\rwith which you vaunted over your wine before the Trojan princes, saying\rthat you would fight Achilles son of Peleus in single combat?"\r\rAnd Aeneas answered, "Why do you thus bid me fight the proud son of\rPeleus, when I am in no mind to do so? Learn, then, my lineage\rif you will- and it is known to many. \r\r"In the beginning Dardanus was the son of Jove, and founded Dardania,\rfor Ilius was not yet stablished on the plain for men to dwell in,\rand her people still abode on the spurs of many-fountained Ida. "Let not the Trojans," he cried, "keep you at arm\'s length, Achaeans,\rbut go for them and fight them man for man. However valiant I may\rbe, I cannot give chase to so many and fight all of them. Even Mars,\rwho is an immortal, or Minerva, would shrink from flinging himself\rinto the jaws of such a fight and laying about him; nevertheless,\rso far as in me lies I will show no slackness of hand or foot nor\rwant of endurance, not even for a moment; I will utterly break their\rranks, and woe to the Trojan who shall venture within reach of my\rspear." \r\rThus did he exhort them. Meanwhile Hector called upon the Trojans\rand declared that he would fight Achilles. "Be not afraid, proud Trojans,"\rsaid he, "to face the son of Peleus; I could fight gods myself if\rthe battle were one of words only, but they would be more than a match\rfor me, if we had to use our spears. Even so the deed of Achilles\rwill fall somewhat short of his word; he will do in part, and the\rother part he will clip short. I will go up against him though his\rhands be as fire- though his hands be fire and his strength iron."\r\rThus urged the Trojans lifted up their spears against the Achaeans,\rand raised the cry of battle as they flung themselves into the midst\rof their ranks. But Phoebus Apollo came up to Hector and said, "Hector,\ron no account must you challenge Achilles to single combat; keep a\rlookout for him while you are under cover of the others and away from\rthe thick of the fight, otherwise he will either hit you with a spear\ror cut you down at close quarters." \r\rThus he spoke, and Hector drew back within the crowd, for he was afraid\rwhen he heard what the god had said to him. Achilles then sprang upon\rthe Trojans with a terrible cry, clothed in valour as with a garment.\rFirst he killed Iphition son of Otrynteus, a leader of much people\rwhom a naiad nymph had borne to Otrynteus waster of cities, in the\rland of Hyde under the snowy heights of Mt. Tmolus. Achilles struck\rhim full on the head as he was coming on towards him, and split it\rclean in two; whereon he fell heavily to the ground and Achilles vaunted\rover him saying, "You he low, son of Otrynteus, mighty hero; your\rdeath is here, but your lineage is on the Gygaean lake where your\rfather\'s estate lies, by Hyllus, rich in fish, and the eddying waters\rof Hermus." \r\rThus did he vaunt, but darkness closed the eyes of the other. The\rchariots of the Achaeans cut him up as their wheels passed over him\rin the front of the battle, and after him Achilles killed Demoleon,\ra valiant man of war and son to Antenor. He struck him on the temple\rthrough his bronze-cheeked helmet. The helmet did not stay the spear,\rbut it went right on, crushing the bone so that the brain inside was\rshed in all directions, and his lust of fighting was ended. Then he\rstruck Hippodamas in the midriff as he was springing down from his\rchariot in front of him, and trying to escape. He breathed his last,\rbellowing like a bull bellows when young men are dragging him to offer\rhim in sacrifice to the King of Helice, and the heart of the earth-shaker\ris glad; even so did he bellow as he lay dying. He, in his folly and showing off the\rfleetness of his feet, was rushing about among front ranks until he\rlost his life, for Achilles struck him in the middle of the back as\rhe was darting past him: he struck him just at the golden fastenings\rof his belt and where the two pieces of the double breastplate overlapped.\rThe point of the spear pierced him through and came out by the navel,\rwhereon he fell groaning on to his knees and a cloud of darkness overshadowed\rhim as he sank holding his entrails in his hands. \r\rWhen Hector saw his brother Polydorus with his entrails in his hands\rand sinking down upon the ground, a mist came over his eyes, and he\rcould not bear to keep longer at a distance; he therefore poised his\rspear and darted towards Achilles like a flame of fire. When Achilles\rsaw him he bounded forward and vaunted saying, "This is he that has\rwounded my heart most deeply and has slain my beloved comrade. Not\rfor long shall we two quail before one another on the highways of\rwar." \r\rHe looked fiercely on Hector and said, "Draw near, that you may meet\ryour doom the sooner." Hector feared him not and answered, "Son of\rPeleus, think not that your words can scare me as though I were a\rchild; I too if I will can brag and talk unseemly; I know that you\rare a mighty warrior, mightier by far than I, nevertheless the issue\rlies in the the lap of heaven whether I, worse man though I be, may\rnot slay you with my spear, for this too has been found keen ere now."\r\rHe hurled his spear as he spoke, but Minerva breathed upon it, and\rthough she breathed but very lightly she turned it back from going\rtowards Achilles, so that it returned to Hector and lay at his feet\rin front of him. There he let him lie and stayed\rDemouchus son of Philetor, a man both brave and of great stature,\rby hitting him on the knee with a spear; then he smote him with his\rsword and killed him. Or as one who yokes\rbroad-browed oxen that they may tread barley in a threshing-floor-\rand it is soon bruised small under the feet of the lowing cattle-\reven so did the horses of Achilles trample on the shields and bodies\rof the slain. The axle underneath and the railing that ran round the\rcar were bespattered with clots of blood thrown up by the horses\'\rhoofs, and from the tyres of the wheels; but the son of Peleus pressed\ron to win still further glory, and his hands were bedrabbled with\rgore. \r\r----------------------------------------------------------------------\r\rBOOK XXI\r\rNow when they came to the ford of the full-flowing river Xanthus,\rbegotten of immortal Jove, Achilles cut their forces in two: one half\rhe chased over the plain towards the city by the same way that the\rAchaeans had taken when flying panic-stricken on the preceding day\rwith Hector in full triumph; this way did they fly pell-mell, and\rJuno sent down a thick mist in front of them to stay them. The waters resounded, and the banks rang again,\ras they swam hither and thither with loud cries amid the whirling\reddies. As locusts flying to a river before the blast of a grass fire-\rthe flame comes on and on till at last it overtakes them and they\rhuddle into the water- even so was the eddying stream of Xanthus filled\rwith the uproar of men and horses, all struggling in confusion before\rAchilles. \r\rForthwith the hero left his spear upon the bank, leaning it against\ra tamarisk bush, and plunged into the river like a god, armed with\rhis sword only. Therefore\ras Jove is mightier than any river that flows into the sea, so are\rhis children stronger than those of any river whatsoever. As one who would water\rhis garden leads a stream from some fountain over his plants, and\rall his ground-spade in hand he clears away the dams to free the channels,\rand the little stones run rolling round and round with the water as\rit goes merrily down the bank faster than the man can follow- even\rso did the river keep catching up with Achilles albeit he was a fleet\rrunner, for the gods are stronger than men. I will leave off, since you so desire it, and let son\rleave off also. Jove heard as he was sitting\ron Olympus, and laughed for joy when he saw the gods coming to blows\ramong themselves. They were not long about beginning, and Mars piercer\rof shields opened the battle. Sword in hand he sprang at once upon\rMinerva and reviled her. "Why, vixen," said he, "have you again set\rthe gods by the ears in the pride and haughtiness of your heart? Baby, why keep your bow thus idle? Monster that he is; would indeed that the gods loved him no better\rthan I do, for so, dogs and vultures would soon devour him as he lay\rstretched on earth, and a load of grief would be lifted from my heart,\rfor many a brave son has he reft from me, either by killing them or\rselling them away in the islands that are beyond the sea: even now\rI miss two sons from among the Trojans who have thronged within the\rcity, Lycaon and Polydorus, whom Laothoe peeress among women bore\rme. With a loud cry he called Diphobus and asked him\rfor one, but there was no man; then he saw the truth and said to himself,\r"Alas! the gods have lured me on to my destruction. My doom has come upon me; let me not then die\ringloriously and without a struggle, but let me first do some great\rthing that shall be told among men hereafter." \r\rAs he spoke he drew the keen blade that hung so great and strong by\rhis side, and gathering himself together be sprang on Achilles like\ra soaring eagle which swoops down from the clouds on to some lamb\ror timid hare- even so did Hector brandish his sword and spring upon\rAchilles. Achilles mad with rage darted towards him, with his wondrous\rshield before his breast, and his gleaming helmet, made with four\rlayers of metal, nodding fiercely forward. The thick tresses of gold\rwi which Vulcan had crested the helmet floated round it, and as the\revening star that shines brighter than all others through the stillness\rof night, even such was the gleam of the spear which Achilles poised\rin his right hand, fraught with the death of noble Hector. He eyed\rhis fair flesh over and over to see where he could best wound it,\rbut all was protected by the goodly armour of which Hector had spoiled\rPatroclus after he had slain him, save only the throat where the collar-bones\rdivide the neck from the shoulders, and this is a most deadly place:\rhere then did Achilles strike him as he was coming on towards him,\rand the point of his spear went right through the fleshy part of the\rneck, but it did not sever his windpipe so that he could still speak.\rHector fell headlong, and Achilles vaunted over him saying, "Hector,\ryou deemed that you should come off scatheless when you were spoiling\rPatroclus, and recked not of myself who was not with him. Fool that\ryou were: for I, his comrade, mightier far than he, was still left\rbehind him at the ships, and now I have laid you low. The Achaeans\rshall give him all due funeral rites, while dogs and vultures shall\rwork their will upon yourself." \r\rThen Hector said, as the life ebbed out of him, "I pray you by your\rlife and knees, and by your parents, let not dogs devour me at the\rships of the Achaeans, but accept the rich treasure of gold and bronze\rwhich my father and mother will offer you, and send my body home,\rthat the Trojans and their wives may give me my dues of fire when\rI am dead." \r\rAchilles glared at him and answered, "Dog, talk not to me neither\rof knees nor parents; would that I could be as sure of being able\rto cut your flesh into pieces and eat it raw, for the ill have done\rme, as I am that nothing shall save you from the dogs- it shall not\rbe, though they bring ten or twenty-fold ransom and weigh it out for\rme on the spot, with promise of yet more hereafter. But\rAchilles said, speaking to the dead body, "Die; for my part I will\raccept my fate whensoever Jove and the other gods see fit to send\rit." \r\rAs he spoke he drew his spear from the body and set it on one side;\rthen he stripped the blood-stained armour from Hector\'s shoulders\rwhile the other Achaeans came running up to view his wondrous strength\rand beauty; and no one came near him without giving him a fresh wound.\rThen would one turn to his neighbour and say, "It is easier to handle\rHector now than when he was flinging fire on to our ships" and as\rhe spoke he would thrust his spear into him anew. \r\rWhen Achilles had done spoiling Hector of his armour, he stood among\rthe Argives and said, "My friends, princes and counsellors of the\rArgives, now that heaven has vouchsafed us to overcome this man, who\rhas done us more hurt than all the others together, consider whether\rwe should not attack the city in force, and discover in what mind\rthe Trojans may be. We should thus learn whether they will desert\rtheir city now that Hector has fallen, or will still hold out even\rthough he is no longer living. But why argue with myself in this way,\rwhile Patroclus is still lying at the ships unburied, and unmourned-\rhe Whom I can never forget so long as I am alive and my strength fails\rnot? Though men forget their dead when once they are within the house\rof Hades, yet not even there will I forget the comrade whom I have\rlost. His mother\rtore her hair, and flung her veil from her with a loud cry as she\rlooked upon her son. His father made piteous moan, and throughout\rthe city the people fell to weeping and wailing. It was as though\rthe whole of frowning Ilius was being smirched with fire. Hardly could\rthe people hold Priam back in his hot haste to rush without the gates\rof the city. He grovelled in the mire and besought them, calling each\rone of them by his name. "Let be, my friends," he cried, "and for\rall your sorrow, suffer me to go single-handed to the ships of the\rAchaeans. His own father is even such another as myself- Peleus,\rwho bred him and reared him to- be the bane of us Trojans, and of\rmyself more than of all others. Hecuba then raised the cry of wailing among\rthe Trojans. "Alas, my son," she cried, "what have I left to live\rfor now that you are no more? Night and day did I glory in. you throughout\rthe city, for you were a tower of strength to all in Troy, and both\rmen and women alike hailed you as a god. So long as you lived you\rwere their pride, but now death and destruction have fallen upon you."\r\rHector\'s wife had as yet heard nothing, for no one had come to tell\rher that her husband had remained without the gates. She was at her\rloom in an inner part of the house, weaving a double purple web, and\rembroidering it with many flowers. She told her maids to set a large\rtripod on the fire, so as to have a warm bath ready for Hector when\rhe came out of battle; poor woman, she knew not that he was now beyond\rthe reach of baths, and that Minerva had laid him low by the hands\rof Achilles. She heard the cry coming as from the wall, and trembled\rin every limb; the shuttle fell from her hands, and again she spoke\rto her waiting-women. "Two of you," she said, "come with me that I\rmay learn what it is that has befallen; I heard the voice of my husband\'s\rhonoured mother; my own heart beats as though it would come into my\rmouth and my limbs refuse to carry me; some great misfortune for Priam\'s\rchildren must be at hand. May I never live to hear it, but I greatly\rfear that Achilles has cut off the retreat of brave Hector and has\rchased him on to the plain where he was singlehanded; I fear he may\rhave put an end to the reckless daring which possessed my husband,\rwho would never remain with the body of his men, but would dash on\rfar in front, foremost of them all in valour." \r\rHer heart beat fast, and as she spoke she flew from the house like\ra maniac, with her waiting-women following after. Her eyes were then shrouded as with the darkness\rof night and she fell fainting backwards. She tore the tiring from\rher head and flung it from her, the frontlet and net with its plaited\rband, and the veil which golden Venus had given her on the day when\rHector took her with him from the house of Eetion, after having given\rcountless gifts of wooing for her sake. You are now going into the house\rof Hades under the secret places of the earth, and you leave me a\rsorrowing widow in your house. The child, of whom you and I are the\runhappy parents, is as yet a mere infant. Now that you are gone, O\rHector, you can do nothing for him nor he for you. Even though he\rescape the horrors of this woful war with the Achaeans, yet shall\rhis life henceforth be one of labour and sorrow, for others will seize\rhis lands. The day that robs a child of his parents severs him from\rhis own kind; his head is bowed, his cheeks are wet with tears, and\rhe will go about destitute among the friends of his father, plucking\rone by the cloak and another by the shirt. Some one or other of these\rmay so far pity him as to hold the cup for a moment towards him and\rlet him moisten his lips, but he must not drink enough to wet the\rroof of his mouth; then one whose parents are alive will drive him\rfrom the table with blows and angry words. \'Out with you,\' he will\rsay, \'you have no father here,\' and the child will go crying back\rto his widowed mother- he, Astyanax, who erewhile would sit upon his\rfather\'s knees, and have none but the daintiest and choicest morsels\rset before him. They made haste\rto prepare the meal, they ate, and every man had his full share so\rthat all were satisfied. As soon as they had had had enough to eat\rand drink, the others went to their rest each in his own tent, but\rthe son of Peleus lay grieving among his Myrmidons by the shore of\rthe sounding sea, in an open place where the waves came surging in\rone after another. Presently the sad spirit of Patroclus drew\rnear him, like what he had been in stature, voice, and the light of\rhis beaming eyes, clad, too, as he had been clad in life. The spirit\rhovered over his head and said- \r\r"You sleep, Achilles, and have forgotten me; you loved me living,\rbut now that I am dead you think for me no further. Bury me with all\rspeed that I may pass the gates of Hades; the ghosts, vain shadows\rof men that can labour no more, drive me away from them; they will\rnot yet suffer me to join those that are beyond the river, and I wander\rall desolate by the wide gates of the house of Hades. The mare had been given to\rAgamemnon by echepolus son of Anchises, that he might not have to\rfollow him to Ilius, but might stay at home and take his ease; for\rJove had endowed him with great wealth and he lived in spacious Sicyon.\rThis mare, all eager for the race, did Menelaus put under the yoke.\r\rFourth in order Antilochus, son to noble Nestor son of Neleus, made\rready his horses. These were bred in Pylos, and his father came up\rto him to give him good advice of which, however, he stood in but\rlittle need. "Antilochus," said Nestor, "you are young, but Jove and\rNeptune have loved you well, and have made you an excellent horseman.\rI need not therefore say much by way of instruction. You are skilful\rat wheeling your horses round the post, but the horses themselves\rare very slow, and it is this that will, I fear, mar your chances.\rThe other drivers know less than you do, but their horses are fleeter;\rtherefore, my dear son, see if you cannot hit upon some artifice whereby\ryou may insure that the prize shall not slip through your fingers.\rThe woodman does more by skill than by brute force; by skill the pilot\rguides his storm-tossed barque over the sea, and so by skill one driver\rcan beat another. If a man go wide in rounding this way and that,\rwhereas a man who knows what he is doing may have worse horses, but\rhe will keep them well in hand when he sees the doubling-post; he\rknows the precise moment at which to pull the rein, and keeps his\reye well on the man in front of him. I will give you this certain\rtoken which cannot escape your notice. There is a stump of a dead\rtree-oak or pine as it may be- some six feet above the ground, and\rnot yet rotted away by rain; it stands at the fork of the road; it\rhas two white stones set one on each side, and there is a clear course\rall round it. It may have been a monument to some one long since dead,\ror it may have been used as a doubling-post in days gone by; now,\rhowever, it has been fixed on by Achilles as the mark round which\rthe chariots shall turn; hug it as close as you can, but as you stand\rin your chariot lean over a little to the left; urge on your right-hand\rhorse with voice and lash, and give him a loose rein, but let the\rleft-hand horse keep so close in, that the nave of your wheel shall\ralmost graze the post; but mind the stone, or you will wound your\rhorses and break your chariot in pieces, which would be sport for\rothers but confusion for yourself. Therefore, my dear son, mind well\rwhat you are about, for if you can be first to round the post there\ris no chance of any one giving you the goby later, not even though\ryou had Adrestus\'s horse Arion behind you horse which is of divine\rrace- or those of Laomedon, which are the noblest in this country."\r\rWhen Nestor had made an end of counselling his son he sat down in\rhis place, and fifth in order Meriones got ready his horses. They\rthen all mounted their chariots and cast lots.- Achilles shook the\rhelmet, and the lot of Antilochus son of Nestor fell out first; next\rcame that of King Eumelus, and after his, those of Menelaus son of\rAtreus and of Meriones. The last place fell to the lot of Diomed son\rof Tydeus, who was the best man of them all. They took their places\rin line; Achilles showed them the doubling-post round which they were\rto turn, some way off upon the plain; here he stationed his father\'s\rfollower Phoenix as umpire, to note the running, and report truly.\r\rAt the same instant they all of them lashed their horses, struck them\rwith the reins, and shouted at them with all their might. They flew\rfull speed over the plain away from the ships, the dust rose from\runder them as it were a cloud or whirlwind, and their manes were all\rflying in the wind. At one moment the chariots seemed to touch the\rground, and then again they bounded into the air; the drivers stood\rerect, and their hearts beat fast and furious in their lust of victory.\rEach kept calling on his horses, and the horses scoured the plain\ramid the clouds of dust that they raised. \r\rIt was when they were doing the last part of the course on their way\rback towards the sea that their pace was strained to the utmost and\rit was seen what each could do. The horses of the descendant of Pheres\rnow took the lead, and close behind them came the Trojan stallions\rof Diomed. They seemed as if about to mount Eumelus\'s chariot, and\rhe could feel their warm breath on his back and on his broad shoulders,\rfor their heads were close to him as they flew over the course. Diomed\rwould have now passed him, or there would have been a dead heat, but\rPhoebus Apollo to spite him made him drop his whip. Tears of anger\rfell from his eyes as he saw the mares going on faster than ever,\rwhile his own horses lost ground through his having no whip. Minerva\rsaw the trick which Apollo had played the son of Tydeus, so she brought\rhim his whip and put spirit into his horses; moreover she went after\rthe son of Admetus in a rage and broke his yoke for him; the mares\rwent one to one side the course, and the other to the other, and the\rpole was broken against the ground. Eumelus was thrown from his chariot\rclose to the wheel; his elbows, mouth, and nostrils were all torn,\rand his forehead was bruised above his eyebrows; his eyes filled with\rtears and he could find no utterance. But the son of Tydeus turned\rhis horses aside and shot far ahead, for Minerva put fresh strength\rinto them and covered Diomed himself with glory. \r\rMenelaus son of Atreus came next behind him, but Antilochus called\rto his father\'s horses. "On with you both," he cried, "and do your\rvery utmost. I do not bid you try to beat the steeds of the son of\rTydeus, for Minerva has put running into them, and has covered Diomed\rwith glory; but you must overtake the horses of the son of Atreus\rand not be left behind, or Aethe who is so fleet will taunt you. Why,\rmy good fellows, are you lagging? I tell you, and it shall surely\rbe- Nestor will keep neither of you, but will put both of you to the\rsword, if we win any the worse a prize through your carelessness,\rfly after them at your utmost speed; I will hit on a plan for passing\rthem in a narrow part of the way, and it shall not fail me."\r\rThey feared the rebuke of their master, and for a short space went\rquicker. Presently Antilochus saw a narrow place where the road had\rsunk. The ground was broken, for the winter\'s rain had gathered and\rhad worn the road so that the whole place was deepened. Menelaus was\rmaking towards it so as to get there first, for fear of a foul, but\rAntilochus turned his horses out of the way, and followed him a little\ron one side. The son of Atreus was afraid and shouted out, "Antilochus,\ryou are driving recklessly; rein in your horses; the road is too narrow\rhere, it will be wider soon, and you can pass me then; if you foul\rmy chariot you may bring both of us to a mischief." \r\rBut Antilochus plied his whip, and drove faster, as though he had\rnot heard him. They went side by side for about as far as a young\rman can hurl a disc from his shoulder when he is trying his strength,\rand then Menelaus\'s mares drew behind, for he left off driving for\rfear the horses should foul one another and upset the chariots; thus,\rwhile pressing on in quest of victory, they might both come headlong\rto the ground. Menelaus then upbraided Antilochus and said, "There\ris no greater trickster living than you are; go, and bad luck go with\ryou; the Achaeans say not well that you have understanding, and come\rwhat may you shall not bear away the prize without sworn protest on\rmy part." \r\rThen he called on his horses and said to them, "Keep your pace, and\rslacken not; the limbs of the other horses will weary sooner than\ryours, for they are neither of them young." \r\rThe horses feared the rebuke of their master, and went faster, so\rthat they were soon nearly up with the others. \r\rMeanwhile the Achaeans from their seats were watching how the horses\rwent, as they scoured the plain amid clouds of their own dust. Idomeneus\rcaptain of the Cretans was first to make out the running, for he was\rnot in the thick of the crowd, but stood on the most commanding part\rof the ground. The driver was a long way off, but Idomeneus could\rhear him shouting, and could see the foremost horse quite plainly-\ra chestnut with a round white star, like the moon, on its forehead.\rHe stood up and said among the Argives, "My friends, princes and counsellors\rof the Argives, can you see the running as well as I can? There seems\rto be another pair in front now, and another driver; those that led\roff at the start must have been disabled out on the plain. I saw them\rat first making their way round the doubling-post, but now, though\rI search the plain of Troy, I cannot find them. Perhaps the reins\rfell from the driver\'s hand so that he lost command of his horses\rat the doubling-post, and could not turn it. I suppose he must have\rbeen thrown out there, and broken his chariot, while his mares have\rleft the course and gone off wildly in a panic. Come up and see for\ryourselves, I cannot make out for certain, but the driver seems an\rAetolian by descent, ruler over the Argives, brave Diomed the son\rof Tydeus." \r\rAjax the son of Oileus took him up rudely and said, "Idomeneus, why\rshould you be in such a hurry to tell us all about it, when the mares\rare still so far out upon the plain? You are none of the youngest,\rnor your eyes none of the sharpest, but you are always laying down\rthe law. You have no right to do so, for there are better men here\rthan you are. Eumelus\'s horses are in front now, as they always have\rbeen, and he is on the chariot holding the reins." \r\rThe captain of the Cretans was angry, and answered, "Ajax you are\ran excellent railer, but you have no judgement, and are wanting in\rmuch else as well, for you have a vile temper. I will wager you a\rtripod or cauldron, and Agamemnon son of Atreus shall decide whose\rhorses are first. You will then know to your cost." \r\rAjax son of Oileus was for making him an angry answer, and there would\rhave been yet further brawling between them, had not Achilles risen\rin his place and said, "Cease your railing Ajax and Idomeneus; it\ris not you would be scandalised if you saw any one else do the like:\rsit down and keep your eyes on the horses; they are speeding towards\rthe winning-post and will be bere directly. You will then both of\ryou know whose horses are first, and whose come after." \r\rAs he was speaking, the son of Tydeus came driving in, plying his\rwhip lustily from his shoulder, and his horses stepping high as they\rflew over the course. The sand and grit rained thick on the driver,\rand the chariot inlaid with gold and tin ran close behind his fleet\rhorses. There was little trace of wheel-marks in the fine dust, and\rthe horses came flying in at their utmost speed. Diomed stayed them\rin the middle of the crowd, and the sweat from their manes and chests\rfell in streams on to the ground. Forthwith he sprang from his goodly\rchariot, and leaned his whip against his horses\' yoke; brave Sthenelus\rnow lost no time, but at once brought on the prize, and gave the woman\rand the ear-handled cauldron to his comrades to take away. Then he\runyoked the horses. \r\rNext after him came in Antilochus of the race of Neleus, who had passed\rMenelaus by a trick and not by the fleetness of his horses; but even\rso Menelaus came in as close behind him as the wheel is to the horse\rthat draws both the chariot and its master. The end hairs of a horse\'s\rtail touch the tyre of the wheel, and there is never much space between\rwheel and horse when the chariot is going; Menelaus was no further\rthan this behind Antilochus, though at first he had been a full disc\'s\rthrow behind him. He had soon caught him up again, for Agamemnon\'s\rmare Aethe kept pulling stronger and stronger, so that if the course\rhad been longer he would have passed him, and there would not even\rhave been a dead heat. Idomeneus\'s brave squire Meriones was about\ra spear\'s cast behind Menelaus. His horses were slowest of all, and\rhe was the worst driver. Last of them all came the son of Admetus,\rdragging his chariot and driving his horses on in front. When Achilles\rsaw him he was sorry, and stood up among the Argives saying, "The\rbest man is coming in last. Let us give him a prize for it is reasonable.\rHe shall have the second, but the first must go to the son of Tydeus."\r\rThus did he speak and the others all of them applauded his saying,\rand were for doing as he had said, but Nestor\'s son Antilochus stood\rup and claimed his rights from the son of Peleus. "Achilles," said\rhe, "I shall take it much amiss if you do this thing; you would rob\rme of my prize, because you think Eumelus\'s chariot and horses were\rthrown out, and himself too, good man that he is. He should have prayed\rduly to the immortals; he would not have come in fast if he had done\rso. If you are sorry for him and so choose, you have much gold in\ryour tents, with bronze, sheep, cattle and horses. Take something\rfrom this store if you would have the Achaeans speak well of you,\rand give him a better prize even than that which you have now offered;\rbut I will not give up the mare, and he that will fight me for her,\rlet him come on." \r\rAchilles smiled as he heard this, and was pleased with Antilochus,\rwho was one of his dearest comrades. So he said- \r\r"Antilochus, if you would have me find Eumelus another prize, I will\rgive him the bronze breastplate with a rim of tin running all round\rit which I took from Asteropaeus. It will be worth much money to him."\r\rHe bade his comrade Automedon bring the breastplate from his tent,\rand he did so. Achilles then gave it over to Eumelus, who received\rit gladly. \r\rBut Menelaus got up in a rage, furiously angry with Antilochus. An\rattendant placed his staff in his hands and bade the Argives keep\rsilence: the hero then addressed them. "Antilochus," said he, "what\ris this from you who have been so far blameless? You have made me\rcut a poor figure and baulked my horses by flinging your own in front\rof them, though yours are much worse than mine are; therefore, O princes\rand counsellors of the Argives, judge between us and show no favour,\rlest one of the Achaeans say, \'Menelaus has got the mare through lying\rand corruption; his horses were far inferior to Antilochus\'s, but\rhe has greater weight and influence.\' Nay, I will determine the matter\rmyself, and no man will blame me, for I shall do what is just. Come\rhere, Antilochus, and stand, as our custom is, whip in hand before\ryour chariot and horses; lay your hand on your steeds, and swear by\rearth-encircling Neptune that you did not purposely and guilefully\rget in the way of my horses." \r\rAnd Antilochus answered, "Forgive me; I am much younger, King Menelaus,\rthan you are; you stand higher than I do and are the better man of\rthe two; you know how easily young men are betrayed into indiscretion;\rtheir tempers are more hasty and they have less judgement; make due\rallowances therefore, and bear with me; I will of my own accord give\rup the mare that I have won, and if you claim any further chattel\rfrom my own possessions, I would rather yield it to you, at once,\rthan fall from your good graces henceforth, and do wrong in the sight\rof heaven." \r\rThe son of Nestor then took the mare and gave her over to Menelaus,\rwhose anger was thus appeased; as when dew falls upon a field of ripening\rcorn, and the lands are bristling with the harvest- even so, O Menelaus,\rwas your heart made glad within you. He turned to Antilochus and said,\r"Now, Antilochus, angry though I have been, I can give way to you\rof my own free will; you have never been headstrong nor ill-disposed\rhitherto, but this time your youth has got the better of your judgement;\rbe careful how you outwit your betters in future; no one else could\rhave brought me round so easily, but your good father, your brother,\rand yourself have all of you had infinite trouble on my behalf; I\rtherefore yield to your entreaty, and will give up the mare to you,\rmine though it indeed be; the people will thus see that I am neither\rharsh nor vindictive." \r\rWith this he gave the mare over to Antilochus\'s comrade Noemon, and\rthen took the cauldron. Meriones, who had come in fourth, carried\roff the two talents of gold, and the fifth prize, the two-handled\rurn, being unawarded, Achilles gave it to Nestor, going up to him\ramong the assembled Argives and saying, "Take this, my good old friend,\ras an heirloom and memorial of the funeral of Patroclus- for you shall\rsee him no more among the Argives. I give you this prize though you\rcannot win one; you can now neither wrestle nor fight, and cannot\renter for the javelin-match nor foot-races, for the hand of age has\rbeen laid heavily upon you." \r\rSo saying he gave the urn over to Nestor, who received it gladly and\ranswered, "My son, all that you have said is true; there is no strength\rnow in my legs and feet, nor can I hit out with my hands from either\rshoulder. Would that I were still young and strong as when the Epeans\rwere burying King Amarynceus in Buprasium, and his sons offered prizes\rin his honour. There was then none that could vie with me neither\rof the Epeans nor the Pylians themselves nor the Aetolians. In boxing\rI overcame Clytomedes son of Enops, and in wrestling, Ancaeus of Pleuron\rwho had come forward against me. Iphiclus was a good runner, but I\rbeat him, and threw farther with my spear than either Phyleus or Polydorus.\rIn chariot-racing alone did the two sons of Actor surpass me by crowding\rtheir horses in front of me, for they were angry at the way victory\rhad gone, and at the greater part of the prizes remaining in the place\rin which they had been offered. They were twins, and the one kept\ron holding the reins, and holding the reins, while the other plied\rthe whip. Such was I then, but now I must leave these matters to younger\rmen; I must bow before the weight of years, but in those days I was\reminent among heroes. For all\rwhich may the grace of heaven be vouchsafed you in great abundance."\r\rThereon the son of Peleus, when he had listened to all the thanks\rof Nestor, went about among the concourse of the Achaeans, and presently\roffered prizes for skill in the painful art of boxing. He brought\rout a strong mule, and made it fast in the middle of the crowd- a\rshe-mule never yet broken, but six years old- when it is hardest of\rall to break them: this was for the victor, and for the vanquished\rhe offered a double cup. Then he stood up and said among the Argives,\r"Son of Atreus, and all other Achaeans, I invite our two champion\rboxers to lay about them lustily and compete for these prizes. He\rto whom Apollo vouchsafes the greater endurance, and whom the Achaeans\racknowledge as victor, shall take the mule back with him to his own\rtent, while he that is vanquished shall have the double cup."\r\rAs he spoke there stood up a champion both brave and great stature,\ra skilful boxer, Epeus, son of Panopeus. He laid his hand on the mule\rand said, "Let the man who is to have the cup come hither, for none\rbut myself will take the mule. I am the best boxer of all here present,\rand none can beat me. Is it not enough that I should fall short of\ryou in actual fighting? Still, no man can be good at everything. I\rtell you plainly, and it shall come true; if any man will box with\rme I will bruise his body and break his bones; therefore let his friends\rstay here in a body and be at hand to take him away when I have done\rwith him." \r\rThey all held their peace, and no man rose save Euryalus son of Mecisteus,\rwho was son of Talaus. Mecisteus went once to Thebes after the fall\rof Oedipus, to attend his funeral, and he beat all the people of Cadmus.\rThe son of Tydeus was Euryalus\'s second, cheering him on and hoping\rheartily that he would win. First he put a waistband round him and\rthen he gave him some well-cut thongs of ox-hide; the two men being\rnow girt went into the middle of the ring, and immediately fell to;\rheavily indeed did they punish one another and lay about them with\rtheir brawny fists. One could hear the horrid crashing of their jaws,\rand they sweated from every pore of their skin. Presently Epeus came\ron and gave Euryalus a blow on the jaw as he was looking round; Euryalus\rcould not keep his legs; they gave way under him in a moment and he\rsprang up with a bound, as a fish leaps into the air near some shore\rthat is all bestrewn with sea-wrack, when Boreas furs the top of the\rwaves, and then falls back into deep water. They set him down in a swoon\rand then went to fetch the double cup. \r\rThe son of Peleus now brought out the prizes for the third contest\rand showed them to the Argives. These were for the painful art of\rwrestling. For the winner there was a great tripod ready for setting\rupon the fire, and the Achaeans valued it among themselves at twelve\roxen. For the loser he brought out a woman skilled in all manner of\rarts, and they valued her at four oxen. He rose and said among the\rArgives, "Stand forward, you who will essay this contest."\r\rForthwith uprose great Ajax the son of Telamon, and crafty Ulysses,\rfull of wiles rose also. The two girded themselves and went into the\rmiddle of the ring. They gripped each other in their strong hands\rlike the rafters which some master-builder frames for the roof of\ra high house to keep the wind out. Their backbones cracked as they\rtugged at one another with their mighty arms- and sweat rained from\rthem in torrents. Many a bloody weal sprang up on their sides and\rshoulders, but they kept on striving with might and main for victory\rand to win the tripod. Ulysses could not throw Ajax, nor Ajax him;\rUlysses was too strong for him; but when the Achaeans began to tire\rof watching them, Ajax said to ulysses, "Ulysses, noble son of Laertes,\ryou shall either lift me, or I you, and let Jove settle it between\rus." \r\rHe lifted him from the ground as he spoke, but Ulysses did not forget\rhis cunning. He hit Ajax in the hollow at back of his knee, so that\rhe could not keep his feet, but fell on his back with Ulysses lying\rupon his chest, and all who saw it marvelled. Then Ulysses in turn\rlifted Ajax and stirred him a little from the ground but could not\rlift him right off it, his knee sank under him, and the two fell side\rby side on the ground and were all begrimed with dust. It would hold six measures, and\rfar exceeded all others in the whole world for beauty; it was the\rwork of cunning artificers in Sidon, and had been brought into port\rby Phoenicians from beyond the sea, who had made a present of it to\rThoas. Eueneus son of jason had given it to Patroclus in ransom of\rPriam\'s son Lycaon, and Achilles now offered it as a prize in honour\rof his comrade to him who should be the swiftest runner. For the second\rprize he offered a large ox, well fattened, while for the last there\rwas to be half a talent of gold. He then rose and said among the Argives,\r"Stand forward, you who will essay this contest." \r\rForthwith uprose fleet Ajax son of Oileus, with cunning Ulysses, and\rNestor\'s son Antilochus, the fastest runner among all the youth of\rhis time. They stood side by side and Achilles showed them the goal.\rThe course was set out for them from the starting-post, and the son\rof Oileus took the lead at once, with Ulysses as close behind him\ras the shuttle is to a woman\'s bosom when she throws the woof across\rthe warp and holds it close up to her; even so close behind him was\rUlysses- treading in his footprints before the dust could settle there,\rand Ajax could feel his breath on the back of his head as he ran swiftly\ron. Thus did he pray,\rand Pallas Minerva heard his prayer; she made his hands and his feet\rfeel light, and when the runners were at the point of pouncing upon\rthe prize, Ajax, through Minerva\'s spite slipped upon some offal that\rwas lying there from the cattle which Achilles had slaughtered in\rhonour of Patroclus, and his mouth and nostrils were all filled with\rcow dung. But Ajax took the ox and stood with\rhis hand on one of its horns, spitting the dung out of his mouth.\rThen he said to the Argives, "Alas, the goddess has spoiled my running;\rshe watches over Ulysses and stands by him as though she were his\rown mother." Thus did he speak and they all of them laughed heartily.\r\rAntilochus carried off the last prize and smiled as he said to the\rbystanders, "You all see, my friends, that now too the gods have shown\rtheir respect for seniority. Ajax is somewhat older than I am, and\ras for Ulysses, he belongs to an earlier generation, but he is hale\rin spite of his years, and no man of the Achaeans can run against\rhim save only Achilles." \r\rHe said this to pay a compliment to the son of Peleus, and Achilles\ranswered, "Antilochus, you shall not have praised me to no purpose;\rI shall give you an additional half talent of gold." He then gave\rthe half talent to Antilochus, who received it gladly. \r\rThen the son of Peleus brought out the spear, helmet and shield that\rhad been borne by Sarpedon, and were taken from him by Patroclus.\rHe stood up and said among the Argives, "We bid two champions put\ron their armour, take their keen blades, and make trial of one another\rin the presence of the multitude; whichever of them can first wound\rthe flesh of the other, cut through his armour, and draw blood, to\rhim will I give this goodly Thracian sword inlaid with silver, which\rI took from Asteropaeus, but the armour let both hold in partnership,\rand I will give each of them a hearty meal in my own tent."\r\rForthwith uprose great Ajax the son of Telamon, as also mighty Diomed\rson of Tydeus. When they had put on their armour each on his own side\rof the ring, they both went into the middle eager to engage, and with\rfire flashing from their eyes. The Achaeans marvelled as they beheld\rthem, and when the two were now close up with one another, thrice\rdid they spring forward and thrice try to strike each other in close\rcombat. Ajax pierced Diomed\'s round shield, but did not draw blood,\rfor the cuirass beneath the shield protected him; thereon the son\rof Tydeus from over his huge shield kept aiming continually at Ajax\'s\rneck with the point of his spear, and the Achaeans alarmed for his\rsafety bade them leave off fighting and divide the prize between them.\rAchilles then gave the great sword to the son of Tydeus, with its\rscabbard, and the leathern belt with which to hang it. \r\rAchilles next offered the massive iron quoit which mighty Eetion had\rerewhile been used to hurl, until Achilles had slain him and carried\rit off in his ships along with other spoils. He stood up and said\ramong the Argives, "Stand forward, you who would essay this contest.\rHe who wins it will have a store of iron that will last him five years\ras they go rolling round, and if his fair fields lie far from a town\rhis shepherd or ploughman will not have to make a journey to buy iron,\rfor he will have a stock of it on his own premises." \r\rThen uprose the two mighty men Polypoetes and Leonteus, with Ajax\rson of Telamon and noble Epeus. They stood up one after the other\rand Epeus took the quoit, whirled it, and flung it from him, which\rset all the Achaeans laughing. After him threw Leonteus of the race\rof Mars. Ajax son of Telamon threw third, and sent the quoit beyond\rany mark that had been made yet, but when mighty Polypoetes took the\rquoit he hurled it as though it had been a stockman\'s stick which\rhe sends flying about among his cattle when he is driving them, so\rfar did his throw out-distance those of the others. All who saw it\rroared applause, and his comrades carried the prize for him and set\rit on board his ship. \r\rAchilles next offered a prize of iron for archery- ten double-edged\raxes and ten with single eddies: he set up a ship\'s mast, some way\roff upon the sands, and with a fine string tied a pigeon to it by\rthe foot; this was what they were to aim at. "Whoever," he said, "can\rhit the pigeon shall have all the axes and take them away with him;\rhe who hits the string without hitting the bird will have taken a\rworse aim and shall have the single-edged axes." \r\rThen uprose King Teucer, and Meriones the stalwart squire of Idomeneus\rrose also, They cast lots in a bronze helmet and the lot of Teucer\rfell first. He let fly with his arrow forthwith, but he did not promise\rhecatombs of firstling lambs to King Apollo, and missed his bird,\rfor Apollo foiled his aim; but he hit the string with which the bird\rwas tied, near its foot; the arrow cut the string clean through so\rthat it hung down towards the ground, while the bird flew up into\rthe sky, and the Achaeans shouted applause. Meriones, who had his\rarrow ready while Teucer was aiming, snatched the bow out of his hand,\rand at once promised that he would sacrifice a hecatomb of firstling\rlambs to Apollo lord of the bow; then espying the pigeon high up under\rthe clouds, he hit her in the middle of the wing as she was circling\rupwards; the arrow went clean through the wing and fixed itself in\rthe ground at Meriones\' feet, but the bird perched on the ship\'s mast\rhanging her head and with all her feathers drooping; the life went\rout of her, and she fell heavily from the mast. Meriones, therefore,\rtook all ten double-edged axes, while Teucer bore off the single-edged\rones to his ships. \r\rThen the son of Peleus brought in a spear and a cauldron that had\rnever been on the fire; it was worth an ox, and was chased with a\rpattern of flowers; and those that throw the javelin stood up- to\rwit the son of Atreus, king of men Agamemnon, and Meriones, stalwart\rsquire of Idomeneus. But Achilles spoke saying, "Son of Atreus, we\rknow how far you excel all others both in power and in throwing the\rjavelin; take the cauldron back with you to your ships, but if it\rso please you, let us give the spear to Meriones; this at least is\rwhat I should myself wish." \r\rKing Agamemnon assented. So he gave the bronze spear to Meriones,\rand handed the goodly cauldron to Talthybius his esquire.\r\r----------------------------------------------------------------------\r\rBOOK XXIV\r\rThe assembly now broke up and the people went their ways each to\rhis own ship. There they made ready their supper, and then bethought\rthem of the blessed boon of sleep; but Achilles still wept for thinking\rof his dear comrade, and sleep, before whom all things bow, could\rtake no hold upon him. This way and that did he turn as he yearned\rafter the might and manfulness of Patroclus; he thought of all they\rhad done together, and all they had gone through both on the field\rof battle and on the waves of the weary sea. As he dwelt on these\rthings he wept bitterly and lay now on his side, now on his back,\rand now face downwards, till at last he rose and went out as one distraught\rto wander upon the seashore. Then, when he saw dawn breaking over\rbeach and sea, he yoked his horses to his chariot, and bound the body\rof Hector behind it that he might drag it about.
