But Apollo would not suffer it to be disfigured, for\rhe pitied the man, dead though he now was; therefore he shielded him\rwith his golden aegis continually, that he might take no hurt while\rAchilles was dragging him. \r\rThus shamefully did Achilles in his fury dishonour Hector; but the\rblessed gods looked down in pity from heaven, and urged Mercury, slayer\rof Argus, to steal the body. All were of this mind save only Juno,\rNeptune, and Jove\'s grey-eyed daughter, who persisted in the hate\rwhich they had ever borne towards Ilius with Priam and his people;\rfor they forgave not the wrong done them by Alexandrus in disdaining\rthe goddesses who came to him when he was in his sheepyards, and preferring\rher who had offered him a wanton to his ruin. \r\rWhen, therefore, the morning of the twelfth day had now come, Phoebus\rApollo spoke among the immortals saying, "You gods ought to be ashamed\rof yourselves; you are cruel and hard-hearted. Did not Hector burn\ryou thigh-bones of heifers and of unblemished goats? And now dare\ryou not rescue even his dead body, for his wife to look upon, with\rhis mother and child, his father Priam, and his people, who would\rforthwith commit him to the flames, and give him his due funeral rites?\rSo, then, you would all be on the side of mad Achilles, who knows\rneither right nor ruth? He is like some savage lion that in the pride\rof his great strength and daring springs upon men\'s flocks and gorges\ron them. Even so has Achilles flung aside all pity, and all that conscience\rwhich at once so greatly banes yet greatly boons him that will heed\rit. man may lose one far dearer than Achilles has lost- a son, it\rmay be, or a brother born from his own mother\'s womb; yet when he\rhas mourned him and wept over him he will let him bide, for it takes\rmuch sorrow to kill a man; whereas Achilles, now that he has slain\rnoble Hector, drags him behind his chariot round the tomb of his comrade.\rIt were better of him, and for him, that he should not do so, for\rbrave though he be we gods may take it ill that he should vent his\rfury upon dead clay." \r\rJuno spoke up in a rage. The old man went straight into the house\rwhere Achilles, loved of the gods, was sitting. There he found him\rwith his men seated at a distance from him: only two, the hero Automedon,\rand Alcimus of the race of Mars, were busy in attendance about his\rperson, for he had but just done eating and drinking, and the table\rwas still there. King Priam entered without their seeing him, and\rgoing right up to Achilles he clasped his knees and kissed the dread\rmurderous hands that had slain so many of his sons. \r\rAs when some cruel spite has befallen a man that he should have killed\rsome one in his own country, and must fly to a great man\'s protection\rin a land of strangers, and all marvel who see him, even so did Achilles\rmarvel as he beheld Priam. The others looked one to another and marvelled\ralso, but Priam besought Achilles saying, "Think of your father, O\rAchilles like unto the gods, who is such even as I am, on the sad\rthreshold of old age. It may be that those who dwell near him harass\rhim, and there is none to keep war and ruin from him. Yet when he\rhears of you being still alive, he is glad, and his days are full\rof hope that he shall see his dear son come home to him from Troy;\rbut I, wretched man that I am, had the bravest in all Troy for my\rsons, and there is not one of them left. I had fifty sons when the\rAchaeans came here; nineteen of them were from a single womb, and\rthe others were borne to me by the women of my household. The greater\rpart of them has fierce Mars laid low, and Hector, him who was alone\rleft, him who was the guardian of the city and ourselves, him have\ryou lately slain; therefore I am now come to the ships of the Achaeans\rto ransom his body from you with a great ransom. Fear, O Achilles,\rthe wrath of heaven; think on your own father and have compassion\rupon me, who am the more pitiable, for I have steeled myself as no\rman yet has ever steeled himself before me, and have raised to my\rlips the hand of him who slew my son." \r\rThus spoke Priam, and the heart of Achilles yearned as he bethought\rhim of his father. He took the old man\'s hand and moved him gently\raway. The two wept bitterly- Priam, as he lay at Achilles\' feet, weeping\rfor Hector, and Achilles now for his father and now for Patroclous,\rtill the house was filled with their lamentation. But when Achilles\rwas now sated with grief and had unburthened the bitterness of his\rsorrow, he left his seat and raised the old man by the hand, in pity\rfor his white hair and beard; then he said, "Unhappy man, you have\rindeed been greatly daring; how could you venture to come alone to\rthe ships of the Achaeans, and enter the presence of him who has slain\rso many of your brave sons? You must have iron courage: sit now upon\rthis seat, and for all our grief we will hide our sorrows in our hearts,\rfor weeping will not avail us. The immortals know no care, yet the\rlot they spin for man is full of sorrow; on the floor of Jove\'s palace\rthere stand two urns, the one filled with evil gifts, and the other\rwith good ones. He for whom Jove the lord of thunder mixes the gifts\rhe sends, will meet now with good and now with evil fortune; but he\rto whom Jove sends none but evil gifts will be pointed at by the finger\rof scorn, the hand of famine will pursue him to the ends of the world,\rand he will go up and down the face of the earth, respected neither\rby gods nor men. Even so did it befall Peleus; the gods endowed him\rwith all good things from his birth upwards, for he reigned over the\rMyrmidons excelling all men in prosperity and wealth, and mortal though\rhe was they gave him a goddess for his bride. But even on him too\rdid heaven send misfortune, for there is no race of royal children\rborn to him in his house, save one son who is doomed to die all untimely;\rnor may I take care of him now that he is growing old, for I must\rstay here at Troy to be the bane of you and your children. And you\rtoo, O Priam, I have heard that you were aforetime happy. They say\rthat in wealth and plenitude of offspring you surpassed all that is\rin Lesbos, the realm of Makar to the northward, Phrygia that is more\rinland, and those that dwell upon the great Hellespont; but from the\rday when the dwellers in heaven sent this evil upon you, war and slaughter\rhave been about your city continually. Bear up against it, and let\rthere be some intervals in your sorrow. Mourn as you may for your\rbrave son, you will take nothing by it. You cannot raise him from\rthe dead, ere you do so yet another sorrow shall befall you."\r\rAnd Priam answered, "O king, bid me not be seated, while Hector is\rstill lying uncared for in your tents, but accept the great ransom\rwhich I have brought you, and give him to me at once that I may look\rupon him. May you prosper with the ransom and reach your own land\rin safety, seeing that you have suffered me to live and to look upon\rthe light of the sun." \r\rAchilles looked at him sternly and said, "Vex me, sir, no longer;\rI am of myself minded to give up the body of Hector. My mother, daughter\rof the old man of the sea, came to me from Jove to bid me deliver\rit to you. Moreover I know well, O Priam, and you cannot hide it,\rthat some god has brought you to the ships of the Achaeans, for else,\rno man however strong and in his prime would dare to come to our host;\rhe could neither pass our guard unseen, nor draw the bolt of my gates\rthus easily; therefore, provoke me no further, lest I sin against\rthe word of Jove, and suffer you not, suppliant though you are, within\rmy tents." \r\rThe old man feared him and obeyed. Then the son of Peleus sprang like\ra lion through the door of his house, not alone, but with him went\rhis two squires Automedon and Alcimus who were closer to him than\rany others of his comrades now that Patroclus was no more. These unyoked\rthe horses and mules, and bade Priam\'s herald and attendant be seated\rwithin the house. They lifted the ransom for Hector\'s body from the\rwaggon. but they left two mantles and a goodly shirt, that Achilles\rmight wrap the body in them when he gave it to be taken home. Then\rhe called to his servants and ordered them to wash the body and anoint\rit, but he first took it to a place where Priam should not see it,\rlest if he did so, he should break out in the bitterness of his grief,\rand enrage Achilles, who might then kill him and sin against the word\rof Jove. When the servants had washed the body and anointed it, and\rhad wrapped it in a fair shirt and mantle, Achilles himself lifted\rit on to a bier, and he and his men then laid it on the waggon. He\rcried aloud as he did so and called on the name of his dear comrade,\r"Be not angry with me, Patroclus," he said, "if you hear even in the\rhouse of Hades that I have given Hector to his father for a ransom.\rIt has been no unworthy one, and I will share it equitably with you."\r\rAchilles then went back into the tent and took his place on the richly\rinlaid seat from which he had risen, by the wall that was at right\rangles to the one against which Priam was sitting. "Sir," he said,\r"your son is now laid upon his bier and is ransomed according to desire;\ryou shall look upon him when you him away at daybreak; for the present\rlet us prepare our supper. Even lovely Niobe had to think about eating,\rthough her twelve children- six daughters and six lusty sons- had\rbeen all slain in her house. Apollo killed the sons with arrows from\rhis silver bow, to punish Niobe, and Diana slew the daughters, because\rNiobe had vaunted herself against Leto; she said Leto had borne two\rchildren only, whereas she had herself borne many- whereon the two\rkilled the many. Nine days did they lie weltering, and there was none\rto bury them, for the son of Saturn turned the people into stone;\rbut on the tenth day the gods in heaven themselves buried them, and\rNiobe then took food, being worn out with weeping. They say that somewhere\ramong the rocks on the mountain pastures of Sipylus, where the nymphs\rlive that haunt the river Achelous, there, they say, she lives in\rstone and still nurses the sorrows sent upon her by the hand of heaven.\rTherefore, noble sir, let us two now take food; you can weep for your\rdear son hereafter as you are bearing him back to Ilius- and many\ra tear will he cost you." \r\rWith this Achilles sprang from his seat and killed a sheep of silvery\rwhiteness, which his followers skinned and made ready all in due order.\rThey cut the meat carefully up into smaller pieces, spitted them,\rand drew them off again when they were well roasted. Automedon brought\rbread in fair baskets and served it round the table, while Achilles\rdealt out the meat, and they laid their hands on the good things that\rwere before them. As soon as they had had enough to eat and drink,\rPriam, descendant of Dardanus, marvelled at the strength and beauty\rof Achilles for he was as a god to see, and Achilles marvelled at\rPriam as he listened to him and looked upon his noble presence. When\rthey had gazed their fill Priam spoke first. "And now, O king," he\rsaid, "take me to my couch that we may lie down and enjoy the blessed\rboon of sleep. Never once have my eyes been closed from the day your\rhands took the life of my son; I have grovelled without ceasing in\rthe mire of my stable-yard, making moan and brooding over my countless\rsorrows. Now, moreover, I have eaten bread and drunk wine; hitherto\rI have tasted nothing." \r\rAs he spoke Achilles told his men and the women-servants to set beds\rin the room that was in the gatehouse, and make them with good red\rrugs, and spread coverlets on the top of them with woollen cloaks\rfor Priam and Idaeus to wear. So the maids went out carrying a torch\rand got the two beds ready in all haste. Then Achilles said laughingly\rto Priam, "Dear sir, you shall lie outside, lest some counsellor of\rthose who in due course keep coming to advise with me should see you\rhere in the darkness of the flying night, and tell it to Agamemnon.\rThis might cause delay in the delivery of the body. And now tell me\rand tell me true, for how many days would you celebrate the funeral\rrites of noble Hector? Tell me, that I may hold aloof from war and\rrestrain the host." \r\rAnd Priam answered, "Since, then, you suffer me to bury my noble son\rwith all due rites, do thus, Achilles, and I shall be grateful. You\rknow how we are pent up within our city; it is far for us to fetch\rwood from the mountain, and the people live in fear. Nine days, therefore,\rwill we mourn Hector in my house; on the tenth day we will bury him\rand there shall be a public feast in his honour; on the eleventh we\rwill build a mound over his ashes, and on the twelfth, if there be\rneed, we will fight." \r\rAnd Achilles answered, "All, King Priam, shall be as you have said.\rI will stay our fighting for as long a time as you have named."\r\rAs he spoke he laid his hand on the old man\'s right wrist, in token\rthat he should have no fear; thus then did Priam and his attendant\rsleep there in the forecourt, full of thought, while Achilles lay\rin an inner room of the house, with fair Briseis by his side.\r\rAnd now both gods and mortals were fast asleep through the livelong\rnight, but upon Mercury alone, the bringer of good luck, sleep could\rtake no hold for he was thinking all the time how to get King Priam\raway from the ships without his being seen by the strong force of\rsentinels. He hovered therefore over Priam\'s head and said, "Sir,\rnow that Achilles has spared your life, you seem to have no fear about\rsleeping in the thick of your foes. You have paid a great ransom,\rand have received the body of your son; were you still alive and a\rprisoner the sons whom you have left at home would have to give three\rtimes as much to free you; and so it would be if Agamemnon and the\rother Achaeans were to know of your being here." \r\rWhen he heard this the old man was afraid and roused his servant.\rMercury then yoked their horses and mules, and drove them quickly\rthrough the host so that no man perceived them. When they came to\rthe ford of eddying Xanthus, begotten of immortal Jove, Mercury went\rback to high Olympus, and dawn in robe of saffron began to break over\rall the land. Priam and Idaeus then drove on toward the city lamenting\rand making moan, and the mules drew the body of Hector. No one neither\rman nor woman saw them, till Cassandra, fair as golden Venus standing\ron Pergamus, caught sight of her dear father in his chariot, and his\rservant that was the city\'s herald with him. Then she saw him that\rwas lying upon the bier, drawn by the mules, and with a loud cry she\rwent about the city saying, "Come hither Trojans, men and women, and\rlook on Hector; if ever you rejoiced to see him coming from battle\rwhen he was alive, look now on him that was the glory of our city\rand all our people." \r\rAt this there was not man nor woman left in the city, so great a sorrow\rhad possessed them. Hard by the gates they met Priam as he was bringing\rin the body. Hector\'s wife and his mother were the first to mourn\rhim: they flew towards the waggon and laid their hands upon his head,\rwhile the crowd stood weeping round them. They would have stayed before\rthe gates, weeping and lamenting the livelong day to the going down\rof the sun, had not Priam spoken to them from the chariot and said,\r"Make way for the mules to pass you. Afterwards when I have taken\rthe body home you shall have your fill of weeping." \r\rOn this the people stood asunder, and made a way for the waggon. When\rthey had borne the body within the house they laid it upon a bed and\rseated minstrels round it to lead the dirge, whereon the women joined\rin the sad music of their lament. Foremost among them all Andromache\rled their wailing as she clasped the head of mighty Hector in her\rembrace. "Husband," she cried, "you have died young, and leave me\rin your house a widow; he of whom we are the ill-starred parents is\rstill a mere child, and I fear he may not reach manhood. Ere he can\rdo so our city will be razed and overthrown, for you who watched over\rit are no more- you who were its saviour, the guardian of our wives\rand children. Our women will be carried away captives to the ships,\rand I among them; while you, my child, who will be with me will be\rput to some unseemly tasks, working for a cruel master. Or, may be,\rsome Achaean will hurl you (O miserable death) from our walls, to\ravenge some brother, son, or father whom Hector slew; many of them\rhave indeed bitten the dust at his hands, for your father\'s hand in\rbattle was no light one. Therefore do the people mourn him. You have\rleft, O Hector, sorrow unutterable to your parents, and my own grief\ris greatest of all, for you did not stretch forth your arms and embrace\rme as you lay dying, nor say to me any words that might have lived\rwith me in my tears night and day for evermore." \r\rBitterly did she weep the while, and the women joined in her lament.\rHecuba in her turn took up the strains of woe. "Hector," she cried,\r"dearest to me of all my children. So long as you were alive the gods\rloved you well, and even in death they have not been utterly unmindful\rof you; for when Achilles took any other of my sons, he would sell\rhim beyond the seas, to Samos Imbrus or rugged Lemnos; and when he\rhad slain you too with his sword, many a time did he drag you round\rthe sepulchre of his comrade- though this could not give him life-\ryet here you lie all fresh as dew, and comely as one whom Apollo has\rslain with his painless shafts." \r\rThus did she too speak through her tears with bitter moan, and then\rHelen for a third time took up the strain of lamentation. "Hector,"\rsaid she, "dearest of all my brothers-in-law-for I am wife to Alexandrus\rwho brought me hither to Troy- would that I had died ere he did so-\rtwenty years are come and gone since I left my home and came from\rover the sea, but I have never heard one word of insult or unkindness\rfrom you. When another would chide with me, as it might be one of\ryour brothers or sisters or of your brothers\' wives, or my mother-in-law-\rfor Priam was as kind to me as though he were my own father- you would\rrebuke and check them with words of gentleness and goodwill. Therefore\rmy tears flow both for you and for my unhappy self, for there is no\rone else in Troy who is kind to me, but all shrink and shudder as\rthey go by me." \r\rShe wept as she spoke and the vast crowd that was gathered round her\rjoined in her lament. Then King Priam spoke to them saying, "Bring\rwood, O Trojans, to the city, and fear no cunning ambush of the Argives,\rfor Achilles when he dismissed me from the ships gave me his word\rthat they should not attack us until the morning of the twelfth day."\r\rForthwith they yoked their oxen and mules and gathered together before\rthe city. Nine days long did they bring in great heaps wood, and on\rthe morning of the tenth day with many tears they took trave Hector\rforth, laid his dead body upon the summit of the pile, and set the\rfire thereto. Then when the child of morning rosy-fingered dawn appeared\ron the eleventh day, the people again assembled, round the pyre of\rmighty Hector. When they were got together, they first quenched the\rfire with wine wherever it was burning, and then his brothers and\rcomrades with many a bitter tear gathered his white bones, wrapped\rthem in soft robes of purple, and laid them in a golden urn, which\rthey placed in a grave and covered over with large stones set close\rtogether. Then they built a barrow hurriedly over it keeping guard\ron every side lest the Achaeans should attack them before they had\rfinished. When they had heaped up the barrow they went back again\rinto the city, and being well assembled they held high feast in the\rhouse of Priam their king. \r\rThus, then, did they celebrate the funeral of Hector tamer of horses.\r\rTHE END\r\r----------------------------------------------------------------------\r\rCopyright statement:\rThe Internet Classics Archive by Daniel C.
